THE BED PAGE OF AUSTRALASIAN NOTES AND QUERIES .
1. —Red Hair.
‘R.’ incorrectly states (10/6/97) that the preju-
dice against red hair is peculiar to English-speak-
log peoples, and that ‘ amongst the Germans this
prejudice does not exist.’ As a matter of fact,
Hermans are possibly more prejudiced against red
dair than Anglo-Saxons. Of this, as a German
aative, lam confident. Two proverbs well-known
throughout German-speaking Europe are : ‘ Rothe
3iaare uud Erlenholz wachsen auf keinem guten
(Red hair and aldervvood never grow on
food soil), and ‘ Die Rothen und die Gezeichneten
Gabon's hinter den Ohren ’ (The red [haired] and
Jhe marked ones [such as cross-eyed, &e.] are to be
yarded against). I don’t think ‘ haben’s hinter
den Ohren ’ has an English equivalent; but it is
employed to denote people you can’t trust. As to
other races, let your correspondent ask a Jew, or
an Arab, or even Frenchmen pr Spaniards, how
much they ‘particularly admire’ red hair, and
three times out of four he will be told to beware
of red-haired folk.—J.B.W., Perth, W.A.
‘ R,. ’ says antipathy to red hair is peculiar to
Anglo-Saxons, and that nearly all other races
admire it. In a description of the uses of hair-
rowder given by Parini in his satire ‘ II Mattino ’
Btumble on
In bianco volse
II biondo, il nero, e Vodiato rosso.
The epithet scarcely means that Italians admire
red hair. By the way, lam told that in Turkey
*ll Jews have red hair. Is this correct?-
M.T.
4. —Marsupial Reproduction.
It is not surprising that there should still be
division of opinion among bushmen on this sub-
ject, for even philosophers took a long time to
make up their minds. Our aboriginals observed
the facts correctly, for P. Cunniugham, writing
in 1827 (‘Two Years in New South Wales,’p.
313), says the natives assert that young marsu-
pials are born in the usual way, and that the
mother places them in the pouch. So far as
regards Australia, European scientists had few
Opportunities of investigation in the early days;
but this was not the ease with American pouched
animals, whose conditions of birth nevertheless
for long remained mysterious. Even dissection
was not at first conclusive. James Mudie (‘ The
Picture of Australia, ’ 1829, p. 161) says ‘dissec-
tions, and most minute ones, have been made, and
careful observations taken, by and in concert with
Sir Everard Home, but the result has only
shortened the time [i.e., observed the young in
the pouch at an earlier stage] without removing
the difficulty.’ Westgai th, writing in IH4B (‘Aus-
tralia Felix,’ pp. 129, 13U) is assured that the
young marsupial is bom in utao.
There is an interesting reference to marsupials
(when the reproduction-question was still unset-
tled) in Oliver Goldsmith’s ‘ History of the Earth*
(Glasgow, 1835, vo-1. ii, pp. 447 et sq. ). The book
says—
Pouched animals were known at first only in America.
All the species found on that continent agree so com-
pletely in general organisation, as well as in their peculiar
confirmation of the genitals, that Linnaeus found in
them the elements of a single genus, which he called
Didelphas, or double-w'ombed. Afterwards from the East
Indies, and still later from the regions of Australasia,
animals arrived equally distinguished by the possession
of the abdominal pouch. At first an opinion arose that
the young of these animals were actually produced in the
abdominal pouch hi side the mainmee of the mother. It
is nearly two centuries since Musgrave has said: ‘The
pouch is properly the matrix of the Cariqueya (Didelphas
Opossum). I have been unable to find any other ; this is
a point whioh I have ascertained by dissection. The
semen is produced there, and the young are formed.’
Pison confirms the same facts, having, as he observes,
dissected many of the opossums. Valentyn makes the
same assertion in his account of the Molucca Islands:
‘The pouch of the philanders is a matrix in which the
young are conceived. This pouch is not what is usually
supposed. The mammas are, with regard to the young,
what stalks are to their fruit.. The young remain attached
to the mammae until they have obtained maturity, and
then separate from them as the fruit, drops from the
stalk.’ Those notions are also commorrin Virginia, even
among physicians. Beverly says that the young opos-
sums exist in the false belly, without ever entering the
true, and are developed on the teats of the mother. The
Marquess of Chartellux makes a similar remark. Hence
Pennant says, ‘ that, suspended to the mammae of the
mother, they remain there at first without motion ; this
lasts until they have acquired some development and
strength, but then they undergo a second birth.’
Two opossums (Didelphas Virginia), male and female,
were domesticated in the house of M. d’Abovile in 1783 ;
these animals copulated, and the effects were attentively
observed by that gentleman ; in about ten days the edge
of the orifice of the pouch grew thicker, a phenomenon
which afterwards grew more peroeptible. As the pouch
increased in size, the orifice widened. On the thirteenth
day the female did not quit her retreat except to eat,
drink, and evacuate ; on the fourteenth day she did
not stir from ifflj M. d’Aboville then determined to
seize and examine her ; the pouch, the aperture of
which had widened before, was now nearly closed,
a slimy secretion moistened the hairs on its circum-
ference. On the fifteenth day, a finger w 7 as intro-
duced into the pouch, and a round body about the size of
a pea was plainly felt at the bottom. This examination
was made with difficulty on account of the impatience of
the mother, who had before been always mild and tran-
quil. On the seventeenth, she permitted a further
examination, and M. d’Aboville discovered two bodies
about the size of a pea. There was, however, a great
number of these young ones. On the twenty-fifth day,
they moved very perceptibly, yielding to the touch ; on
the fortieth, the pouch was sufficiently open for them to
be plainly distinguished ; and on the sixtieth, when the
mother lay down, they were seen hanging to the teats,
some outside the pouch, some inside. The nipple is
about two-eighths of an inch in length ; hut it soon dries
up, and at last drops off, after the manner of the umbilical
oord.
M. Geoffroy, lamenting the vagueness and obscurity
existing on the subject of pouched animals, wrote an
article in 1819, with this query as title: ‘Are the pouched
animals horn attached to the teats of their mother? ’
His object was to call the attention of scientific men to
the subject, and more especially of those who possess the
means of investigation in those countries which form the
habitat of the animals. His observations are highly in-
teresting and important. On the pouch, he remarks that
it is not in the adult female a cavity of equal capacious-
ness at all times. M. d’Aboville observed it to increase
in magnitude tinder the influence of the phenomena of
generation, and M. Geoffroy himself has observed its
relative dimensions in females of the same species. It is
small previous to sexual intercourse, large to excess when
the young ones are about to drop from the mammas, and
of a moderate size in the period immediately following.
Thus the pouch cannot he considered tneiely as a second
domicile , without spring or activity ; it is a true place of
incubation, extending by degrees, acquiring more and
more volume, as happens to any other domicile of the
foetus. Well, therefore, might it he called a second
uterus, and the most, important of the two.
As to the mode in whioh the young are placed in the
external pouch, or, rather, attached to the nipple, nothing
is accurately known. A communication between the ex-
ternal uterus and this pouch has been asserted to exist,
but never demonstrated. Some have imagined that the
mother placed the young there herself with her hand and
feet: hut this is not very likely. Another opinion was
that the pouch extended to the orifice of the vagina ; hut
the_ muscles do not seem disposed for such an arrange-
ment, and some species have no pouch.
Pouched animals derive their appellation of Marsupiata,
or, as some call them, marsupials, from the character of
the pouch. It may. however, be well questioned whether,
as a generic or classic term, it is unobjectionable. There
are many species in which this Character of the pouch does
not exist, while, on the contrary, there are none without
the double matrix, which would render the Linniean ap-
pellation of Didelphas more universally suitable to all
G. Gerhard.
H.G.R. (17/7/97) says ‘both kangaroos and
platypi are monotremes.’ This is a mistake. The
only members of the monotremata are platypus
and echidna (see Nicholson’s ‘ Manual of Zoology ’
p. 656, Blackwood, 1880). —
Nil, S.A.
17. —Habits of Persons Lost in Bush.
A number of 1 bushed ’ people scatter their
clothes about with a view to letting any pro-
spective search-party know that the scent is hot.
The vast majority of those rescued in a state of
nudity, and delirious, can, >n subsequent recovery,
give no explanation why they shed their garments.
The most feasible theory seems to be that
the thirst and starvation usually associated
with such cases conduce to a high state of
fever, and the clothes are thrown away in order
to cool the delirious sufferer—just as a typhoid
patient would, if permitted, throw his bed-clothing
bn one side. Regarding the walking in circles to
the left, bushed people turn in this direction 99
times out of 100. In the remaining instance they
take a route straight ahead, and I know of no
case when anybody lost in the busli turned to the
right. This peculiarity seems due to the fact that
the right leg is—invariably, I think—longer and
stronger than the left; and the general compara-
tive weakness of the left side of the body leads to
an inclination that way, especially if the walker
be at all exhausted. —McG.
I think it will be found, on referring to all
known cases, that no sane man has ever parted
with his clothing when bushed. It is when the
mind becomes unhinged that the clothes are oast
off, because then any covering whatever is op-
pressive and irritating. As to circling, the sanest
man that ever trod the bush will circle if inex-
perienced. The reason, I think, may be found in
an inequality of the eyes. The right eye is, I
think, generally keener than its companion, and
can thus locate or distinguish objects at a longer
range. Though these appear to be straight ahead,
they are in reality a little to the right of a direct
line, and a man steering by these distant ob-
jects is gradually executing a circle to the right.
Another reason may be found in the varying powers
of the legs, if the sight is accepted as trustworthy.
I have been told by old bushmen that if you
sight always with the same eye, and from the
same end, it is impossible to erect a long line of
fence without curving it.
The following is the rule generally followed by
old hands when they find themselves ‘in a fog ’ ;
First locate the four principal points of the Com-
pass by the sun ; at night by the stars ; then steer
by successions of two objects at short range. It is
nearly impossible to go straight to a single object
at great distance ; it is always safe to have two in
a line. If the person ‘ bushed ’ is insufficiently
versed in astronomy to take bearings by the planets
he should look to the ‘lay ’ of the country.
Never ascend a hill; make for the lowest ground,
for there’s sure to be a water-course at the end of
it, and, no matter how small, it will ultimately
lead to a lake or river and habitations.
The following incidents may bear fruit:—
Some years ago I started from Myrtle Creek to
11 (it* through the bush to Wooroowoolgen station
a distance of 20 miles. After four hours steady
ndmg. and as I was looking out for the clearing
above the homestead, X saw a creek before me.
Act until I had ridden up and down that creek
several times, viewing and reviewing the hut on
the other side, did 1 recognise it as my camp—the
spot I had started from. The horse had gradually
edged round without my noticing it. A similar
case came under my notice a short time ago. An
overseer started from his house for a tank six
miles away. There were no fences—no track. He
was going straight as the crow flies, and in two
hours pulled up behind a stable. There he sat
nonplussed, lie couldn’t think what stable it
was, never having noticed any such building
between the station and the tank before. Then
he rode round to the front, and nearly fell out of
the saddle when he saw his wife’s face appear at
the window. Physically the horse had turned him
round ; mentally he was shaped the other way.
Therefore home was behind him, and the tank
should be—- ■ ‘ls that you, Reginald ?’-
E.S.S.
18. —The Maginnis.
I am not sure ‘ A.E.0.8.’ (17/7/97) is right in
referring tins phrase to an Irish-American origin.
I remember the term in very general use thirty
years ago, and I heard an explanation by an old
Scotchman, who was a famous wrestler and boxer.
It was to the effect that, in the olden days, a
chieftain of the clan Mclnnes (ignorantly printed
Maginnis) had a knack in catching hold
of his opponent—an arm crooked round the neck
which always left him master of the situation.
Thus the term putting ‘ the crook of Mclnnes ’ on
anyone meant putting a complete block on the
opponent’s movements, or, in other words, com-
pelling a victory. ‘The crooked Maginnis,’ is, of
oourse, a corruption.-
M.C.
19.—'The Real Mackay. ’
Pronounce ‘ Muckeye.’ I don’t think the deri-
vation has anything to do with Port Mackay, Q.
The old Scotch song, * He’s no the. rale Sandy
Mackay !’ is the original legend as far as
Australia is concerned—printed and published
long before rum' and niggers brought the Northern
port into good and bad repute respectively. Fifty
years ago the expression ‘not the real Mackay’
was as common as ‘not the clean potato.’— SCOTTY
the Rumklkr.
The phrase ‘real Mackay ’ dates back to long
before there was any * Mackay, Q.’ There was a
very respectable forger in Sydney in the early
thirties whose name was Mackie, and whose per-
mits and discharges were unfailingly successful in
passing the authorities. It was said that a ‘pass
into Heaven if written by Mackie would deceive
St. Peter.’ Hence a ‘real Mackie’ document
was valued. This on the authority of my father,
who was a surgeon in the days of the old regime.
WORRY M.
31 —Australian Slang.
i append a few examples of bush slang from
N.S. Wales and Queensland. Some I have only
heard quite recently : most are commonly used by
swagmen and others who follow work at the
shearing-sheds.
Carrying Swag—‘Pushing the knot,’ Very
common in Q. and N.S.W. since 1891. Intro-
duced by the city element who yearly come up-
country for the wool season. Probably from the
nautical knot, for mile.
Swag—‘ National debt.’ This is more in vogue
in settled districts, especially Blue Mountain
way. Seldom used out back. The root idea is
that each is a burden.
Cook—‘Poisoner,’ Well known through N.S.W.
and Q. for many years. First applied to bad
cooks, but now generalised.
Flour—‘ Carrington tooth-powder.’ I have
heard only recently on Castlereagh and Barwon
Rivers, N.S.W., e.g. : A traveller, on getting a
very small pint of flour, remarked to his mate
‘ This is another Carrington tooth-powder station.’
Damper—‘Night-mare.’ New, though I’ve
heard it occasionally in Q. as well as in N.S.W.
Johnny-Cake— ‘ Death Adder.’ Recent. Used
mainly on the Dqrling River, N.S.W. Intro-
duced hy city bushmen.
Loaf of Bread ‘Dodger.’ Common in N.S. W.
settled districts. Query: from ‘Dodging the
baker’—begging a loaf of bread ?
Baking Powder—‘Dynamite.’ Common through-
out the bush. It makes things ‘rise.’
Mutton —‘Dunlop.’ New slang. Used mostly
on the Darling and Western border of Q. Dunlop
is one of the largest sheep-runs on the Darling,
and owners give liberal quantities of mutton to
travellers.
Beef—‘Tyson.’ Old. Tyson runs mostly cattle,
and the main diet on his stations is beef.
Shoulder of Mutton— ‘ Banjo.’ Ribs of Mutton
—‘ Concertina.’ . Ancient, and very common
throughout Australia. Both terms, of course,
refer to the meat’s appearance.
Bone—‘Point it.’ Quite a new term to me.
Heard in Western Wales only. A chestnut
still goes round the bush re an old swagman who,
when he had no meat, used to point his bread at a
bone and let imagination do the rest.
Cabbage—‘ Leprosy.’ Only lately used in Q.
and western N.S. Wales, from the association of
leprosy with the Chinese bush-gardener. ‘Pass
the leprosy ! ’ Ugh 1 (Often heard at dinner-time
in shearers’ huts.)
Tobacco—‘Snout.’ Old and common. Query:
a remnant of Botany Bay times, when prisoners
on road-works used to point to their mouths by
way of silently asking passers-by for tobacco?
Match—‘Anarchist.’ New. Only heard re-
cently in western N.S.W.
Beer— 1 Catchup.’ New to me. Used by
N.S.W. bush-town beer-cadgers. Query ; from
‘ Catch up to him ; lie’s going to shout! ’
Long- glass of beer—‘War Cry.’ Common in
western N.S.W. and Q. Used since the Salvation
Army has come into the bush.
Bottle (of anything)—‘Aristotle.’ Fairy-tale—
‘Joe Rail.’ These two are rarely used, and only
of late. I’ve heard them both in N.S.W. and Q.
They are obviously constructed on the plan of
London thieves’ patter, by substituting a word
with similar terminal sound— e.g ., ‘ Anna Maria ’
for fire; ‘daisy-roots ’ for boots; etc.
Delirium tremens— ‘ Elephants.’ When I first
heard this a year or two ago in Western N.S.W.,
I hardly understood its meaning. - ‘ He’s got ele-
phants,’ I was told by a chap vviiose mate was ill
at a wayside pub. Have heard it frequently since.
Does it spring by antithetical perversion from
‘ snakes ’ — the old slang?
Battling— i.e., struggling—‘On the Johnnie
Ru-sel.’ A city term, quite new in the bush.
Used in Q. atid N.S.W. towns—mainly by public-
house loafers.
Blankets—‘ Early risers,’ Old and common.
Bush men are seldom burdened with much ‘nap’
(another term for blanket), and on cold winter
mornings are hunted out of bunk at daybreak in
search of the nearest fire.
New slang terms are soon circulated in the bush.
Swagmen are always on the move, and always
proud to be the first to introduce the latest epithet
in camps or shearers’ huts.—6 xB.
QUERIES.
32. —Most Australian wild birds lay their eggs
in very open places—often on an open plain, with-
out troubling to make a nest; yet, as far as I
have seen, it is very seldom the eggs are interfered
with. But I have several times noticed that, on
removing them to a very secluded spot, the crow
will find them out in ten minutes. Is this others'
experience? If so, what is the cause?- R. WIL-
LIAMS, Gilgunnia.
33. —What is the origin of term ‘ broombies’ or
‘brumbies,’ applied to wild horses? Some years
ago, I think, ‘ Bulletin ’ said it is derived from an
entire and mares that had broken away from Cox’s
‘ Broombie ’ station. This, I lately learned, has
been denied by the Messrs. Cox. Another more
recent derivation was from Baramba station, in
the Maryborough (Q.) district; but the evidence
did not seem satisfactory.—A.L.
34. What is the origin of the word ‘squatter ’ ?
I believe it is American, and from ‘squat,’ but
would like some dates and references, and know-
ledge how it came to Australia.—V.
35. When did the practice of ring-barking trees
commence in Australia? Is it common elsewhere?
—R.Q.
36. Did the poet Gordon leave or have any
children? Is his widow still living—where, and
in what circumstances? Does she derive any
income from the sale of Gordon’s poems ?—J.G.L.
37. Dr. P. Maloney, of Melb., writes in the
‘lntercolonial Medical Journal,’ re the beard:
‘The European who is without it belongs to an
easily-reeognisable class of sexual impotents, and
nations such as the Chinese, whoso undeveloped
faces are well-nigh bald, belong to a cruel, corrupt
and stagnant civilisation, their Japanese oon-
querors succeeding, doubtless, by their slighter
advance in hirsutem-ss. That idiotic and savage
monster, Philip V., of Spain, was by nature
beardless, and when he issued his edict that his
subjects;should shave, the Spaniards, the foremost
men in the world of that day, exclaimed that their
souls had gone out of them with their beards.

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN AUGUST.
O, almond blossoms, almond blooms, I think
you are a cloud
That sank from heaven’s depths, too tired to
further sail along,
And rested on the crooked stems that are so
sweetly proud
To bear you, that their leaves burst forth a
green thanksgiving song !
A cloud you were, soft, soft and white, and Juno
on you rested
When she put off her royalty to lounge her
languid leisure,
And Cytherea’s doves, cloud pure, with wings
cloud soft, have breasted
Their fairy way, their trackless way, through
you to do her pleasure.
The birds know, and the bees know this, your
worshippers and lovers—
The birds have seen your pride above, and give
you one devotion :
And all the bees from miles away crowd round,
their briskness hovers
Above you, and the calm’s disturbed of all the
air’s dim ocean.
Still, cloud that once a goddess bore, your pride
has more been heightened
Since you alighted here this spring than when
you sailed above ;
For one has stood beneath your shade by whom
the world is brightened
Who makes the dawn seem dim and dark—my
own and lovely love !
Adelaide.
ROSAMOND BENHAM.
DREAMS AND DEEDS.
In dreams—l wield the lightning’s flash
And whip the wearying planet’s pace.
In deed—l tremble at the dash
Of cabman’s whip flicked near my face.
In dreams —with joyous gods I dine,
And nectar’s none too good for me.
In fact—l take another line ;
A very mildish kind of tea.
In dreams—l lead an armed host
To victory through storm and stress.
In deed—my fiercest fight at most
Is but an ill-played game of chess.
In dreams—l own a business vast,
And in huge industries engage.
In deed—at risks I stand aghast
And tremble for my weekly wage.
In dreams—my well-kept garden knows
The harvest of my steady toil.
In deed—the weeds in serried rows
Possess my patch of city soil.
Good Sancho gave his thanks for Sleep,
But, when t see how sordid seems
This world of those who toil and weep,
I offer up my thanks for Dreams.
They come with cruel Fact to cope,
And temper stern old Fate’s decrees ;
They dower day with art and hope,
And night with varied fantasies.
And, if you add up Life’s account,
You’ll find the dreams, though filmy-light,
Are far the best of the amount :
The things that make the balance right.
Sydney.
ZEF.
THE FAR-AWAY LAND.
The far-away, far land where nobody lives
Excepting the people I made,
A glowing and comforting noon of light gives,
Or a sunset of solacing shade,
And a while of contemplative, quiet content
When the powers are weak or the forces are spent.
I put down my irksome, slow pen and I go I . . .
Though only a mere thought away,
It’s a million or billion of furlongs or so
From the rim of the wearisome day !
It is so far away that the thunderous sound
Of the travail and work of the Earth-day is
drowned.
There men with unquenchable passion for Right
The highways of courage pursue ;
They challenge the speeches of riotous Might,
And combat the deeds it would do.
Oh, ’tis pleasant to see their brave, scorn-thrilling
eyes
Flashing fire on the helmeted captains of lies !
And they of the studious, arduous soul
Pore over old volumes and scrolls,
Athirst and aflame to find out how the whole
Mystic Universe pivots and rolls.
It is vain ; but the pageants of day and of night
Fill them full of exultant, delirious delight.
And those, too, endowed with a quickened, keen
sense
For the merry, glad aspect of things—
When the faces of others are tortured and tense,
Their faces make laughter that rings.
Oh, ’tis peasant to look at them rallying there,
Launching shallops of joy on soft ripples of air !
And they with the star-beaming eyes of soft blue,
And the many with large eyes of grey,
Are some of them trusty and loyal and true,
And some of them set to betray,
But all of them fashioned to lure or command
In this far-away, far-away, thought-shapen land.
I made these and others in prose and in verse
In the past with slow labor of brain ;
And when I now see them and hear them rehearse
Their virtues and foibles again,
I conceive as I once again take up my pen
That perhaps the good God sometimes so visits
men !

IT MAKES NO DIFFERENCE.
“ The Primate of Australia is henceforth to rank as Archbishop.” — Cable.
CABBY (to most Rev. Bob Cob Smith): “Oh, yer a Archbishop now, are ye? Well, I don’t care aif yer the Archfienel hisself; yer ain’t a-goin’ to ride to no
Bishopcourt in a first-class outfit like this here for no bob fare! ” —(Drives of with language unfit for a Bishop to list a to —to say nothing of an Archbishop.)

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 7, 1897.
The “Unearned Increment.”
The various disasters that have lately be-
fallen the New South Wales land-tax have
roused up B. R. Wise, ex-M.L.A., to tell
in the Sydney daily papers a tale about how
that tax came into existence, and the
treachery by which it was created, and its
wickedness and foolishness and want of
scientific incidence, and other things to the
same efiect. Other people have risen to
support Wise, and to contradict him, and to
do other things to him ; and the ghost of
our old friend, the “unearned increment,”
together with other spectres that have
troubled the local world but little of late,
loom out of the mists of controversy. B. R.
Wise’s objection to the impost itself, lodged
presumably on behalf of the Single-tax
party, may be best told in his own words :
The land-tax, as passed into law, is so different in
principle from that which was recoin mended to the elec-
tors that, quite apart from carelessness in drafting and
mistakes in form, it has an altogether different scope to
which the previous arguments are not intelligently refer-
able. It is avowedly a tax upon land as a species of pro-
perty—a taking away from the owner a part of that which
he has bought and paid for. The land-tax recommended
to the country was a tax only upon the betterment. If
there was no betterment there was to be no tax; but if
there was a betterment, then it was thought reasonable
and just that a portion of that which the State had given to
the owner by the expenditure of public monevs should
come back to the State in the form of taxation. From
the application of this principle no one was to be exempt
and no complicated machinery was to be required to
bring it into operation, since every owner was to assess
himself, subject to a right of purchase by the Government
at an advance upon his valuation, or, possibly, by private
persons at a greater advance.
* * * *
According to B. R. Wise, Premier Reid
virtually pledged himself to impose, not a
tax on land but a tax on the enhanced value
given to land by circumstances for which the
owner was not responsible. In other
words, he pledged himself to tax only the
betterment, or the unearned increment
and then he meanly went back on his pledge
for sordid reasons which won’t bear inspec-
tion. As Premier Reid’s version of the
story has not been heayd yet, his alleged
back-down is a matter of some uncertainty •
but The Bulletin takes comparatively little
interest in that part of the question. What
it is mainly interested in is the sudden resur-
rection of its venerable acquaintance, the
Unearned Increment. This world is mostly
governed by phrases ; almost any creed which
has a crisp little gag attached to it can main-
tain some kind of existence on the strength
of it. Bi-metallism lives to a great extent
on such expressions as “ the yellow man with
the white money ” and “ the white man with
tke yellow money ” ; and “ the crime of
1873 ” also comes in handy. The Single-tax
party has valuable assets in the terms “ bet-
terment ” and “ unearned increment,” and
in the good, old, hopelessly-exploded argu-
ment, ‘‘all wealth comes from the land.”
All wealth doesn’t come from the land, for
land without water is mere Sahara and pro-
duces practically nothing ; wealth comes
from moisture and atmosphere and sunlight
and human agency and land ; but the phrase
goes on just the same. Similarly, one of the
agencies which won Cleveland his second
Presidential election was the war-cry of
‘‘ Franky and her baby ” ; it meant nothing
in particular, but it was a good, loud phrase,
and did some service. And, a/rrupos of
Phrases in general, The Bulletin desires
just here to damn the unearned increment
and all its equivalents in the Single-tax
vocabulary, as an expression that has been
done to death until it has become a weari-
ness to the flesh and a burden to the spirit.
* * * *
In theory, no doubt, the idea which B. R.
Wise advances is strictly logical. The State
sold much of its land alarmingly cheap as
present ideas go; still it did sell it and it
can’t honorably go back on its bargain.
When the bulk of it was sold nothing was
said about a land-tax ; if anything had been
said the purchaser might have refused to
give even the moderate price demanded in
the early days. To impose a tax on the
whole value of the land means that the State
forcibly takes back part of what it sold and
received payment for, and to a dreadfully
sensitive conscience that is a questionable
proceeding. On the other hand, the “ better-
ment” or the “unearned increment” or
whatever it is called, was not in the original
bond. The land which the State sold in its
unimproved state for £1 an acre may now be
worth 30s. or £lO or £4O or £2OO, still in its
unimproved state. The increase is caused
partly by the increase of population for
w hich the owner isn’t responsible (except in
a limited degree) ; partly by the expenditure
of money for which he also isn’t responsible.
According to the most rigid variety of theo-
letical justice, it is a fair thing to tax this
betterment, or unearned increment, and it is
not fair to tax the original purchase value.
It is a beautiful theory, but the remorseless
logic of Things As They Are interferes very
badly with the application of it.
* * * *
The betterment is a legitimate object of
taxation when it can be got at and located,
but in most cases it has dispersed in many
directions, and the person who got it can’t
be found. The State sold its land to the first
pioneer. Smith, at (say) £1 an acre, in the
40’s. Smith sold out to Brown at £4 in the
60's, and disappeared with his share of the
unearned increment or the betterment, and
his present address is the universe generally.
Brown passed the land on to Jones at £8
and died, and his descendants dissipated the
money, and the present residence of the
family is at the soim-kitchen or looking in
the dust-bin at night for broken meat.
Jones mortgaged it to a bank at a value of
£l2 an acre, and lost the money silver-boom-
ing, and Jones’s earthly tenement is an
unmarked grave somewhere or other, because
he didn’t leave enough increment to pay for
a tombstone. The bank liquidated, and the
land was finally passed on to the present
holder at £4O an acre, and has since gone
back in value to £2O. Therefore, there has
been a betterment of £l9 an acre, possibly
arising entirely through the expenditure of
btate money around the property, but the
only man who can be got at in respect of it is
the present holder of the property, and his
share of it is less than nothing. Considering
the enormous amount of land which changed
hands in the boom days, and the enormous
and unanimous slump which has taken place
since then, it is probable that a third or one-
half of the present landholding community
hasn’t got any unearned increment whatever.
On the other hand, there are cases where the
value of land has remained practically
stationary, and where the acres which were
sold for 20s. many years ago are not, worth
quite 30s. to-day, and never have been worth
more. Consequently, if it is proposed to make
the present holder liable for the unearned
increment (and he is the only person who
could be made liable), the man above-
described who had lost £2O an acre would be
taxable on £l9, or 95 per cent, of the value
of lus property ; and the other man who lost
nothing would be liable on 10s., or 33 per-
cent. of the value of his property, and there
would be chaos all round.
* * * *
The betterment or unearned increment
would be a fair subject of taxation, if it
could be identified, but it seems to be a great
deal too widely scattered. Some of it is
diowned off the Horn, and some away drink-
ing in Johannesburg, or tacking off a lee
shore in the Chinese seas, or administering
the Dogberry variety of justice from an
English magisterial bench. It has melted in
the Cosing colony of Paraguay, and frozen
on the new goldfields of the Yukon, and
vanished among Westralian wild-cat pro-
moters in London. Except in the compara-
tively rare cases where property has remained
m tlie same family for a long series of years,
the betterment can seldom be traced in any
satisfactory fashion. Taxing it, in a vast
number of cases, would only mean taxing the
man who hasn't got any betterment, because
he bought his property from the man who
did get it, or who came after the man who got
it. 1 his is how it would work out if the tax
was levied on the betterment which has
already accrued. The matter might be put
on a basis of cast-iron justice by starting
fioni present values, and simply taxing all
future unearned increment, but in that case
the land-tax, as a source of revenue, would
be worth next to nothing for years to come,
and wouldn’t pay expenses in its earlier
stages.
* * » *
Taxation in the main is a coarse, sordid
effort to get money. To prevent the public
kicking too hard an attempt is made to dis-
tribute the burden with some semblance of
equity, and to get the money with the least
amount of injury to the community, but
when the question lies between pure theore-
tical justice and getting money, theoretical
justice generally has to go down. The land-
tax, as B. R. Wise points out, is “a tax on
land as a species of property ; ” if it wasn’t a
species of property, and therefore of some
cash value, probably very few people would
pay any tax on it, and the threat that it
would be seized and sold in case of non-
payment would be of very little value. It is
also, as B. R. Wise points out, a “ taking
away from the owner a part of that which he
has bought and paid for.” A tax has to be
a taking away of something from somebody ;
if the btate didn’t take away something from
somebody it obviously wouldn’t get anything
from anybody. And it must take away some-
thing that the owner has bought and paid
for, or that his ancestors bought and paid for,
the sufficient reason being that the goods
which are acquired dishonestly or without
payment each year in this small community,
even if the State could find them all and
take them away, wouldn’t cover the cost of
Government or anything distantly resembling
it. And, considering that the people are the
State and are really taking away some of
their own property from themselves to give
it to themselves for their own use, the griev-
ance doesn’t look so very bad after all.' The
IST.S. W. land-tax is probably a coarse and
unscientific grabbing after ready money, but
that seems unavoidable. And it has one
great recommendation : it isn’t decorated
with beautiful phraseology about unearned
increment and similar learned wearinesses.
Whatever evils and imperfections there may
be about Premier Reid’s land-tax, the fact
that it leaves out all mention of the unearned
increment will always be remembered in its
favor.
The Turner Government: A Wail
“ R.P.S,” writes in a bitter spirit as follows :
The dearth of large-brained and clean-handed Demo-
crats is very noticeable at the present time, and no surer
proof of this could be given than Tub Bulletin’s adula-
tion, off and on, of the Victorian Ministry. Sir Hodge
Turner is elevated to the plane of Higinbotham and
Ballance, and the result of a fortunate accident is set
down to the credit of his statesmanship. After having
fairly committed itself to Turner, The Bulletin was
placed in a nasty position by the idol’s acceptance of a
Knighthood. An attempt was made to minimise the
force of the blow. The shabbily-dressed, unambitious
Premier on whom the unsought honor was thrust, etc.
would probably, by his contemptuous indifference for it’
do more to bring the title into disrepute than the
scornful refusal of another, and so on. The said im-
ambitious Premier, whose own remarks on tus dis-
inclination for power seem to have been accepted
at face-value by The Bulletin, belongs to the
freemasons, the Orange Lodge, the Foresters
and the Natives, and also to the St. Hilda Council. It was
his modest desire for seclusion, no doubt, which drove
him from the fierce glare of domestic life to those lonely
places of obscurity. As for his statesmanship, it consisted
of sitting tight and doing nothing. Turner came in on a
rising market, and he just sat and watched it rise. Vic-
toria, with its great natural resources, could not be kept
down for ever. To hasten on the results of this beneficent
statesmanship, the Westralian craze developed, and the
unemployed problem was solved. A sum ranging up to
£.30,000 a month poured into the colony, and the unem-
ployed, instead of beiny a clog*, as on previous Govern-
ments, became a buoy, assisting Sir George and crew to
float.
Peacock, in Bulletin parlance, is the “brainy” Pea-
cock. <^uery—the “zany” Peacock? He brought in a
Factories Act, which is more or less a botch, owing to
numerous provisions for evasion, and a Saturdav half-
holiday scheme which was a failure. He also displayed a
Legree sort of talent for flogging up the underpaid female
slaves of the Education Department, who have no votes.
Then Isaacs (of the double somersault), popularly sup-
posed to be, alter the Age, the brains of the Ministry
and who wins Bulletin praise for stern incorruptibility'
goes round witli the hat cadging Salvation Army votes’
In fact, public posturing and vote-cadging are more
characteristic of this Government than of any for a long
time. That it has travelled so far on its press-lauded
imaginary virtues is due to the steadfast, natural inevi-
table recovery which Victoria lias been making from the
ills of the depression, which recovery is actua ly ascribed
to the talents of a Ministry which had as little to do with
it as they have witli the rising of the tide.
The rest of the gang are merely gapstoppers. Cimi-
BERT and Best are nobodies, and Duffy is hauled into the
team to please the Catholic party, which is suffering more
than any other body from the absolute lack of a good
public man of its creed. At the same time, The Bulletin
is unjust to him, and has caused disgust by allowing itself
to be dragged at Syme's chariot-wheel and converted into
a weekly echo of the Age, which bitterly and venomously
hates Duffy for being the son of his father and for bein'---
Irish and Catholic. "
The success of Victoria's Ministers is due to the associa-
tion, or set of associations, to which nearly every member
belongs. In regard to the “ unambitious ” Turner this is
particularly the case, (woof his associations being secret
societies, whieh are the curse of this province. Another
cause is that our politicians, like the Age and The Bulle-
tin have sensitive ears cocked for the first whisper of
public opinion. Those who appear to rule, and those who
pretend to direct, are themselves ruled and directed by
the opinions or inclinations of the masses, whose shaiii
leaders they are.
*’* * .
This is a fair sample of many letters which
come to The Bulletin office on political
subjects in general, and the writer is a fair
sample of the unintelligent individual who
can’t understand the existence of a paper which
has no party ties whatever. The business
of a party paper is to uphold everything its own
man does, even when he does wrong, and to
amn everything the other man does even when
he does right. Therefore, the party paper, ii
it generally approved of Turner or anybody
else, would have to approve of him always—
unless he left his party. The Bulletin, not
being a party paper, hasn’t “committed itself”
to lurner or anybody else. It isn’t bound to
admire him all the time or to curse him all the
time, and it only keeps on admiring him
because it feels so inclined up to date. Being
absolutely independent it was able to tell the
sohd truth about Turner’s title—that he
sbouldn t have taken the mangy gewgaw which
wiH probably drag him down in the end to
political lophet—but that, as he never labelled
himself a Democrat, or said he wouldn’t take a
title or openly derided titles in general, he
stood in a better position than certain other
1 renders who had done all these things. If
this isn t solid fact then the wild correspondent
above-mentioned is invited to explain what the
solid facts really are.
r * * *
The Bulletin is perfectly aware that, as a
Democratic paper, it is a woeful disappoint-
ment to many. A popular idea of a Democratic
paper is one whose career is one long streak of
damn-blast and sulphurous adjectives, which is
against everything except the shearer and the
pick-and-shovel man, which wants to destroy
everything, and whose creed is that everything
done by the horny-handed person is right, and
everything done by everyone else is wrong.
Not being pledged to uphold any party, whether
right or wrong, The Bulletin never got into
that frame of mind. It objects to Orangemen
and lawyers, and knights and Freemasons in
general, but it declines to lie about Turner’s
able financial policy, even though he is a lawyer
and an Orangeman, and a knight and P.C., and
a ridiculously-gewgawed Freemason and various
other things. It never undertook to go back
on its own opinions, even for the sake of differ-
ing from the Age. If the Age likes to hold the
same views as The Bulletin on manv matters,
and to be a daily echo of The Bulletin, this
paper won’t change its views on that account,
even if all Victoria is “disgusted” in conse-
quence. In fact, it rather likes to hear that
somebody is disgusted.
* * * »
The Bulletin supports Turner, first and
foremost, on account of his financial policy. He
didn’t “ come in on a rising market; ” no more
stupid statement was ever uttered. He came
in upon the worst falling market these pro-
vinces have seen for many years. It fell lor
two years after he assumed office. Shiels, with
over £1,200,000 more revenue than Turner
received in his worst year, had .£700,000 deficit ;
Monro, with £1,800,000 more revenue had
nearly £BOO,OOO deficit; Patterson, with
£200,000 more revenue, had £600,000 deficit.
Turner took hold when things were going
down to the very lowest, and made ends meet
all but an inconsiderable fraction. His biggest
deficit was £330,000, and as £250,000 of that
was created by the repayment of Shiels’
Treasury bills lie really only went £BO.OOO to
leeward. No Treasurer in these provinces for
25 years past has faced such a stupendous diffi-
culty and done it so well. It was done parl ly
by levying new taxation, and mostly by hard
cheese-paring economy, but there were no other
ways of doing it, and there was no other man,
until Turner appeared, who had the courage
to do it. Yet in three years, even in these
desperately hard times, when the temptation
to fill the gap by borrowing was well-nigh
irresistible, he spent only £550,000 of loan
money just about as much as even the
best of his immediate predecessors was ac-
customed to spend in a single year. Squar-
ing the finances, in bad times, is the most
thankless work going ; also it is the most
essential duty of a Democratic Government,
ciim the one most neglected. It is compurti-
tively easy to whoop through Democratic legis-
lation that costs nothing and brings plenty of
applause and advt. But to face the eternal
grind of making 9d. do the work of a
shilling, to be constantly groping for the
odd half-penny, to be cursed by one-half
of the community for levying new taxation and
by the other for inventing new economies—to
be a miser and a money-grubber for the good of
posterity when it is so much easier to float a
big loan, and shove enough ordinary expendi-
ture into loan charges to make ends meet, and
have a large, beautiful, dishonest surplus—is a
very different thing.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s good opinion of the Turner
Government is based further on its income-tax
policy ; its valuable Companies Act, which
tackled a glaring evil with very good effect ;
its State Land Bank,-; its minimum wage
policy ; it's Factories Act; its support of the
b ederation movement; and many other things.

for any unintelligent, splashy correspondent

blance of a reason, but when a meeting was
called in Melbourne recently to condemn it the
result was a solid vote given in its favor, so ap-
parently the people who live under it regard
it with a certain amount of approval. The
lurner Government may not have a very kum
list of valuable reforms at its credit, yet it lias
more than any other Ministry in Australia or
l asmama and the things it has accomplished in
t le way of adjusting the finances are sufficient
in itself to account for most of its time. The
theory that the Premier who did such splendid
work in stopping the drift towards national in-
solvency owes his success solely to his associa-
tion with secret societies is ludicrous.
* * * '.
Cutiibert, no doubt, is a man of not much
account, but, at the same time, he looks the
best, or nearly the best, man available to repre-
sent the Government in the Council, which
contains little talent and is, in the main, bitterly
hostile to boot. Some of the other Ministers
are also of small account; it is difficult to find
ten superlative geniuses in a small Legislature *
but, on the whole, the present Victorian
Ministry is head and shoulders above the
average Australian Cabinet. The £3O 000 a
month which now comes into Victoria from
W. A. doesn t account, as “ R.P.S.” alleges for
the success of Turner, as compared with
Shiels, Munro, and others ; in their days there
was about £lOO,OOO per month more of borrowed
money coming in from England than comes in
now. therefore the influx is £70,000 less than it


wa g__not <£30,000 more. Even now, though
things are better than they were and a distinct
re covery has set in, that infallible barometer,
tlie State revenue, shows that they are not so
rood as they were in the days of Munro,
g HIKLS , and Patterson ; so the story of Turner
d Co. living on the “natural return of
prosperity” is mere Ananias. The present
Yie. Ministry faced the three worst years
of drought and ruin and slump the province
lias seen in the last quarter-century. It practi-
cally squared the finances when its predecessors,
with far larger resources, went headlong to
ruin- dispensed almost wholly with the
ruinous borrowing policy, and, even if it doesn’t
advertise itself as a Democratic Government,
its policy has been Democratic throughout. Its

hereby gives them as much support as it is

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Democratise the Municipalities!
Victorian candidates are stumping for the
Vic. municipal elections in August. Are the
socialistic and other democratic ratepayers
doin'* anything in the matter ? The munici-
pality has proved a magnificent object of
attack in England on a much narrower fran-
chise. Why should it not be captured here?
The serious flaws in the present constitution
of Vic. municipalities are as follows :
1. Only those liable to be rated and who have paid
their rates by the 10th June in each year are allowed to
vote.
2. Some ratepayers have two and others three votes.
3. There is a property-qualification for councillors.
4. Lodgers and residents, other than ratepayers, have
no votes.
5. There is no power to rate land on the “ unin p.-oved
land-value” basis.
Most of this could be easily altered by forcing
the Councils to petition for their alteration.
Parliament couldn’t withstand this. And an
alteration of the personnel of the Councils by
the united efforts of the democratic ratepayers
is the first requisite to such petitions. The
following questions asked at every election
meeting would both prepare the ratepayers for
the change, and gradually bring about the
change itself :
1. Are you in favor of every person liable to be rated
being entitled to vote, whether he has paid his rates or
not ?
2. Are you in favor of one adult one vote at municipal
elections?
3. Are you in favor of rating being based on the unim-
proved value of lands in the municipality ?
A great many councillors will be afraid to
answer the first question otherwise than in the
affirmative, and, though they may hum and ha
about the other two safely enough for a time,
gaining the first will soon mean the rest. A
lodger, and even a residence franchise, may
then be hoped for. And after ? Well, then it
may be wise to try to open the councils’ eyes to
the extensive powers they have already got in
socialistic directions, and to extend said powers.
Another appropriate question just now shouldn’t
be forgotten :
Are you in favor of a minimum-wage clause being in-
serted in all municipal contracts ?
The Murderer's Mash.
Social Purity societies have a choice subject
for moral discourse in the Eaglehawk (Vic.)
mystery, which is now solved, to the Law’s
satisfaction, by a jury verdict of wilful murder
against Charles Hall. The probable guilt, or
possible innocence, of the condemned man in
this case is a question apart from the abso-
lutely certain folly of his sexual unrighteous-
ness, and the pale, clammy saints who look for
social “sin” through gimlet-holes may con
gratulate themselves not so much upon Hall’s
conviction as upon the evidence that brought it
to pass. Charles Hall was a miner, young,
married, inconstant. One morning his wife was
found dead — Hall himself being the first to
announce the discovery—with her head in a
bucket, and the circumstances were altogether
so suggestive of the poor woman having been
done to death that her husband presently
tried for the alleged murder. The Crown
prosecution brought to light a reasonable ap-
pearance of motive for such a crime in the
person of a barmaid who admitted her-
self to be Hall’s enslaver, and laid bare
the facts of the amorous connection. She
had borne a baby by another man, and the
impecunious Hall helped her to pay for its
support. She took his money, and wrote, at his
suggestion, an anonymous letter to Mrs. Hall
with a view to bringing about a divorce. All
this she told to the Court at Hall’s first
trial, which ended in the jury’s disagreement
on the vital issue. Hall was tried a second
time, before another Judge and jury, and this
time the girl of his heart gave evidence which
doomed him. Claiming to have been aggrieved
by her severe cross-examination at the first
trial, she now said to prisoner’s counsel—“ I
have myself to consider. You did not consider
me in the Supreme Court at Bendigo. I am
not giving this additional evidence out of
revenge, although it is partly, but not solely,
the reason.” The indignant barmaid—partly
out of revenge for the publication of her
damaged reputation, forsooth ! —stated that
Hall once asked her to get sixpennyworth of
strychnine wherewith to poison his wife, also
that he had casually remarked— re the poor
woman’s tendency to cramp in the stomach—
“ if he put her head in a tub of water no one
would have the slightest suspicion ” With
these additional confessions before them
the second jury found Hall guilty. In the cell
dedicated to hanging cases Charles Hall is at
liberty to muse upon his wasted crime, and
wonder why he could have brought himself to
kill a faithful, affectionate little woman for the
purpose of putting the barmaid in her
place. If the Law’s last dread sentence be
duly carried out, Halt, will die penitent, with-
out a doubt. Penitence must surely have been
thrust upon the prisoner when the sweetheart
he had longed and crimed for put a rope around
his neck—partly out of revenge for her out-
raged womanly feelings ! There is but one
consolation for Charles Hall in his misery.
The thought that he might have lived to marry
the fascinaling young creature from behind the
bar.
Exported Products.
The rejection of the Exported Products Bill by
the Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords is a
noble example of crass ignorance and stupidity
and several other things which it would pos-
sibly be impolite to mention. The 48 Rich
Landlords, as a rule, look on any bill sent up
by the Turner Government with grave doubt,
as something that is liable to have democratic
tendencies or other explosives concealed about
it, and their first idea is always to throw it out
on suspicion. And the Exported Products Bill
was thrown out on suspicion. Yet it was a
purely business measure, which was simply cal-
culated to bring more money into the country
and then let the Fat Man and the Lean Man
scramble for possession of it when it arrived,
with every chance that the Eat Man would
get the larger share of it. There wasn’t
a semblance of any political instinct—
Democratic, Tory, or otherwise—about it.
Victoria, like the other six provinces, lives
and struggles to pay its interest bill by means
of its exports. Most of the produce it exports
is of the best quality, but here and there some
dunderheaded individual—generally a small
producer of the Democratic class which the 48
Rich Landlords utterly detest, who hasn’t the
means or the knowledge to get the best appli-
ances—sends to England bad butter or inferior
fruit or ill-flavored cheese or other rubbish, and
t his, being sold as Victorian, brings down the
value of Vic. produce in general. If all ex-
ported Vic. produce could be guaranteed as
excellent, it is reckoned that the extra prices
which it would realise would bring probably
.£5OO, OOOor some other large sum more per annum
into the province. The obvious way to secure this
uniform excellence is to prohibit the export of
goods which can’t pass a rigid inspection. This
only means blocking a very small quantity of
produce which often doesn’t realise enough to
pay freights, and which does little or no good
to the exporter himself, while doing much harm
to other people. The Exported Products Bill
was built on these lines, and, barring that
eternal minority, Murray Smith, M.L.A., it
passed the Assembly almost without opposition.
Its one audible opponent in the Council was
dull-witted Rupert Clarke, who urged no
objection to the Bill as a business proposition,
but asked if it was actually proposed to prevent
a free citizen sending any goods he had to sell
to any market he pleased, in any condition that
he liked. And this made it so clear to
the 48 Rich Landlords that the measure was a
sinful interference with the Liberty of the Sub-
ject that it was thrown out by 13 votes to 12.
Yet the Bill was mainly for the benefit of
the obtuse politicians who gave it so cold
a reception. They are all rich men, and
many of them large exporters. Being rich,
and blessed with much more business know-
ledge than political acumen, and able to get the
best appliances and to hire expert assistance,
their goods are probably almost always shipped
in the best condition. But small men, with
antiquated methods and obsolete appliances,
send goods to England which give Vic. produce
in general a bad name, and keep down prices.
Therefore the goods of the 48 Rich Landlords
fetch less than their value, and these bloated
persons are robbed of the money which would
otherwise come their way, and the want of that
money makes the country more depressed than
it need be, and, as a consequence, the value of
the Fat Man’s property is lower and his bank-
shares are less valuable than they should be, and
all manner of bad things happen to him. The
Exported Products Bill was a device to enable
every Vic. exporter to get the highest possible
value for his goods, and as the Fat Man and his
banks and finance co.’s own most of Victoria,
he would have reaped the largest part of the
benefit. The spectacle of Rupert Clarke
indignantly asking why other men shouldn’t be
allowed to damage his interests if they want to
—for that is exactly what his attitude amounted
to —is very fine indeed.
The Court of Appeal.
On the motion of (lawyer) Minister Car-
ruthers, who is fast moving every great or
useful idea out of the Federation scheme,
N.S.W. Assembly decided that there ought to
be no Federal Court of Appeal, but that the old
system of appeal to the British Privy Council
should continue. The same three arguments
which are always served up when this subject
is on the board did duty as usual. Car-
ruthers observed that it was advisable “ to
remove appeal cases from all possibility of bias
which was imperceptibly derived from local
surroundings.” The fact that the Privy
Council might have imperceptible bias from its
own local surroundings was not mentioned.
And as Privy Council appeals are only allowed
in the very few civil cases where £5OO or
more is involved, it apparently doesn’t matter
whether the other 99 per cent, or so of legal
decisions are biassed or not. Crick and
Haynes both lamented that the abolition of
Privy Council appeals would “ sever one more
of the ties which bind us to the empire.” And
Crick and Ewing asked, tragically, if we had
any right to deprive four or five million people
of “ the right 'to appeal for the redress of
wrongs to the highest Court of the empire? ”
This good old wheeze implies, of course, that it
is always the party with justice on his side who
appeals, and that it is never a case of having
the redress of urgent wrongs indefinitely post-
poned till the other man has exhausted all
possibilities of “ appeal to the highest Court of
the empire.” Moreover, it assumes that
the four or five million people have
the aforesaid right of appeal as matters
now stand, whereas not one-twentieth of
them possess it. No one has any right of appeal
to the Privy Council which is worth a copper
for practical purposes, unless he is rich enough
to put up the necessary security for costs and
to have £5OO worth of property involved in a
law-suit. To the great bulk of the community
the right of appeal to the Privy Council is just,
as practical as the right to become King of
Great Britain and Ireland, and Emperor of
India, &c. Every individual subject of the
empire has an absolute right to become King if
he can get up a large enough rebellion to upset
the present dynasty and force Parliament to
entail the crown on himself and his heirs in the
usual form. No amount of written or printed
law can make any headway against that argu-
ment. And to the ordinary subject one privi-
lege is of just about as much practical value as
the other.
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent “ J.”
is under the erroneous impression that the
claims in the N.S. W. cyanide patent are identi-
cal with those in the British patent; and, as
the whole of his contention is based on this
misapprehension, it is valueless. “ J.” says in
reply to my letter : “A technical slip was
made in including in the specifications this bad
claim,” etc. Allow me to inform him that
the N.S.VV. specification contains no other
claim —the second claim in the English specifi-
cations for a certain dilute solution does not
appear in the N.S. W. patent. Concerning
claim 1 of the English patent —which, as I have
stated, is the only claim in the N.S. W. specifi-
cations—the High Court of Appeal in England
held (9th April, ’95) that the “ claim 1 wherein
plaintiff claimed generally the use of cyanide
in solution to extract gold from ore was too
wide, and was useless, and therefore bad.”—
Arthur Griffith.
Practically the Mine Accidents Commission
incjuiry at Broken Hill petered out. One or
two union officers gave evidence warily, and,
of course, all the mine managers ; but of the
general body of men none except a few out-of-
works who had recently been discharged came
forward. The mine-owners’ attorney promised
that no workman should suffer now or in the
future for giving “ true” evidence ; but as the
workman’s idea of what is true probably differs
from the employer’s, and as the employer
might easily decide that all the evidence
which told against him wasn’t true and
act according, the workmen prudently held
their tongues. One union official who had
been selected to represent the surface workmen
at the inquiry attended to the business
instead of to his mine work, and was
promptly sacked. The two or three who did
give unfavorable evidence were as promptly de-
nounced as frauds and failures ; and in con-
tempt apparently of the whole proceedings the
surface men’s representative suggested that the
inquiry had better be wound up. So Judge
Murray raced through the remaining witnesses
and got back to Sydney.
Attorney-General Isaacs seems to be doing
as much as an able draftsman can do to ensure
the carrying out of the intention of Yic. Par-
liament re the minimum wage in Government
contracts. He has just remodelled the con-
ditions for Treasury contracts, and it is under-
stood the Public Works and Railway conditions
are to be altered similarly. The new wording
makes the Treasurer sole judge as to whether
the spirit of the contract has been carried out,
and allows him, without waiting for cumber-
some proceedings before a court of law, to
deduct wages found to be owing from progress
payments or the deposit. There is, however,
one thing badly needed in connection with the
minimum-wage provisions. It is to give the
workmen interested a right to appear as
parties interested in cases where the mini-
mum wage is involved. At present, as
the contract which has the minimum wage
condition in it is only between the department
and the contractor, the workman has no stand-
ing in the courts at all, and, as the provisions
were intended by Parliament solely for his
benefit and as a right which he could claim, it
is absolutely necessary, if the minimum wage
condition is not to become a farcical one at
the hands of an unsympathetic Treasurer, that
the men should personally be able to claim
arrears as a right. The present effect of the
minimum wage provisions in both Government
and municipal contracts is apparently to pro-
duce an abundant, almost universal, crop of
perjury among contractors, managers, and
workmen. The honest contractor is squeezed
out by the man who is prepared to swear any-
thing (generally by proxy), while the workman
has to swear he got his minimum-wage, or
starve.
An American magazine, The Cosmopolitan,
sent Julian Hawthorne, a w'ell-known Ameri-
can writer, to enquire into the causes of the
Indian famine, and thus sums up in its July
number the results of his investigations :
We are compelled to recognise that, if Mr. Hawthorne
is a competent observer, British rule in India is of the
clumsiest and least intelligent order. The impression
made upon the mind from reading the story here told is
that the government has been administered with a view
to revenue, rather than by minds deeply intent on giving
happiness to the hundreds of millions of India—by well-
meaning, indifferently-educated, haphazard officials, who
want to avoid mutiny and such civil troubles as would
scandalise Christendom, while collecting the revenues
carefully and getting all the personal enjoyment possible
out of their own clubs and limited foreign society.
All sorts of little problems seem to have been taken up
in a fair-enough-minded way; but the great problem of
these millions of almost helpless creatures, with its plan
solving their destinies upon the broadest social principles,
seems scarcely, if at all, to have been touched—though,
it is, perhaps, not quite fair for us to criticise too severely,
at this distance, these servants of Government, who are
doubtless doing their best with the facilities at their com-
mand.
But of Enqland—the nation—we have the right to de-
mand : “ Why should this be so ? ”
The mental and physical photographs which Mr. Haw-
thorne has brought back are pictures of inconceivable
conditions. Doubtless similar horrors have existed in the
world’s history, but no record has been left sufficiently
authentic to bring them vividly to our understandings.
In the photographs there is told a story of human misery
and suffering beyond which nothing more terrible can be
pictured. It is said that millions of people are thus slowly
perishing of want while their fellow-subjects, ruled by the
same Gracious Queen and Empress, are preparing to spend
in display enough millions of pounds to save the loss of
every life
What is this sham of a Christianity which knows of
these horrors and yet fails to raise its hand in protest ?
What is this sham of a civilisation which countenances
such inequalities? What is this boasted society which,
by its heartless expenditures at such a time, proves itself
so innately vulgar?
What the daily press is too “ respectable ” to
print otherwise than as an “ American story ”
of the Duke of York’s alleged morganatic
marriage, figured extensively in the English
press, some time back, with tolerably precise
indications of the distinguished official paternity
of the lady who, with her two children, was
said to have been calmly shunted when the
Duke of Clarence’s death made it dynastieally
desirable that York should mate with his
brother’s betrothed almost before “ poor
Clarence was a-cold ! ” L’he alleged first
marriage—being with a “subject”—would be
illegal, in any case ; and the whole miserable
story, if true, would simply demonst rate that
Dynasticism—which ordained that the Pi olemies,
themselves the offspring of royal incest, should
marry only their own sisters —is just as shame-
lessly ready, in its own interests, to-dav to put
its sexual relations upon the level of the beasts
of the field ! And, now that this characteristic
GuELPH-story has had public attention focussed
on it afresh by a clever “ allusion novel,” the
“ silent-contempt ” device won’t work any
longer. The thing has got to be officially and
categorically denied—if it can be !

To The Bulletin from Albany, W.A.:—
You recently said that the Forrest Government is about
to make a big effort to divert the mail steamers from
Albany to Fremantle, and that Albany and districts would
offer lively objection. What about the other provinces
objecting to the proposed diversion ? I send you a copy
of the clauses of the new contract, dealing with this
point, and you will see that, if the mail steamers go to
Fremantle, they get an allowance of time above the con-
tract time. Westralia gets the English mails at least
three days before S.A., and is able to post at least three
days later. It was understood that one of the main
points of the new contract was a quicker service, and yet,
merely to gratify the vanity of the Perth and Fremantle
people, all the other provinces are willing to suffer delay.
Nobody believes that the mail steamers can safely use
Fremantle harbor, but, as John Forrest desires them to
do so, people are looking for a miracle. When Forrest
took office, he made a wild plunge. He passed railways
to Southern Cross and to Mullens (Murciiison-road), and
the miracles of the Coolgardie arid Murchison fol-
lowed ; and, since then, every blunder he has committed
has been covered by a miracle of the same sort. And now
the people believe that he cannot do wrong. No man who
was not regarded somewhat, in the light, of a deity could
have persuaded the old Westralians who filled the Assem-
bly in last Parliament to vote two and a-half millions to
pump water 360 miles at the rate of 5,000,000 gallons
(or 22,000 tons) a day, and sell it at 3s. 6d. per 1000 gallons,
or 4Jd. per ton !! They are looking for the usual miracle
in regard to this scheme, and also as to Fremantle harbor
scheme, which is being carried out in direct defiance
of the opinion of the late John Coode. When the old
Westralians find that John Forrest is not a god, then
things in this province may fall again into their natural
condition. The frantic desire to get the mail steamers to
Fremantle and to supply the fields with water from near
Perth arises from fear of Esperance. It is felt that the
day of Esperance must come, and it is believed that if the
mails and the water of Coolgardie come from Perth they
will form two strong ties between the new districts and
the old.
The Gospel of the Lash.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[The recent severe flogging- of two prisoners at Fre-
mantle (W.A.) for the hideously-unnatural offence of
making a dash for liberty (without using violence) puts
up a record even for the gory Gospel of the Lash. There
isn’t, at best, any more logic in punishing an attempt to
break gaol than in chastising an effort to get rid of a
disease ; and, though the shooting at an escaping prisoner
may be taken, constructively, as an attempt to detain
him, the deliberate torturing of him when again safely
under key is a senseless barbarity worthy only of a com-
munity which, like Lagland, has been taught by its leg-
iron traditions to consider personal liberty as more or lesr
of an accursed thing, anyhow.]
To all who cross the border
Of the law, in moments rash,
We, the Friends of Laic and Order,
Preach the Gospel of the Lash.
With serpent-like hiss ! hiss !
The Lash swings in the air ;
’Twill kiss with bloody kiss
The victim’s body bare.
His heart with fearful throbs
Is beating, and we note
How suffocating sobs
Are bursting from his throat.
Let him curse in desperation,
And his teeth in anguish gnash !
But Society’s Salvation
Is the Gospel of the Lash.
Ho, Blogger, see that you
Your shoulders open well!
Put pith in what you do,
His rebel spirit quell !
If in his breast remain
Of human love a spark,
Bring down the lash again
And quench that in the dark !
If he’s still above brute-level
Stripe and score him, cut and gash,
Till ye make of him a devil !
’Tis the Gospel of the Lash.
He’s poor ? Then full of vice
He must be, art and part,
So up the scoundrel trice !
Cut out the ruffian’s heart!
He swoons ? He mustn’t swoon ;
He’s yot to feel his pain,
A few more cuts will soon
Restore his wits again.
To the Flogger then we fling him,
To be mangled to a hash ;
For the tidings glad we bring him
Is the Gospel of the Lash.
But when the man who sports
Silk hat and coat of black
Arrives within your courts
Touch not his tender back.
He may have been sent there
For hell’s most hideous sin,
But never, never dare
To touch his sacred skin !
Never flog the well-connected,
Flog alone the common trash,
Let the Black Coat be respected
Is the Gospel of the Lash.
Humanitarians’ cries
For mercy do not heed,
For we —the Rich and Wise—
Say Flogging is our creed !
“ Thu Lord will gauge your worth,”
Prates each presumptuous elf—
Why, when He came on earth
We flogged the Lord Himself!
For the world’s regeneration"
Kindness isn’t worth a dash !
Sirs, the only true salvation
Is the Gospel of the Lash.

graph combined with a low price. This new

found in every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*
