The first night in Sydney of the ‘ ‘ Gay
Parisienne” crowded Her Majesty’s to the last
extreme, and set one wondering how things would
go if the supports of the gallery suddenly handed
in their resignation and the whole concern tipped
forward and emptied its contents into the stalls.
Judging by present appearances, business ought to
continue prosperous for some time. The “ Parisi-
enne” has all the elements of a good musical
burlesque-farce-comedy, or whatever the proper
name of that gilded nondescript may be. It is
full of frill, and drapery, and snowy underskirt
with flounces on it, and contains a reasonable
amount of song and stocking and dance and
jewellery. A limited selection of plot is inserted
to hold it up. An aggressively British married
man, who staggers under mountains of moral re-
spectability, gets away alone to Paris, and there
takes a fainting girl home in a cab under circum-
stances which call at the top of their voice for
further inquiry. Whereupon he is rushed by a
thin streak of humanity who calls himself the
lady’s husband, and he gets out of it for the
moment by promising marriage, and bolts for his
old home, and the familiar Sunday-school, and
the tabernacle where the Pev. Stiggins holds
forth on Sunday. In the breach-of-promise case
which follows he is struck by heavy damages,
and has to disguise himself as a red-headed
Scotch Highlander and lie low at a Ger-
man spa, where the mineral water tastes
like something dead. A blighted French girl
goes away to wander over the face of the world
without any damages, and her Frenchman, who
hoped to settle down comfortably on the loot,
again makes his precarious crust by sketching ze
German fortification. These few solid facts can
be dug out here and there from the confusion of
much stocking and underskirt with frill on it;
and, having got thus far, the show suddenly goes
mad in Germany for the rest of the even.ng, and
only comes to itself in time to make a hurried
explanation and drop the curtain at a late hour.
* * * *
The dry oones of a burlesque, however,
matter very little ; the only essential is some kind
of framework to hang a lot of mixed frivolities on,
and if the frivolities are all right almost any kind
of dry bone will do for the mere purpose of sus-
pension. The “ Parisienne ” goes with a rush and
a bang and a clatter all through. Ernest Shand’s
Frenchman is.the biggest half of it, and as a
bright, hilarious joke that leggy Gaul is especially
commendable. He is a thing of beauty and a
shriek for ever, and his career is a continuous
jump of three hours’ duration. Why he doesn’t
collapse from sheer exhaustion at 10.30, having
jumped himself to death, is a matter which calls
for explanation. If he were omitted from the
cast, and G. Kignold took his place, it might
possibly dawn on the public that the “Gay
Parisienne ” was an empty thing and of
no account, bqt as matters are it looks about
the brightest affair of its kind which Sydney has
seen since a date which The Bulletin can’t locate
at the moment, the memo, on its shirt-cuff having
been washed off. Miss Ada Willoughby, who is
a good deal of the other half of the show, is a
dream of loveliness. She is a moderate success
from an acting point of view, and a very moderate
one from a singing standpoint, but as a dream she
is all there. George Lauri and Coleman are two
powerful comedians ; and though it is impossible
to disguise the fact that Miss Alice Leamar
doesn't sing “ Sister Mary Ann’s top-note” any-
thing like so well as it was lately done by Maggie
Moore, still she is another good-sized item in the
programme. The fact that people of the calibre
of Misses Juliet Wray and Florence Seymour,
Arthur Lissant, P. Bathurst, George de Lara, and
others, are scattered round in comparatively small
characters only goes to show the lavish fashion in
which the Firm has spread itself over the “ Gay
Parisienne.’' When the show has settled down
somewhat after the howl and riot of the first night,
on which occasion 25 characters and a chorus and a
large orchestra struck the audience in the eye all
at once, this paper will endeavor to further sort
out these people and a lot of other people not yet
particularised. Up to date, however, the general
impression is favorable all round, so the new pro-
duction is hereby let out on light bail pending
further consideration of its case.
** * *
A faint, odor of warm horse is wafted
through the classic theatre where the Second Mrs.
Tanqueray used to work out the tragedy of her
existence, and the comic mule has taken Dot
Boucicault’s place behind the footlights. Those
who remember the Sydney Cri. in the days of its
glory, when Dot played the French count and
Mrs. Brough was the eldest of the Amazons, can
hardly imagine Dynamite, the smallest perform-
ing ass on record, as their successor, but he is
there all the same. The flavor of hot, American
mule is spread over the establishment, and a stout
Amurrikin gentleman and a blind dramatic horse
argue with each other on that historic stage, and
the burlesque mule professes to bite the Amur-
rikin gentleman, and then tries to establish an
alibi by mixing itself up with the operatic mule
so that its proprietor won’t know which was
which. Professor Bristol, in a large, slow way of
his own, is a cute showman who covers up any
deficiencies on the part of the animals by clever
patter, and his team is a very smart mass of horse.
The list of feats is a very long one ; all of them are
clever, and some of them are really astonishing,
and the low comedy mule in particular is an
animal of immense versatility and mental grasp.
All the same, horse palls after a time, and the
show gets through in the presence of depress-
ing thin audiences. Even the fact that a
thing is wholly remarkable, isn’t in itself
sufficient to ensure success. One of the
most remarkable things going is the dead
fly in a complete state of preservation in a
lump of amber, with the guarantee of many
learned scientists that he has probably been there
doing nothing at all for 20 centuries, and that no
one knows how he got there, but that he is exactly
the same kind of fly that you blow off the meat
to-day. All the same, the lufnp of amber with
the fly in it, despite its beastly remarkableness,
wouldn’t be much of an evening’s entertainment,
and the present writer feels the same way after
2| hours of thrilling mule. Prof. Bristol’s horses
do a number of things that this scribe never saw
any horse do before, and he confesses regretfully
that he doesn’t care in the least if no horse ever
does them again. Even a very clever horse only
manages to sit down in a chair after several
attempts, and then he doesn’t appear restful, and
he is only a strained-looking horse half-way in a
chair after all, and the person who writes this
could do the business a lot better himself.
** * *
Sydney Royal has struck a patch of very
inferior luck—in fact, since the season opened
with the unsatisfactory “Johanna” nothing very
good has happened to the old house. By way of
a new departure, therefore, the “ Brand of Cain ”
—reported to be a melodrama of vast proportions
and great thrill—goes up on Saturday. The
Bulletin trusts that this production will throw
some light on a case of which only one side has
hitherto been heard. It has always regarded it
as possible that the person above-mentioned hit
Abel with a branch under great provocation, and
that the latter was a mild, objectionable, pallid
individual with green spectacles. Further, there is
no evidence that Abel wasn’t laying for Cain with
road-metal when the trouble commenced. At all
events, if the latter has anything to say in his
defence on Saturday night, this paper bespeaks
him a patient hearing and an impartial report in
the daily papers.
** * *
The Tivoli is the only Sydney theatre,
apart from Her Majesty’s, which is doing any
business worth mentioning at present. Rickards’
show and the Firm’s burlesque are crowded to the
door as a general thing, and the rest is mostly
silence and melancholy blank.
«* * *
Janies MacMahon, the desperately ener-
getic little man with the big hat, keeps his cine-
matographe still going at the old address, 237
Pitt-street, Sydney, and consignments of new
pictures arrive frequently ; some have just come,
The Lyceum is closed as usual, and the “silver
coin ” co. at the Royal Standard was wrestling
with “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” at latest advices.
#* * *
A new management, whose fell design of
re-opening Sydney Opera House, lately deserted
by Farley, was indirectly alluded to in a recent
issue, thought better of it, and is lying low at
present. An individual calling himself the
“ Bohemian Lecturer ” has rushed into the breach,
however, and announces that he will occupy the old
house with the curse on it on Saturday. If he is
anything like his name he ought to be the most
luckless person who ever struck a thoroughly luck-
less establishment. Even when Farley’s show
was at its best and playing at reduced prices, the
only sure way to keep even a thin audience was to
beguile it in with promises of drink and then take
away its clothes to prevent it escaping. Yet
Sydney likes opera and likes Farley, whereas it
hates lectures and has no kind of interest in
Bohemia.
** * *
Miss Elsie Adair’s benefit was announced
for Wednesday, but the exigencies of a paper
which goes to press on Tuesday night makes it
impossible to do justice to it in this issue. By the
time next issue comes out it will be ancient his-
tory, and no one will have any further interest in
the subject, so the less said about it the better.
In its very young days The Bulletin used to get
over such difficulties by writing in advance a
vague and general notice to the effect that every-
thing passed off well, and Miss Smith, in particu-
lar, sang charmingly. Then the cussedness of
things generally led to the entertainment being
postponed at the last moment because Miss Smith
had been fatally run over by a cab, and, as the
notice looked particularly foolish under these cir-
cumstances, this journal takes no chances now.
It has gone right out of the prophet business.
** * *
Howard Vernon, having laid the ghost of
his late opera season, is now giving melodrama a
chance at Melbourne Alexandra. The house
doesn’t overflow with humanity as yet, but it
looks less unfilled than before, and when the vast
Haleck is not very empty the managerial prospects
are encouraging, asa rule. Vernon’s present bill
is “ A River Mystery,” an honest five-act horror
with much murder and brain disease and un-
scrupulous lawyer in it. The lawyer who sticks
at nothing is supported by a doctor, who runs the
local lunatic asylum and supplies sudden deaths
on reasonable terms. Thanks to an original
dramatic device the medical man is able to kill ’em
with impunity. Even the unscrupulous legal
practitioner sniffs a possibility of danger in poison-
ing the heroine, until the doctor says, “ You for-
get ; I am the coroner ! ” whereupon the twin
assassins exchange a meaning smile, and shift the
heiress from their path with a large dose of Fatal
Drug. Luckily she comes back from the grave in
the last act. Howard Vernon, Glover, Frank
Norton, J. G. Patten, Wilson Forbes, Miss Castles
and Miss Ada Laurence are principal persons con-
cerned in the Alexandra undertakings. “East
Lynne ” will attempt to raise another sob on Fri-
day evening only, and “ A Brother’s Sin ” is fixed
for Saturday.
** * *
The Flying Jordans and Co. have struck a
rather cool time at Melbourne Royal, where they
are booked for a five Weeks’ stay. In a couple of
small particulars the entertainment grew wiser
after the opening night, and the “ American
Queen of Song” no longer warbles the word
“eye” with a sentimental aspirate. She con-
tinues to sing, however, and her sovereign
supremacy is still asserted on the programme. As
regards the two “star” turns—both of which
would be better appreciated in the Exhibition
Building— most people seem to prefer the imposing
lady on the wire to the family on the trapezes;
also, most people have realised that the perform-
ance, in each case, lacks the thrill of previous
efforts in the same branches of business. The Jor-
dans are finished artists, but none of them “ fly ” as
Walter Silbon did, for instance. They swing, or
drop, right into the grip of puppa Jordan, and the
cleverness of their feats is not more apparent than
the credit due to the fixing of the apparatus.
Miss Virginia Aragon is quite remarkable on the
wire. All the same, she cannot astonish a public
that saw Caicedo, in jack-boots, turn somersaults
on his wire, with never a netting to catch him if
he fell. And why does the lady not place her
performance across the stage, where everybody
could see it easily, instead of going aloft to trip on
a high wire with a high net stretched beneath her ?
** * *
The Hambourg boom, at Melb. Town
Hall, is to extend beyond the original arrange-
ment, after the manner of most booms, musical or
otherwise. Extra concerts will be given to-night
(Thursday) and Saturday, with a Friday matinee
sandwiched between. Further hearing of the
Hambourg brethren increases one’s respect for
their genius. Young Jakoff, not so nervous
as at first, plays the violin as wonderfully
as Mark punished the piano two years
ago, and when he begins to be a man and the
warmth of a Musin comes to him, Jakoff no
doubt will rank equal to his long-haired relative,
and together they will stand unrivalled. The
shock of falling off his bike last Sunday will pro-
bably have put a little more feeling into Jakoff’s
touch before these remarks appear in print.
Which reminds The Bulletin that two lads who
promise to stand peerless five years hence, bar
accidents, would do well to dispose of those bikes
at auction. What prices they would fetch !
* * * •
All this while the battle of the gods rages
on Pluto against Christus, which is to say
“Charley’s Aunt” versus “Sign of the Cross.”
The Aunt turns money away from the Bijou on
Saturday nights, and the Sign is in the fifth week
of triumphant success at the Princess’s. There is
nothing special to say of these rival attractions,
except that the two managements might as well
agree to swap leading men for a week or two. For
real dramatic interest there would be nothing to
choose between Julius Knight, as the Lady from
Brazil, and Frank Thornton, as Marcus, digesting
the solemn intelligence that the Kingdom of
Heaven is not meat and drink, or bath buns.

At Poverty Point .
Pianist Mark Hambourg rapidly acquires the elfish,
weird, vulpine, high-dried appearance of a Paganini.
With his spectacles on, for the rare occasions when he has
to read music, he looks over 30.
The clerical orgy about “The Sign of the Cross” reached
its apogee in Norwich, Eng. The Bishop gave his im-
primatur. Mr. Ben Creedy, newly shaven, of the “ Sign
of the Cross” Dramatic Co., clad in a surplice, most re-
verently read the prayers on Sunday morning in the
principal church ! How is that for high ? Biggest house
of the season. Topping collection. Better than a play.
Nero saying his prayers into his shiny, black belltopper,
after the orthodox overture fashion. Mercia in the latest
London sweet confection of a bonnet. Marcus in trousers.
The Flying Jordans, like Nat. Goodwin before them,
were astonished at the sudden drop in their Melb.
business after opening to a crowded house. Money
turned away on the Saturday ; nice, soft notices in the
Monday journals, but 01 the fall at night. It had
taken Sydney a fortnight to come to the conclusion which
Melbourne spotted right away.
Judging by the daily paper notices of the death of T.
H. Aspinall (of Thiodon’s Wonders fame) T.H.A. appears
to have deceased principally for the purpose of calling
attention to the fact that “ Mr. E. Lewis Scott ” used to
hold up the lecturing end of the show. And yet Aspinall
lived to 74 !
The Sydney dailies did not do half credit to the in-
genuity of poor old Thiodon’s Wonders. Out of
date and behind the times as that kind of show
is now, it is doubtful if mechanism has ever
been produced to equal it. The natural move-
ments of the figures, including taking out and put-
ting back a handkerchief, firing a gun, and setting down
and picking up a wheelbarrow, were all obtained without
the use of a single wire or thread. No mere marionettes
ever could approach them. The Wonders were sold and
dispersed after the last disastrous effort at the scene of
former triumphs—the Sydney School of Arts.
“S.S." : “ The petite Virginia Aragon is a wonder on a
wire. She has one great demerit as a show-woman —she
is too good. Watching her waltzing on the wire one for-
gets the cleverness of the performance for the reason that
the performer is so perfect. It is all too easy to her ; she
is so absolutely at home that there is never a tremor or a
thrill for the safety of those natty limbs of hers, and
thrills are essential to the success of such a spectacle.
The Bulletin has hinted that Virginia will yet break her
pretty neck in a fall from the wire. Wrong, I think, but
she may some day when taking a walk on the grass in a
thousand-acre paddock.”
Mark Hambourg, the genius who makes the piano-keys
smoke, still looks about 18, and Brother Jakoff is 16, or so,
or perhaps a liitle more. Jakoff is of pronounced Jewish
type, with such a nose, and a head of hair like the mane
of a buffalo bull.
Peggie Pryde and Marie Lloyd are among the list of
Rickards’ recent engagements. It is a long list, and the

writes to his people in Australia that he has secured “all
the best artists in London” —not a few of them only, but
everyone of the lot that he had been anxious to get upon
his books.

Pollard’s opera-girls left a lot of aching hearts behind
them at Broken Hill (where the co. is said to have netted
£lOOO for the season). The silver city johnnies became
the girls’ slaves, and volumes of letters have been sent to
the co. since “ good-byes ” were said. The johnnies, you
see, are in blissful ignorance of careful Tom Pollard's rule
of having many of the girls' love-epistles read aloud to
the assembled co. The Pollard Co. is probably the
straightest and most paternally-managed opera troupe
that ever travelled.
N : “The initials H.N.W. to a stinging letter in a
Melb. morning paper, on deadhead clergy at the theatre,
are transparent as tho*e of one of the best-known Melb.
clergymen. One hundred of the cloth take the free
banquet at a single matinee, on the footing of inmates of
the Benevolent Asylum. Mr. Wollaston —for such is
H.N.W.—wants to know why? The clergy are amid the
best-paid members of the community. Probably, not one
of these white-chokered dead-heads has less than £3 a
week, and some of them call £4 a paltry pittance. It
may safely be said that the average screw of this hundred-
strong rush, as for hot rolls at the bakery, was £6 a week.”

Paradis : “ Howard Vernon suddenly, though not cap-
riciously, jerks and dings opera at Melb. Queen’s, late
Aleck, late something else, and ’Gus. Glover, under his
auspices, exhumes with the spade of Black Mullins the
long-laid ghost of ‘Peep o’ Day.’ Born some 30 years
ago, ‘Peep o’ Day’ was the second sensation drama,
‘ Colleen Bawn ’ the first. Edmund Falconer, after play-
ing Danny Mann, under the despotism of author Bouci-
cault, for 400 nights, struck out for himself, at Drury
Lane, with his ‘ Peep o’ Day,’ which earned him a round
£lO,OOO from a year’s run. But, whilst Boucicault con-
tinued to manufacture successful sensations, Falconer
couldn’t get beyond ‘Peep.’ His new ventures failed,
and the little man died of consumption with hardly a bean
to his name.”
Dear Bulletin,— l see that “Young Australian S.S.,
who is younger in experience than in years, lays down
his lawful opinion as follows :—“ Well, the fact remains,
Paulton is a second, small, revised edition of Johnny
Toole.” To which contradiction of my opinion I reply
that my statement remains, and Paulton isn’t another
J. L. Toole. But statements of opinion like these are
valuable only for purposes of comparison by intelligent
readers who have seen Toole and Paulton on the stage.
The real fact which remains always with S.S. and myself
is this : 1 got my first ideas of the two actors when I was
a youth, in London, and I saw no more resemblance
between them at that time than I have seen from their
performances in Australia. S.S. knew nothing of them
until he saw them here, and he has looked at them through
an anti-Hinglish prejudice (as regards humor), a preju-
dice born of secret regret for his provincial training,
which blinds his eyes and paralyses his memory re Hing-
lish low comedians, and makes him utterly unfit to discuss
the matter. — Melbourne.

Percy Abbott is in Sydney this week, and announces
that the Hellers are “ resting” just now, owing to a mis-
hap which lately betel a recently-imported and expensive
serpentine dancer glucosely yclept “La Belle Vera.”
This lady, while trying recently to unite the litheness of
the serpent and the fluttersomeness of the butterfly, met
with the accident now so fashionable with skirt-dancers.
The lady has The Bulletin’s sympathy. Heller has the
only cinegraph film in which the Prince of Wales is
depicted as scratching his royal head. It is comforting
to see this picture, as it confirms one in the belief that
princes are only men, after all. The P. of W. tried to
suppress this picture, but Heller escaped from England
ere the authorities could seize him. A man-o’-war was
sent in pursuit, but, by the simple device of painting the
P. & O. steamer a pale-green during the night, capture
was avoided, and the wonderful art treasure may be seen
free by paying for the rest of the show.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Westralian revenue for July was >£174,425,
(vhich is £12,400 less than that for July, 1896. The land
and mining' revenue are the chief causes of the trouble.
And now what everybody wants to know is whether this
is a mere episode or the beginning of a slump which will
leave the Forrest Government stranded high and dry. So
far whatever else has slumped, the W.A. State revenue
has kept up a very steady increase, but its boom is
tottering on the razor-edge of disaster, and the lovely
surplus may go wrong at any moment. The latest report
is that Acting-Premier Wittenoom has received favorable
news from England about loan matters, and it is generally
understood that a desperate attempt must be made to
float something this month, or next at latest. If anything
goes wrong with that float, Gordelpus.
Victoria pays the London and Westminster
Bank £15,000 a year for managing its public debt in Lon-
don, and the Government figures out that it could do the
work itself at the Agent-General’s office for £33oo—saving
£11,700 per annum. Previous Governments, however, so
far committed the province with the London and West-
minster joint-stock cormorant that the latter disputes
Victoria’s power to deprive it of the business, and wants
£BO,OOO compensation—equal at 3 per cent, to a payment
of £2400 a year for ever—to hand over the job !
* » * *
J.O’N. : Regret that we have been unable to
gather any reliable information, either favorable or other-
wise, about the building society.
* * * *
The Australian Joint Stock Bank for the half-
year ended 30th June, ’97, shows a loss of £53,506. This
is the record loss put up by any Australian bank in one
half-year’s trading within the average man’s memory ;
the next biggest thing to it was the same institution’s loss
of £48,200 last half-year. For the previous half (ending
30th June, ’96) the A. J.S. showed a profit of £20,629, so
the slump is not only very violent but very sudden. The
reduction of deposit interest took effect on 10th May, so
the A. J.S. had nearly two months out of the six at the
reduced rate, which implies a saving of about £lO,OOO,
and this should more than cover all the costs of the new
reconstruction. Therefore it would seem that the A. J.S.
is at least £5OOO worse this half-year than it was last,
simply through continued fading away. Under the new
scheme the old 4J per cent, deposits are to be divided into
inscribed stock at 3J per cent., to be increased to 4$ if
profits admit, and “ A ” and “B ” deposits at 3 per cent,
increasing to 4 per cent, if profits admit, the increase
being governed by several dozen complicated circum-
stances particularised in The Bulletin of April 24. The
result amounts to about this : If the depositors avail
themselves of the option given to take up inscribed 3J
per cent, stock to the extent of £2,000,000, as they
probably will, the largest possible saving will be
about £69,000 a year; if they don’t so avail
themselves, the largest possible saving will be some
£79,000 a year. And the gap to be filled, on last year’s
figures, is over £lOl,OOO a year. Therefore, unless things
improve very materially, the A. J.S. won’t be nearly able
to pay even the minimum rate of reduced interest, to say
nothing of paying dividends, accumulating a reserve, or
providing the proposed extra 1 per cent, to depositors.
Probably they will greatly improve ; the A. J.S. has a
tremendous mass (over £3,000,000 worth) of pastoral in-
vestments, and the drought can’t come every year. Still,
there doesn't look sufficient margin to give the bank a
fair show.
The loss of =£53,506 is written off that myste-
rious thing called the old bank’s reserve-fund. When the
A.J.-S, reconstructed in ’93, the reserve of £747,079 was
set aside to cover possible depreciation. This gradually
faded away until, at June ’96, there only remained
£374,212. Then it vanished altogether out of the balance-
sheet, but it now appears that, after the above writing-off,
there still remains about £96,000 of it and £52,000 of the
new reserve to cover losses. The amount of these losses
is not stated, but one London director alleged that they
would probably swallow most of the paid-up capital
(£1,166,578). parallel case seems to be that
of the Commercial'of Australia. It reached bottom at
30th June, 1896,--when it made a loss of £24,055, and the
immediate result was the writing off of £1,200,000 capital.
If the Commercial stood on the same basis as the A.J.S.,
and had to pay interest on all its original deposits instead
of having turned £2,000,000 into preference capital,
its loss, on that memorable occasion, would have been
£71,000 against the A.J.S.’s £53,000. Therefore, if any
inference can be drawn from the relative sizes of things,
the A. J.S. would require to write off £900,000 capital to
get on an equally solid basis.
Compared with last half-year the note-circula-
tion has declined £35,000 ; deposits have decreased about
£200,000 ; and cash and other liquid assets about £200,000.
The cash in N.S.W. banks in the aggregate, however, has
decreased £1,215,000 in the last year, so the A. J.S. has
only shared the common fate. Apparently if the pastoral
industry and other things get worse the condition of the
A. J.S. is hopeless ; if they remain as bad as they are its
condition is very doubtful indeed ; if they improve to a
reasonable extent it should get through—though per-
haps without very much room to spare. And at present
they are so bad that it seems almost impossible for them
to avoid improving materially. Even at the best, how-
ever, the prospects of the shareholders appear to be
worth about half of nothing for a number of years yet.

There are some interesting deficits among these figures.
The City of Melbourne Bank, which was a feeble weed at
the best, died of its one shortage. The Colonial Bank is
now showing small profits. The B.N.Z. and the Commer-
cial of Australia are doing well by their last balance-
sheets. And as regards the London, E.S. and A., Queens-
land National and A.J.S. the public is waiting to see how
their new arrangements will pan out.

The Ballarat Banking Co. still pays its regular 5J per
cent, dividend (its yearning after that odd J per cent, is
unaccountable), but in view of the steady decline in pro-
fits, and the absence of any prospect of immediate im-
provement, it would do well to think seriously over the
position and reduce the div. to 5 per cent, anyhow. The
amount divided is £2337, and after adding £5OO to the
reserve, which now stands at £53,000, there remains £44 to
add to profit and loss, making £2464 carried forward.
The co. has £69,580 in cash and Government securities,
equal to 33 per cent, of all liabilities to the public, and
looks solid, well-managed and prosperous as usual.

Broken Hill Water Supply Co. (a Melbourne-
owned concern) earned £8879 net profit during the six
months ended June, ’97, and manfully splashed around
£9325 in paying a 10 per cent, dividend, so it naturally
goes £446 to the bad. The co. has about £150,000 of pro-
perty, and in case anything serious happens to this it has
the magnificent sum of £2500 put away to a contingency
account. The desire to pay 10 per cent, whatever hap-
pens, is an incurable complaint with the Melbourne
financier.

Melbourne Gras. Co. realised a profit of £36,444
last half-year, compared with £35,293 and £33,862 for two
previous six-monthly periods. A dividend of 8 per cent,
accounts for £34,288, leaving a balance of £2156. The
dividend is good enough, but, at each meeting, the dread-
fully misguided person who bought £5 gas shares at £l7
about the end of 1890 rises to wail that he is getting only
2J per cent., and asks that the co. will get more for its
gas, or make the meter sprint faster, or something. And
the meter already sprints so fast in the struggle to earn
adequate profits for this person that Melbourne is about
ready to insurrect against its Gas Co.

on capital. This is a Melbourne-owned concern, and its

* * * *
One more scandalous instance of somebody’s
gross stupidity is (Tub Bulletin hears) being carefully
kept out of sight. Last year the N.S.W. duty on methy-
lated spirit was fixed at one shilling per gallon. Some
harassed copy-clerk in an interval between rests made the
list read one penny per gallon. The duty was duly col-
lected as one penny per gallon for months before anyone
in the Govt, woke up enough to detect the error. Even
then, the united intelligence of and Minis-
try was unequal to the strain of putting the matter
straight. So they just go on collecting one penny per
gallon instead of. one shilling—-and importers don’t
grumble. Seeing how many hundreds of thousands of
gallons of methylated spirits are imported yearly, the loss
is no trifle—also, it now pays most handsomely to “fake”
methylated spirit into grog. Between muddled taxes and
muddled duties, “Nearest and Dearest” Reid will have a
fine skein to unravel when he gets his brain free from the
fumes of Himperial fizz.

Editor Bulletin, —Ramornie Meat Works were
honored in your issue of July 24 by a notice signed
“ Medicus,” in which it was stated that we have con-
tracted to supply an English firm with 72 tons of extract
of beef at 4s. (four shillings) per lb. That price is so high
for extract in bulk that the statement has attracted some
notice. May I just briefly say that “Medicus” is in-
accurate, and that, to the best of my belief, it is at present
(and is always likely to be) impossible to obtain anything
like 4s. per ib. for extract in bulk.—C. F. Tindal, Manager
Australian Meat Co., Ramornie. [That being so, how
colossal must be the profits of the middleman !—Ed. B.]

The Brunswick (Melb.) Gas Co. proposes to ex-
tend its mains to Fitzroy and Collingwood at a cost of
£60,000, and promises a better supply of superior gas at a
lower figure than the Metropolitan gives. Petitions from
the ratepayers in favor of the irruptive company are being
extensively signed. And yet, at last week’s meeting of
Met. shareholders, one more outcry against any reduction
in the gas-rate was raised by the inevitable juggins who
had bought his shares at an awful premium.
* * » *
The worst thing that ever happened to silver
within the memory of man struck it week, when the
price went down to 2s. 2%d. per oz.—the very lowest on
record. The next lowest figure was 2s. 3d. per oz. in
1894. Up to 1891 the lowest price recorded was 3s. s£d.
per oz. A comparatively small further drop—small, that
is, compared to what has taken place in the last six
years—would shut up Broken Hill for good unless
economy in production could be carried to a point which,
at present, looks absolutely impossible.
* * * *
The Inspector of Mines at Broken Hill gave the
Mine Accidents Committee sitting there a few illustra-
tions of the absurdity of the present Mining Regulations.
It is provided that boilers shall be tested periodically.
That is all—it isn’t mentioned to what point they shall be
tested ! Neither is it definitely provided that accidents
shall be reported; notwithstanding which the mines’
annual report purports to show them.
* * * *
July 20 was the second anniversary of the out-
break of the fire in Block 11 of the B. 11. Proprietary. On
damp days steam and smoke may still be seen rising in a
faint cloud from the broken timbers at the bottom of the
open-cut; but the burning section is so well closed in now
that the average cost of watching it and keeping the par-
titions in repairs amounts to only £22 per month.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, directors, in their half-yearly report,
say that in the six months to May 31 the lead averaged
a higher price than any realised during the past five years.
Thev anticipate a further advance. But as for silver they
admit that the future is most uncertain. As against the
preceding half-year, the rise in lead means a gain of
£8234 ; but the loss through the fall in silver in the same
period amounts to £20,576. The highest price realised at
any time by the co. for lead was £l5 Is. 6d. per ton ; last
half-year’s average was £ll 11s. 3d., to which it has
steadily recovered from £9 12s. The old fall was due to
the fillip to production of silver-lead ores caused by the
silver legislation of the States ; apart from the prodigious
quantity of silver-lead ores then mined, much lead ore,
even when it contained but little silver, was raised in
Mexico and elsewhere for mixing with the ores rich in
silver preparatory to furnacing. The steady advance in
the price of lead, extending over about four years, seems
to indicate that the stocks then accumulated have been
used up, and that normal conditions again prevail in the
lead-market. But, nevertheless, it is not reasonable to
expect the old level to be reached, seeing that in the last
eight or nine years the normal price-level of almost every-
thing has been brought lower The latest quoted price is
£l2 7s.
* • » *
On the plea of office economy B.H. Prop,
directors propose to distribute profits quarterly from
January next instead of two-monthly. What they don’t
say is whether the quarterly div. will be only as much as
the two-monthly has been. But shareholders may make
up their minds that it will come to that —unless some
quite unexpected development in connection with zinc-
extraction occurs. Last half-year, after paying the divs.
(£144,000) and the machinery bills (£43,000), there was
only three thousand odd to spare —and there would have
been an actual deficit if the Taxation Commissioners had
not made a refund. The directors intend to try and keep
the pot boiling by dealing in Westralian ore tellurides
chiefly—and treating them at Port Pirie, where, they say,
they have all the appliances ready.
• * » *
For labor contracts the B.H. Proprietary last
half-year paid £63,600; in mine wages and salaries,
£35,383. The figures show the extent to which the once
almost universal day-labor has been superseded.
• * * »
B.H. Proprietary’s profit in the last six months
is equal to 10%d. per oz. of silver extracted during the
period. In other words, the price of silver could fall
another 10|d. before all the profit w-ould disappear,
assuming no further economies could be made.
• * * *
Block 10 follows Block 14 and the B.H. Pro-
prietary by introducing horses for haulage underground.
• * * *
Block 10 makes just under 10s. per ton now for
its zinc “ tailings.”
* * * *
Of Broken Hill mines, Block 14 is shown to be
freest from accidents. Zeb. Lane’s influence still remains.
* * • *
What is really the matter with the Ashcroft
Sulphide Works near Newcastle ? Shares have fallen
considerably in London, and all sorts of rumors continue
to fly around without any definite contradiction coming
from the local managers. Probably they think it is none
of the public’s business. Undoubtedly something has
gone wrong with the works—timber has to be substituted
for iron in a considerable part of the establishment, and
the advertising for iron-ores long after starting would in-
dicate something else. The issuing of £lOO,OOO worth of
debentures in London was also, the writer imagines, not
contemplated when the works started.
* • * *
Over JB7OOO spent on the N.S.W. Govt, toy
metallurgical works at Clyde, and no provision for smelt-
ing. Why not publish a monthly return of the work
done ? Monkeying with an elephantine business.
* * * *
New Bushman’s mine, Parkes, N.S.W., has
made a successful first run with its cyanide plant. The-
story told of this mine by the capable mining-man of
Sydney D.T. has been confirmed by results as well as by
the silence of the directors on the subject.
Garibaldi, Hillgrove, N.S.W., applied for three
months’ suspension. The warden granted one month,
and declared that no further time would be given. Bull
is asked to “ put up or shut up.” Considering the amount
he has put up, he had better shut up. What has become
of the £20,000 working capital ? A detailed history of
this mine would give the outside world a startling
illustration of the ridiculous sacrifices which have to be
made in financing Australian mines on the London
market. Mining is not regarded in circles financial as a
legitimate industry.
Peel River Proprietary mine, Tamworth dis-
trict, N.S.W., offered for sale by auction, but failed to
effect a riddance. Few persons want to buy mines under
the hammer. Much money has been lost on this show,
worked off-and-on for many years without profit. Dick
Hamilton, now manager of the Great Boulder, once had
charge of it.
* * * ♦
Myall's United, a N.S.W. mine taken over by
Bull at a big cash-and-share price, some two years ago:
2900 tons, for 9320 z Ojdwts. per ton. Another
severe disappointment, the return being far less t han the
average by the former owners for many years. The big,
new battery has told the tale, indicating very big tons by
the old mill, or gutting the mine for the best ore, which
cannot now be done. No doubt even this return will pay
breaking and crushing exes., but it won’t pay divs. on a
capital of £200,000. Tonnage tells. For years it was
given out that this mine had averaged an ounce to the
ton. Now, when the mine is well-equipped with a big,
new battery, and everything else which a working-capital
of £70,000 commands, the first crushing is less than a third
of that produced by the owners’ “ rattle-trap,” and less
than half the yield foreshadowed by report. And, in the
face of these things, men and newspapers continue to pre-
dict a boom in London for N.S.W. mines !
* • » *
Bluey mine, Stewart’s Brook, N.S.W.: 552 tons
crushed during the half year for 150 oz.—not good enough
to pay, and about enough work has been done and money
lost on this show to “jack” it, as a mine having a fine
reef only wanting another few dwts. to the ton to make
it worth working. The present co.—the third—proposes
to try another level.
• • • *
The Lucknow (N.S.W.) strike has come, owing
to the big English co. seeking to reduce miners’ wages to
425. per week—3s. less than the current Vic. rate, hitherto
the lowest in Australia.
* * * * %
Rumored that the Beacon Co. (London), work-
ing the N.S.W. mine formerly called Taylor’s Reward, has
declared a Is. div.—£lo,ooo. It will take about ten to
return the money put up by the Exploration Co. on this
deal. The character of that great and remorseless organi-
sation is shown by the rapid way they got to work on this
mine.

* * * *
Long Tunnel (Vic.) has cut the reef 2J*ft. thick
at 1822 ft. —100 ft. lower than hitherto worked, showing
good gold. That this run of gold should have gone from
the surface to such a depth without a blank makes it the
most wonderful mine of Australia up to date. How much
of its gold has gone down the river ?
* * * *
In asking for six months’ exemption for the
Lady Loch and Forest King mines, Coolgardie, Fred.
Monger showed how the money had vanished. The
£15,000 working capital for the first had all gone, and
£35,000 had been spent on the Lady Loch —£20,000 from
capital, and £15,000 from gold won. The directors had
been led to think the mines would be self-supporting, the
whole of the funds had been exhausted, the directors had
personally subscribed £2OOO to pay wages, and now prayed
for time to reconstruct. The Warden granted three
months. And they still dream about cheap mining in
W.A. ! The boom was hot enough about a year ago to
give the Lady Loch a market-value —whew !—of over
£250,000.
* * • •
It has more than once happened in Westralia
that the rich mine of to-day is one of the poorest of to-
morrow, and in one or two very notable instances it has
been the other way about. One of the first W.A. mines
floated in England was Mawson’s Reward, at Dundas. It
was taken over by the Goldfields Exploration Co. A bat-
tery proved it for a time a duffer. Having a solid work-
ing capital, and its parent co. having cleared a heap of
money on other floats, it kept going. The shares are now
about par, 60 men are working in the mine, the boss says
he has nearly 20,000 tons in sight which will average
nearly an ounce per ton, and the writer hopes it is all
true.
• * * •
Brookman, at Kalgoorlie, has, probably, found
out by now that it is easier to talk production than to
make It. Instead of 500 tons of five-oz. rock weekly from
the Associated mines being sent to Dry Creek, it seems to
be 200 tons of ore of a little less than half the estimated
value—and this after putting men almost on top of one
another to break it.
* * * *
Reconstruction of co’s owning individual mines
in W.A., and the amalgamation of exploration co’s loaded
to the neck with worthless mines they cannot float, still
go on slowly in London. The Venture Co., which adver-
tises mines for sale in the W.A. papers, proposes to amal-
gamate with the nine other similar co’s with which it has
been associated. Excuses given—saving in administration
exes., a reduction of liability on shares, and a freer
market for the new co. It paid an enormous div. by the
sale of mines in the boom, and it has sufficient assurance
left to say that the mines left on its hands are developing
satisfactorily.
* * * *
The Golden Cliff, Mt. Margaret district, Cool-
gardie, is a big ironstone outcrop, purchased from the
prospectors for £30,000, and floated in London for
£300,000. Now that the boom is over it may go some-
thing under lOdwt. per ton, and will have to pay in
dividends £30,000 a year for 15 years to give hack the
principal with 5 per cent, interest.
* » •
A Coolgardie paper: “We have 16 payable
mines in the district!” That isn’t many out of 2000 or
more mining leases on Coolgardie proper. And this after
three years’ gasconading, the destruction of reams of
paper in their praise, and the swallowing of truck-loads
of champagne towards their success.
» * * *
Burbank’s mine, within a mile or two of the
Londonderry, Coolgardie, is looking very well in the
bottom levels from the three main shafts- 170 ft Ten
heads running, another ten going up to be ready in two
months. Water slowly increasing, and expect to have
enough to run the full plant by the time it is ready.
About nine months’ reserves for 20 stamps opened up,
and there is reason to believe that the 2oz. average can
be maintained. Prof. Nicholas still in command—a good
thing for the co. Burbank’s is one of the very few mines
of the Coolgardie field that has improved by develop-
ment. Is it on account of tellurides, of which the reefs
just show enough to swear by?
* * * *
An offer is made through Clarke & Co. to take
the Boulder Central mine, Kalgoorlie, at £30,000 cash
and 15,000 shares in a £lOO,OOO new co.
* • * *
The disclosures made at the General Gordon
shareholders’ meeting, held recently in Melbourne, show
how cheaply some mines are run in W.A. In a year
£22,000 had been spent, and there was a 10-head mill and
a winding plant to show for it. One manager had been
changed for another. It took one man three months to
prepare a report, and it would probably take shareholders
six to understand it. The broker of the co. said he was
disgusted, and motions indicative of dissatisfaction were
passed. The writer has a sort of hazy idea that he fore-
shadowed these things quite a year ago, and was for so
doing much condemned by the very people who are now
spitting fire and vengeance. Crushed at last 410 tons for
31oz. 15dwt. The gold is “in the tailings,” as usual, and
shareholders are in the soup.
* * * »
Clarke and Co. (Adelaide) have another special
Westralian issue of t heir Investors’ Guide, and the tone
of this handy publication is joyous, not to say jubilant.
“ It is quite within the bounds of possibility that W.A.,
at the end of this year, will stand second only to Vic.
amongst the gold-producing provinces of Australia, and
it is more than probable that bv the end of 1898, Victoria
itself will be headed.” There is comfort in the general
remark that “ wild-cats are being thoroughly sifted out.”
A wild-cat, when sifted, remains much the same as
before so far as shareholders are concerned, and it
doesn’t appear under a savage feline heading in Clarke’s
catalogue, but the moral results of a thorough sift are
doubtless splendid in a market sense, or something of
that sort.

beware !
* • • *
The N.S.W, Co-Operative Wool and Produce
Co. Ltd. is a concern which, having brains equalling its
ambitions, must revolutionise the wool-broking business
of this country. The big wool ring is an uncombined
combination for joint selfish interest; its members ap-
parently helping and trusting, but secretly plotting
against and frustrating each other. The Co-Operative
has now gone into the heart of the enemy’s camp by secur-
ing At wool stores on Circular Quay, Sydney, and as it
offers the producer advantages which the ring does not
afford, the only stumbling block in the Co-Operative’s
progress is the conservative stupidity of the producer
itself. The co., however, will educate the producer up to
its cardinal principle “ that co-operation dispenses with
the services of all unnecessary and profit-making agents,”
and it will enforce this principle by paying every pro-
ducer top market value, and charging only 3 per cent.,
inclusive commission, as against the ring's charge of 3 per'
cent, plus %d. per lb.—which brokers’ charge, on account
of the J4d. bids, really means 3 per cent, plus ]4d. per lb.
So that on an average bale of 6d. per lb. wool the Co-
Operative’s charge is 6s. ; whilst the ring’s charge means
14s. 4d.

The Australasians in ‘ Queen Victoria’s
Ootomal Escort,’commanded by Col. Lassetter,”
witites Ihe Bulletin’s London correspondent,
were Sergeants O’Grady, Williams, M‘Allister,
V\ augh, Corporal Munsie, Troopers Riley, Dooley
and Private Muntz, of the N.S.W. M.R. and
Dancers ; Capt. Nichol, Sergt. Strong, Privates
btandish, Phillips, Prowse, M‘Lellan, Comrie
Paten, and Somerville, of the Vic. M.R • Sersrt
Somerville, Corpl. Cardale, Privates Taranaki Te

Nobblett, Privates Francis and Major, of theS.A.
fhl n 5 Tr d D H e ms , and Private Hade, of
the Q. M.R. though the escort, they were
not within cooee of her Gracious, and the
reason was palpable, for since the bushwhackers
landed they have been the Jubilee for the peoples
of the street. Their reception in the East End at
the military parade was marvellous, and the men
will never forget the way the crowd cheered, and
powerm]. females smacked the horsemen’s taut
thighs as they waited in the many halts. The
outer files were sore and bruised for two or three
days afterwards, but the rough good-nature of the
assault excused much, and after the supercilious
havv-hawism of British officers, it was like a
rub-down with a rough towel to drive out the chill
ot contemptuous patronage. To have put the
colomals close to the Queen would have taken
the tat out of her reception, and left only the suet-
strings, and the management of the Jubilee jaunt
could not have been excelled by the most experi-
enced circus menagerie-owner—it was perfect.
“Speculators bought vacant sites at fabulous
prices, and in one case dragged down a busy build-
l-pfT> T order to erect stands for the wealthy
Ameiicans, Australians, &c., who were ex-
pected to sport their sox ’to see the boss throne-
continues our correspondent. “ Trades-

HE: “ Are you going in evening-dress to-night?”
SHE: “No.”
HE (airing his French): “Oh, I see—simply ‘au naturel.’”—(And then he wondered.)
men opened at 4 a.m., and crowds of people slept
on the route all night. Yet the former did not pay
for gas, the latter could have slept the day through
gnd remained unmolested and undisturbed, for
when the panorama passed there were hardly two
million persons on roof-top, stand, or street that
witnessed the show. Shopkeepers who, six days
before, had replaced their window-goods by a
wooden flight of steps, labelled at two guineas a
15in. space, were glad to tout ricketty pavement-
posers in at the last moment for a few shillings.
Everyone fearing hunger had bought a pocket-
lunch or a hamper, and the big cafes were crowded
with waiters cursing for the Jubilee tips that
came not. The bun-and-tea shops were battle-
merited with sandwiches, scones, cake and
pastry, which the expected hosts made little
perceptible breach in. The buns have by now
become cobble - stones, the sandwiches leather
triangles, useful for tiling, and some of the pro-
prietors bankrupts, or men of sin and violence. It
was such a record slump as might be experienced
if one built a pagoda on the clouds of Paradise and
the floor fell out, landing him midst the desolate
embers of a deserted hell.
“The Jubilee slump will be handed down to
history as the most colossal ‘ busted ’ bubble since
the rampant days of the South Sea speculations.
For weeks and weeks the papers have fairly
frizzled with the figures of abstruse and, more
frequently, obtuse calculations as to the people
who would fight tooth and nail and swear and
gasp and die in their loyal attempts to catch but a
glimpse of the royal pageant. The millions would
faint and collapse from thirst and hunger ; the
restaurants and bars would be invaded and
wrecked by maddened throngs. One scribe
discovered that thousands would be suffocated by
the air exhaled from the myriads, another that
only one in ten would see more than his predeces-
sor’s neck and samples of assorted ears, and a
third floated out in a sea of billions and other
illions on a raft composed of his own cheek, lashed
together by impudence, and discovered that more
wood was used in jubilee stands than had reached
the country during the last five years.”
* * * *
An Australian in England to The Bulle-
tin :
At the Duke of York’s school, Chelsea, a queer incident
occurred. The Yorks and Cambridge were proceeding to
dinner after the ceremony of presenting flags, and the
various officers around the sacred person were invited to
lunch, including the Indians, but excluding the Aus-
tralians. The crowd disappeared into the building, leav-
ing the Australians standing alone, with all eyes on them.
Presently an officer emerged rapidly and, seeing the Aus-
tralians, rushed over to them, spoke to them, ran inside,
stayed several minutes, rushed out again very red in the
face, and took the Australians from outer darkness into
the sweetness and light of May and George.
One of the features of the “ little Jubilee procession ”
(viz., the colonial Premiers and other outsiders sent
ahead to frighten small boys) was the enthusiasm of
George Turner, waving his cocked hat over his head
with great good humor. ( One of the Premieresses, by the
way, kissed her hand right and left to the crowd !) At
every fresh burst of cheering the Vic. Premier went at it like
a marionette, while Premier Reid, who occupied the same
carriage, frowned between his official smiles and turned a
severe eye-glass on him—eventually trying to look as if
Turner was a stranger who had been thrust into the same
carriage accidentally by one of the Imperial bunglers.
The best figure in the “ little procession ” was Laurier, a
pale, cold man, with a hard, intelligent face, who sat
erect and haughty, acknowledging the cheers slightly
and condescendingly. He has been much fussed over,
ported the Austra,ians have been very imperfectly re-
Seddon’s bluff hearty, style seems to have impressed
those who have met him here, and to have disgusted all
those who eonsider Oxford or Cambridge “ culehavv ” in
dispensable to a colonial Premier. He seems to have
had the right word on all occasions.
There was much comment on the stand I was on with
reference to the. loose, swinging, graceful wav the Aus-
tralians rode so preferable to the “ swallowed-t lie-poker ”
The Haussas, Chinese and black contingents from fhe
outskirts of the Empire have a trying time in England.
STERN NECESSITY.
STOPPERS : “Hello! Bludger. Wot, are you working now? ”
BLUDGER : “ Yes, Bill; but 'tain’t my fault —the ole ooman’s in the orspitel!”
The small boy in squadrons falls upon them wherever he
meets them and indulges in grave and cordial shakes of
the hands. _ To compensate for this, hardly a dark or
yellow soldier is to be seen at night without a nice
London girl, hanging proudly and confidingly on his arm.
The London policeman carried off the honors of the
Jubilee. He is certainly a benevolent deity, and a won-
derful manager of crowds, his good humor helping him
where the official snarl of authority of the Australian-
Irish bobby would set the crowd against him. He does
not force, he manages. You ask him how he manages
some nine millions so easily and he modestly sums it all
up in the apothegm—“ Well, you see, sir, the London
crowd’s a crowd that’s used to a crowd. So thev’re easy
to manage.”
There was some humor in the official who put the
Chinese envoy and the Pope’s special envoy in the same
carriage in the procession.
The London ’bus companies must have netted fortunes
by raising the fares 600 and 1200 per cent, during the
Jubilee week. The seat speculators did not come out so
well in all instances, and the prices were preposterous.
The people are being “ God-save-the Queened ” to death.
Bands, street organs, penny-whistles, going all the time,
street-singers, &c. Last night I saw a crowd of 300 people
crossing the Albert Bridge, Battersea, singing the “ Ana-
thema” while a girl went ahead turning handsprings.
As jubilee-honor time drew near the hearts
of many Westralians beat high with hope. Acting-
Premier Wittenoom, placed at the head of the
Cabinet for the first time, had been starring with
great energy, and expected something, while
Attorney-General Burt, the oldest member of the
Westralian Ministry except Forrest, hoped for a
share of whatever was going. Justice Stone and
Saunders, M.L.C., Mayor of Perth, both ex-
pected knighthoods, while President Shenton, of
the Leg. Council, went to England so that he
should not be overlooked. Forrest was to get a
G.C.M.G. in addition to whatever general honors
were handed round to the Premiers. When ex-
pectation was at its height the cable announced—
Forrest’s P.C. -ship and one wretched C. M. G. -ship.
Loyal leading citizendom felt like the obsequious
waiter who is given no tip, or the cabbie paid in
the coin of Bishop Smith. Now there is deep
silence in loyal circles in Perth, save for the voice
of the lawyer-politician who supported the Govern-
ment and “expected silk,” and was disappointed.
* » * *
At the Duke of York’s School ceremony,
when Princess May presented the new colors, the
Australian troops marched into the grounds on
the tail-end of squad of darkies, in this order
Indians, first; Haussas, second ; Australians,
third. The Australians (writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent) evidently felt their degraded position.

An old Queenslander writes to The Bulle-
tin from England :
At the trooping of the colors by the Duke of York’s
Boys Brigade at the military tournament on 28th Mav,
when the colors were marched down the hall every soldier
m the place raised his hand in salute with the exception
of the Australians, who made no sign. Jn one box there
were stweral officers of cavalry, artillery, etc., and one
.N.S.W. officer in a sort of snuff-colored uniform, forage-
cap and broad gold-laced belt. The N.S.W. officer was
slightly in front of the others, and so did not see that
they saluted the colors each time they passed, or perhaps
he would have followed suit. This was remarked by
everyone in the place and put down to ignorance.

Perth (W.A.) City Council used to hold its
sittings m an informal and business-like fashion-
councillors sitting round a table, with the Mayor
unfrilled, unfurred, and unfeathered, at the head
The flummery of .1 übilee got into the air, however,
and the meeting-room was arranged after the
fashion of the local Leg. Council Chamber all
sitting round the r,.om, wi h the table in the
centre ; to honor the improvement, the Mayor and
town clerk Mere 1 educated to appear robed. The
municipal millinery is worn now. Also the coun-
cillors provided themselves with special gold watch-
pendant Jubilee medals, and there was great wrath
and a debate in the council when it was found t hat
the enterprising jeweller had sold extra med ds
from the same die to unauthorised persons. Men
—they are moie vain than women !

The delicate wit of the city councillor.
While Perth (W.A.) City Council was engaged in
naming certain streets, one councillor suggested
(he name of another member as appropriate for a
certain street, and added that it was famous for
the number of houses of ill-fame it contain-d.
then the count-,llor whom it was sought to honor
rose and snorted “Coward, scoundrel, liar ! ”
Rockhampton (Q.) papers report the local
Jrange 12th of July sliivoo as “commemorating
the anniversary of the Battle.of the Boyne.” Said
glorious and immortal scrimmage was fought on
the Ist of July, and the nearest loyal Orange
episode worth mentioning to the 12th is the
anniversary of Aughrini. There Ginkell, the
Dutchman, and St. Ruth, the Frenchman
argued the point, James and William being both
absent. 1 urther, the native Irish wore yellow, or
orange, and the rest of James’s troops had white
tor the l r distinguishing color, while the Duteh-
VVilham crowd flourished green decorations, so
tilings have got, very badly mixed nowadays. The
old Irish 1 Sunburst banner ” would now only
serve to rouse the patriotic Dooley or Mick Pagav
to assault and batthery. °

Light o’ Love, O be kind to me ;
Kiss me awhile and begone, my dear;
Else if you linger, it well may be
Sony the fruit that your kisses bear.
Lighter is Love, doth the proverb say,
Than the jewelled shape of a butterfly ;
When he folds his wings in one’s heart to stay,
Leaden with sorrow his soft plumes lie.
Zadig.

Sydney Telegraph publishes an article
about the advisability of every householder leaving
the scraps and spare food in a clean box at the
back gate for the starving poor—the box to have a
lid to keep off dogs. It is four years now since
Reid guaranteed to abolish the unemployed in a
week or thereabouts,' and the country has so far
advanced on the road to that desirable consumma-
tion that it recognises the advisability of letting
the gaunt,wayfarer get the first show at the scraps,
and leaving the dog for a secondary consideration.

lb was a beautiful advertising - fake for
Sydney Catholic Press (the priests’ paper, be-
gqcra) to get its new machinery publicly
blessed before starting, and so deeply moved
was, this ungodly rag at an invitation to
the function, that it had taken up its hat to
comply when it was stayed by the thought that
it might be thereafter expected to submit its own
unhallowed plant to the bell book-and-eandle
business. When The Bulletin feels like taking
any benison, it will duly advertise in the “reli-
gious” papers for tenders. The lowest or any
tender not necessarily accepted.
* * » *
at the St. Vincent de Paul meeting in
Sydney, the other day, the Salvation Army’s
work was frankly and warmly commended by R.C.
clergymen;-who further asserted that if their own
church did not bestir herself she would be left far
behind in reclamatory and benevolent operations
amongst the slums. The Blood-and-Fire people
must indeed..be making things lively, thus to get
ahead of the most systematically and sincerely
charitable of all the churches. The R.C. priest is
about the only clergyman ever known to die penni-
less—because his charity doesn’t begin at home.
Two ladies, driv-
ing along the Bath-
urst (N.S. W.) Road
recently, overtook a
small boy trudging
wearily along on bare
feet.' When given a
“lift,” it was found
he had-walked from
Lawson to The Valley
(twelve miles) to buy
a loaf of bread, which
was a penny cheaper
there. Twenty - four
miles to save a penny !

The charity of
people living on the
Bathurst-road, the
main route from Syd-
ney to the West, is
sorely taxed by
tramps. The other
day a resident of
Springwood, annoyed
by the frequent re-
quests for meat and
drink, refused one old
swagtnan. Later, he
passed the same man
seated by the road-
side, dipping a few
dry crusts into a billy
of cold water. When
questioned, swaggie
said the crusts were
the last of a loaf of
bread given him in
Bathurst, 80 miles
away. The resident,
filled with remorse,
took him back to the
house,' fed him, and
sent him away with a
full tucker-bag.

According to a C umberland Times (N. S. W.)
contributor, it has been ordered that the works of
certain classic authors are to be vigorously ex-
purgated before being lent to prisoners from the
N.S.-W. gaol libraries. “ The work of expurgation
is now being carried out —the work of clipping
pages and half-pages out of Scott, Dickens,
Thackeray, Lvtton, Byron, and Morris, by a clerk
with a scissors, at a charge of ten bob a day to the
country. Having been in gaol recently I am, of
course, prepared to say that this bright, unclerical
youth has expurgated the whole of the prison
scene—Pickwick in the Marshalsea, for the ver-
dict and costs in the Bardell versus Pickwick
breach of promise action —from the Master’s great
work with a common scissors, and the best-
known works of the other authors have been
treated with even less consideration.”
* * * *
The Council of the Churches (N.S.W.) has
protested to the Friendly Societies Association
against the use of Sunday for the association’s
annual hospital demonstration. The C.C. is
grievously exercised, it says, over the extra Sun-
day labor involved, and doesn’t appear to consider
that the Scriptural dictum about the ass that falls
into a pit on the Sawbath applies to the human
ass—the ass that believes these holy men are ha’.f
as much concerned about the “ violence to the
consciences of church-members ” as they are about
the violence to the collection-plate. The F.S.A.
meeting, hovvever, decided, by 64 votes to 5, that
it wasn’t taking any saintly slush just then.
* * * «
Melbourne dailies had stirring accounts of
the disbandment of the Rupertswood Battery (the
late Baronet Clarke’s military toy), which would
lead one to believe that half the population of the
country was present at the harrowing ceremony,
bathed in bitter and angry tears. But the wretched
weeklies dispelled the illusion with pathetic
photos, showing the abandoned heroes, a dozen or
so, drawn up in a 40-acre paddock somewhere on
the baronial estates of Clarke, and the indignant
public is represented by two girls leaning over a
fence, and a ribald small boy on a log eating a
carrot. It is the great draw-back to the camera
as an adjunct to the press of the day that it cannot
lie —much.
* * * *
The young Hambourg brothers are, in
private life, almost as boyishly unaffected as
Master Betty, the “Infant Roscius” of yore.
They talk cricket with enthusiasm, and take a
lively interest in the Melbourne football struggles
between Collingwood and South, &c. In contrast
to older and inferior performers, who wear their
blessed Art on their sleeves, these two born musi-
cians are quite refreshing. A new-chum tells of
how he met them at a friend’s house, not long ago,
and, figuratively speaking, patted Jakoff Ham-
bourg on the head after conversing with the young-
ster re. his brothers piano-practice. Then Jakoff
obliged the company with a violin solo, and the
visitor felt appalled at his former irreverence. In
the atmosphere of revealed genius the boy loomed
ten feet high.
* * * *
At Port Moresby (New Guinea), the other
day, six young native girls pleaded guilty before
a white magistrate to a charge of theft. As they
were rather young to send to prison, his worship
took each offender in turn across his knee and
spanked her.

A late number of the Clarion (Eng.) sug-
gests to needy Treasurers the establishment of an
order of “ Respectable people, entrance into
OVERHEARD AT THE CLUB.
CYNIC: “I hear that ass Charlie's turned Benedict."
EPICURE: “Poor devil — who’s his Benedictine?”
which could be obtained by payment of a stiff fee.
Although proposed jestingly, it is singular to note
that the very word “ respectable ” arises from the
circumstance that Constantine established it as the
second of the three orders of “nobility” at the
time of the founding of Constantinople. The
three classes were, according to Gibbon, the Illus-
trious, the Respectable, and the Honorable.
There’s little doubt that if the thing were seri-
ously proposed, there’d be a helter-skelter rush by
Sordiddom generally for the “ dignity. ” Or sup-
pose that instead of the “ Respectable ” we made
it “ The Nicest ” ?

The distressed Melb. ex - maiden who
declared that a local dentist had drugged her
when she was in the chair, and that a baby was
the consequence, now goes back on her sensational
tale of woe, and says that an anesthetic was not
employed, and that she had agreed and whispered
“Yes” to the toothpuller. “Melbourne men,”
remarked an eminent Q.C., the other day, “should
have discovered by this that ‘a verbal agreement
is not worth the paper it is written upon.’ It is
wise to get consent in writing.” Later news is
that the lady now says that it wasn’t the dentist
at all, but somebody else, miles away.
* * * *
That Melb. girl’s story, since admitted to
be fabulous, about the outrageous dentist who
became father of her child after only two inter-
views, recalls the yarn about the unkempt news-
boy brought before Dr. Lloyd, the North Melb.
beak. “ Who’s your father, my boy?” demanded
Lloyd of the tearful prisoner. “Dunno, sir,” was
the reply. “What!” exclaimed the court,
“ Don’t know who your father is?” “No, sir,”
explained the lad, “ mother only seen him onct.”

The recent case of a woman stripping her-
self stark-naked in the presence of a Melbourne
court, and calling on all and sundry to compare
her with artists’ models and sculptors’ dreams, is
by no means new. A similar occurrence, with the
same word-accompanirnent, was recorded in Eng-
land a few years ago, and was then said to have
been imitated from France.
* * * *
The case of Ah Kow. The other day four
lepers were shipped to China from the quarantine
station at Queenscliff (Vic.), the most shocking of
them being a 15st. remnant, whose eyes, ears,
nose, gums, teeth and hands and feet have all dis-
appeared. “ When the Chinese sailors on board
the Whampoa saw the state of their fellow
countryman they gave him a wide berth, and
would render no assistance in getting him on
board. Fortunately for the leper he had a brother
with him, and this relative, a powerful man over
60 years old, lifted him and carried him up the
gangway on to the forecastle, depositing his
burden in his bunk.” The brotherly affection
shown by this Chinese is remarkable. For years
past he has been in constant attendance on the
stricken man (his elder brother) nursing and feed-
ing him when no one else would go near the leper,
if he could possibly avoid it. His name is Ah
Kow.
* * » •
Melbourne has just missed a beautiful sen-
sation. The trouble centred round a lady who
arrived at the hospital in a bad state, and ex-
plained that her husband had forced her to drink
poison in a wine-shop—presumably because he
wanted to marry something with yellow hair, and
found his wife in the road. She gave the address
and the exact hour of the tragedy, and things were
going all right till it transpired that the old man
had been arrested on a charge of larceny and
locked up some hours before the alleged incident.
Then she tried to alter the hour and locate the
horror in a different wine-shop, but her story
couldn’t recover its original coherence, and she
presently acknowdedged that she took the poison
herself for reasons that she couldn’t identify with
any certainty, and accused her husband on general
principles and hysterics. Therefore, that particu-
lar house-father is probably very glad he was
locked up. Being in gaol is the only alibi, bar
one, which is absolutely beyond suspicion. Being
dead is the other one.

FRATER, AYE ATQUE YALE !
Upon the sand he waved his hand,
We waving back in merry strain ;
A careless kiss, a gay good-bye—
We only part to meet again !
We sailed away that sweet spring day,
And out across the harbor bar;
The sun shone bright, the waves shone white.
The snowy mountains glittered far.
The cliffs rose grim, the pier grew dim,
And faint the line of bush-clad shore ;
O idle laughter ! light farewell!
W e part, who meet not any more !
In vain, in vain ! we sailed again
Across the angry harbor bar ;
The sun shone bright, the waves leaped white,
The snowy mountains glittered far.
No welcome word that day we heard,
Nor any greeting smile we had ;
A funeral bier across the pier
Was borne in silence, slow and sad.
His bed is deep and sound his sleep,
Beneath his own dear trees, I wis,
Beneath his pines and matipos
And ugaios twined with clematis.
Farewell, farewell! no passing bell
Disturbs their rest who sleep like thee.
Sleep, brother, sleep ! thy bed is deep,
Thy face is towards the open sea.
M.L. Dora Wilcox

Dear Bulletin, —“ H.” misses the point
re front-names. Even John “King” Smith
illustrates a common rule of nomenclature, but I
doubt the acceptance by priest or parson of “ King
John Smith.” As for the R.C. rule, instance a
friend of mine who was inadvertently taken to a,
registrar first and saddled with three “family”
names, for which the humble praenomen Joseph
had to be substituted at the church ceremony.
Invariably called “Joe” in the domestic circle,
but signs all documents in his registered name.—
T.JJ.
* * » ♦
Dear Bulletin, —A recent paragraph re
queer names reminds me that within a mile of
each other in one part of the Hawkesbury district
reside three fruitgrowers, respectively named Veal,
Pye, and Pigg. Apropos a statement which has
been made re the difficulty of inducing clergymen
to acquiesce in the conferment by parents of
strange Christian names upon their children, no
such difficulty, apparently, stood in the way of
Mr. Dan Mayne, of Bathurst (N.S.W.) Sentinel,
two of whose children bear the front names of
“ Colonel ” and “ Cardinal ” respectively ; whilst a
daughter, since deceased, was christened “ Mar-
chioness.” Dan explained to the curious that it
occurred to him it would sound well to hear that
“ Colonel Mayne took the chair at this meeting,”
or “Amongst the visitors to the course was Car-
dinal Mayne ”—and so on.— Oxberrtite.

A LAY OF WESTRALIA.
E¥ a starving wretch is scooting
With sixpennyworth of loot,
And he will not heed the summons
Of a number fourteen boot,
Then, like skin-and-vermin trapper
After bear and bandicoot,
Our bold and fearless bobby
Has the privilege to shoot!
If too indisposed or lazy
To betake him to the track,
He can plead he thought, with terror,
That the rogue was coming back;
So he coolly sights the runner,
And you hear the rifle crack
As he shoots— O shades of brave men !
The poor devil in the back.
Then twelve “ men ” sit on his carcase
Just to squeeze out why he died ;
While the bobby tells his story
With a pardonable pride—
And the coroner’s decision
Is “ Just murder —justified ” !
Hut the Blood cries up to Heaven
That that coroner has lied !
GEMININ.
