A WOMAN’S LETTER .
T . ~ Sydney, August 3, 1897.
Mt Dear Myee—
Sydney Society ” woke up with a sudden start
last week and rushed around to every sort of
festivity it knew. There were so many functions
on that the Nicest had to keep going strong for
the whole time to get through. The various balls
culminated in the Freemasons’ on the 28th, and
the gaudiest sight in the Centennial Hall and its
crowded balconies was Speaker Abbott with all
his Masonic emblems and embroidery hanging
around his manly form. Mayor Ives was a good
runner-up for gorgeousness. It was the men’s
night-out for apparel and glitter. After deep
consideration, I vote the man the more successful
displayer of gewgaws. As an animated jewel-stand
he gets in more awe-inspiring effect than the
woman; there is more of him to make a back-
ground, and he has no long, bright-hued draperies
to confuse the effect. The general impression was
that the Masons managed their party well.
Small economies seldom rule a ball arranged by
men. Hosts rarely spoil the ship for a penn’orth
of tar as hostesses sometimes do.
Mrs. A. Marks, who possesses a daughter Ruby
with a well-trained soprano voice, gave an assem-
bly of guests at the Y.M.C.A. Hall an opportunity
of hearing that young lady sing one afternoon last
week. It was a very fashionable flutter, and the
concert was a success. Miss Marks has already
made a name as a drawing-room singer. Miss
Blanche Thomas supplied the contralto. Mr.
Alfred Hill sang his new song, and Mr. Lawrence
Philip, without whom no recent programme seems
to be complete, let off some effective piano fire-
works.
The shipping companies united their efforts and
house-flags in Paddington Town Hall, last Friday
night, and made their annual ball hum satisfac-
torily with the aid of an untiring band and an
average amount of sustenance. The news of the
loss of life on the Tasmania had not then arrived
to damp the dancers’ spirits, and Huddart-
Parker’s flag floated merrily in festoon along with
the others. The dressing was of the penny plain
order. Girls faced the music in unfresh frocks
with damaged trails of gum-and-calico flowers.
Two or three women showed grey poplin exteriors
a depressing night-color, but a showy texture. A
happy-looking woman in a pink frock and an
obvious state of honeymoon, got all her dances,
and seemed as much “in it” as anybody. An
attractive young thing in a heliotrope frock with
up-to-date no-sleeves added years to her appear-
ance by doing her tresses & la artless little Flo—
no girl out of her teens can wear her hair hanging
down her back convincingly.
The recent craze for shop-made violets was a
fashion fresh born of the Jubilee. Nine ball-
gowns out of ten this season were thus adorned ;
the emblem of modesty in mauve muslin came
high, but wearers were bound to have it. Conse-
quently, the supply of millinery violets ran out iA
two provinces, and there would have been money
in making a corner in the unassuming blossoms
had any enterprising importer formed the boom.
There isn’t much decorative value in violets ; they
are too small, and purple is an unbecoming tint,
anyhow. Brunettes are reading with a sinking
heart the accounts from ’Ome that at all the much-
millinered festivities of last month mauve and
green were to the fore —two bilious tints that can’t
even be painted up to, and don’t match any hair
with brown pigment in it. But dark and fair
alike rushed violets, and now on the evening frocks
of yesterday a few wilted sprays and scatterings
of long green stalks with crushed little purple
knobs at the end still show the dying-out craze.
On Saturday night, in Pitt-street, people were
thronging to two “first nights” and a Govt.
House partly-patronised amateur show at the
Lyceum, and yet I suppose, at that moment, there
wasn’t a thoroughfare in the whole world harder
to get along in. What with the telephone excava-
tion at Park-street, gloomily canvas-covered like a
fresh-dug grave, and the sandy wastes on the side-
walk, when it isn’t wet cement, and the building
going on, which allows but a few inches for pedes-
trians to walk round the scaffolding—for miscel-
laneous and assorted examples of the condition
which the principal street of a big city ought not
to be in, Pitt-street, from King to Market streets,
holds a world’s record.
They threw planking across the pavement in
front of the Lyceum on Saturday night, and only
a few acres of sandy desert outside were trans-
ferred to the stair carpetings. A fairly large
audience, with a long row of notables in the front
of the d.c., sat out “ Duty,” played by the Sydney
Dramatic Club for the fashionable charity of the
hour. Most of the audience had turned up in the
spirit indicated by the title, but roused themselves
during the second act to the consciousness that the
stage-folk were warming up to a surprisingly good
performance. “Duty,” like all dramas bearing
the Sardou trade-mark, goes for a third-act climax
which has to be worked up to intelligently all
through. There were one or two lamentable cir-
cumstances in the cast, such as the moth-eaten
wig of the chief butler, and the hero’s keeping on
his silk hat while he hugged the walking-lady in
his own drawing-room, though the lady was pretty
and well-frocked enough to justify this lapse of
memory about his topper. Perhaps the two most
brightly-played parts in the best all-round per-
formance which the S.D.C. has yet given were
the Uncle Hammond of C. W. Anson and Mrs.
Trelawney Smith of Miss Mabel Hogg.
Vast crowds of smartly-attired women, sporting
the local sky-blue in hat-trimmings and neckties,
thronged the stands at the Cricket Ground last
Saturday, and took an absorbing interest in the
football-match. One interested spectator was
Sylvia Grey (Mrs. R. L. Fenwick, booked for
London by Monday’s P. and O.), who, dressed in
dark-blue cloth, a cream lace cravat, and hat tilted
at the side, chatted in front of the grand-stand,
and displayed her pretty shoes—but Sylvia Grey’s
feet could only be done justice to by a poetical
man-admirer in a reminiscent sonnet recalling her
“ cigarette” dance and her “statue’’dance, and all
the other measures in which those tootsies tripped
into fame. In the vice-regal pen sat some new-
comers—Sydney possesses several distinguished
visitahs just now—a nephew of the Secretary of
State for the Colonies, with the Chamberlain
clean shave, but without the family orchid and
eye-glass, also detached members of neighboring
suites. A naval beauty-man crowned his many
inches with a “ Butler ” cap. A girl in an 1840
bonnet with border of pink roses, successfully
proved that it requires a “ profile ” to set off that
quaint style of headgear.
Spangles and spoof are the chief stock-in-trade
of the Gay Parisienne as pourtrayed by Miss Ada
Willoughby, also a knowledge of high-kicking.
It seems that in gay Paree every girl must learn
to take off the hat of every man she meets with
her shoe-tip, or else she is not considered properly
introduced—judging, that is, by the stage French
girl. The gowns of the newest and gayest Parisi-
enne are really rapturous; in the first, which is
all rosy-pink where it isn’t steely-blue, and gleams
with tinsel wherever tinsel can be sewn on, the
wearer spreads herself until half-time, beneath a
white and plumy hat. The second brings a round
of applause for itself and makes the dainty
Julie look as if she were playing a game of
oranges-and-lemons with herself beneath a gold
and yellow sunset with black points in it, and her
hat and sunshade are sensational. She puts on
so much glitter with her clothes that there is none
left for the other ladies in the cast, who are all
neat but not gaudy. The other Parisian two, the
poodle and Pompier, are both barbered Frenchily
and both possess waists.
A polite hint “evening-dress” on the corner of
the cards for Mr. F. Down’s pupils’ concert was
promptly taken by a festive-looking crowd which
quite filled the Y.M.C.A. Hall, last Wednesday
evening. The entertainment was given by the
singing pupils, who did good average work, and
was brightened by a long interval and the enforced
absence through sickness of one of the principals.
Messrs. L. Philip, Leston and E. P. Truman gave
their professional services.
In connection with the Charity Carnival on
Aug. 28, at the Show Grounds, the Chinese are
going to have a festival. They will borrow all
the paraphernalia from their brethren in Bendigo,
where the Chow procesh and bang drums annually
at the Easter Fair. The shivoo will be altered
slightly from the old style, as up-to-date notions
like bicycle races have crept in. Evidently the
bike has supplanted the old-fashioned kite-flying,
such as the gold-seeking Celestials used to give on
the Victorian diggings in days of yore. There
ought to be lots of money in a Chow festival—and
lots of police also.

Brisbane girls are mourning that their most
charming and popular canon is going from them.
Rev. Stone-Wigg has just been made Bishop of
Guinea, and henceforth his “ soft and musi-
cal voice will be heard wrestling in prayer by the
natives of Papua only. This seductive cleric has
points which the heathen in his blindness won’t
begin to see. He is described as “ handsome, tall,
dark, slight, age about 33, and charming without
an effort. Any well-fed person would be just as
charming without an effort, and just as much ap-
preciated when cooked. Surely such a person is
too good to waste on cannibals. Why not swap
him for—say Bishop Goe?
Brisbane took football fever very badly. The
visiting Maoriland team was responsible'for an
abstracted soloist singing, at the last Liedertafel
concert, A brigand still, a soldier I, my foes my
goalers I defy.” The last line was repeated several
tunes by the singer, who imagined probably he was
fighting over again the battles of the previous
oaturday.
At little Raimond Pechotsch’s benefit, the
financial element floated, but the artistic was con*
siderably sunk. At benefits the people who
pay will good-naturedly tolerate any absurd
person who chooses to assume the title “artist.”
It is simple crucifixion to watch a pseudo-artist
standing with chest rising and falling, with face
purpling, with eyes bulging, while he or she
laboriously forces out a voice that is tied in a knot
at the back of the throat. In those parts of the
civilised world where the general community
understands what to expect from an “artist,” the
pretender is suppressed with hoots, hisses, or a
shower of walking-sticks. Miss Eva Mylott,
Riccardi s pupil, was a star in a firmament of her
own at this benefit. Miss Wynne’s top notes
brought her through successfully, but her lower
ones were not too audible.
Mme. Charbonnet-Killermann created much
mirth in her duo with Herr Pechotsch. After
steadily cantering up to the scratch, Madame sud-
denly raced away at a cracker, her head shaking
so violently that every moment promised to see
the front part of her hair tumble off and get
trampled to death by the galloping keys below.
IJutter, flutter, flutter went the curls; waggle
waggle, waggle went the “bang,” and the Herr
flogged into his violin like a jockey, and strained
every nerve and grew red in the face—and, in
fact, nearly dropped his bundle in his struggles to
keep pace. Of course the 10-year-old god of the
occasion played his violin and looked heavenly
innocent to perfection. He was the one being
who looked natural. He was neither cultivating an
absurd eye-glass nor growing long hair with a curl
at the end like a drake’s tail.

“The Melbourne debutante who figures as fellow-
slave to Mrs. Maesmore Morris in ‘ The Sign of

“ Women still worship at the joint shrine of the
Hambourg boys, though violinist Jakoff is now
first favorite. Not only is he the beauty of the
family, with his slender figure, dark eyes, and halo
of wavy brown hair, but his performance is less
disheartening to female hearers, of whom com-
paratively few attempt the fiddle. It’s all very
well for men who have not wasted half their youth
in futile pianoforte practice to gloat through opera-
glasses over Mark’s digital gymnastics, but the
prodigy’s free-and-easy passage across all the tra-
ditional ‘ asses’ bridges ’ of music is too aggravat-
ing for female flesh to bear. In suburban trains
and trams homeward-bound from Hambourg con-
certs the girls sit in dull despair, only rousing up
now and then to protest against the star’s sensa-
tionally rapid rendering of ‘ Yalse Allemande,’ or
some similar chestnut, as compared to the less-un-
attainable tempo affected by the past and gone He
Beaupuis. Meanwhile, Mark, looking healthier
than he did, makes his old bob-curtseys and
clutches at his front hair as of yore.
“ One of Melbourne’s cleverest pianists num-
bered among her pupils a scion of an extremely
wealthy, extremely sudden, Toorak family, and,
visiting the house for the first time, she had the
temerity to sit on one of the red plush-and-gilt-
framed chairs that crowded up the garish draw-
ing-room. Soon the lydy of the house waltzed in,
almost bursting through a neat little thing in
green and red satin. ‘ Git off them chairs ! ’ she
yelled, gasping in horror at the situation. ‘ D’yer
think we buy chairs like them to be sat on by the
likes of music-teachers ? ’

. “-A certain Tas. wool-king, a staunch blue-
ribbonite, is notoriously absent-minded. Recently
he, with his wife, attended a little social function
m the township nearest his location, and when it
was over, made off to the hotel to get his buggy.
The groom was absent, so the squatter himself
harnessed up and started homewards. After
driving some miles he found that his seat was not
E laced as usual. Investigation showed that he
ad taken the wrong trap. He drove back again
and .exchanged vehicles—then he found that he
ha/a forgotten his wife !

“ The cash she was wont to cut a dash with
having gone in the bank-smash, a daughter of one
of Melbourne’s Nicest families was on the point
of opening a tea-room when an old schoolmate
came forward with an invitation to take a year’s
trip to Europe with her, offering £lOO and all
expenses for the time. The Nicest one—well past
her first youth—accepted, but in the course of
many subsequent conversations with other society
damsels confessed that the most awful horror of
poverty was the associations it compelled. ‘ Look
at me,’she said, pathetically, ‘a lady! And yet
I am forced to become paid companion to a person
of that class.’ So widespread were these com-
plaints that they ultimately reached the voyageuse,
who immediately informed the aristocratic one
that she could not think of inflicting upon her the
degradation of compulsory association with one so
lowly.
“ August is to be a very giddy month. Several
large charity shivoos, two military dances, a
fashionable spinsters’ fling, the Melbourne Hunt
Club ball, the Mayor’s hop, and several small-fry
festivities are among the fixtures.
“ Mrs. (Premier) Turner, I hear from more than
one London correspondent, simply beamed on the
crowd as she drove in the procession on Jubilee
Day. The crowd, unused to such excessive
amiability from the occupants of royal carriages,
cheered her to the echo, and the more they cheered
the more she bowed.
“ More and more flowers are, I have noticed,
being sold in Sydney streets, and it is the same in
Melbourne, where flowers are ever so much
cheaper than on the shores of the Beautiful Har-
bor. A regular flower-market is now established
in Swanston-street on Saturday mornings. Last
‘ Block-day ’ I counted 38 persons between Flin-
ders and Collins-streets, mostly selling button-
holes violets, jonquils, and wattle. Two
camera-men were busy taking snapshots at the
time. Facially the boy-sellers mostly appear to
be either Jews or Graeco Italians ; and are nearly
all employed by one man. I analysed one penny
bunch of violets. It contained 45 violets, 2 brown
leaves, and 3 sprays of maiden-hair, all fastened
by no less than 3ft. 6in. of wire. This for Id. !
Where does the profit come in ?
“Among the passengers on the wrecked Tas-
mania were a bride and bridegroom of less than a
fortnight's standing. They were taken off in one
of the boats, leaving all their wedding-presents to
go down with the ship, but they escaped without
bodily damage beyond sun-peeled noses and chil-
blained knuckles and ears. A girl turned adrift
like that without even a spare hairpin must surely
feel depressingly squalid, though a woman who,
through a similar accident, began her honeymoon
on a raft, testifies to the contrary. She was so in-
tent, she says, on watching for her turn at the tin
of plum jam diluted with water, the only refresh-
ment available, that the thought of her short
ulster and bare feet troubled her very little.
One of the Indian mutiny romances tells of an
English officer who showed himself a regular bear
to his new-made wife, when they were besieged in
a fort, not in pressing danger, but in sweltering
heat and without luggage. There was no water
‘ s P are to wash the heroine’s face, her muslin
wedding-gown grew soiled and draggled, and her
hair hung in wisps all out of curl.
“ An old medical identity, who died at ’Omethe
other day, had shortly before his death taken
a second wife, matron of a hospital. His
grown-up daughters kicked against the match at
first, until a wily friend of pa’s reconciled the
youngest and most rampant of the rebels by point-
ing out that, as one of her elder sisters was married
and another engaged, it would be her sacred duty,
unless she sanctioned his marriage, to comfort her
fathers old age, give up dancing for filial cribbace,
and cultivate the knack of rubbing rheumatic
joints.

Rumored, that the Westralian Govt, will
probably not submit the Federation Bill to Parlia-
ment at all, and that the W. A. delegates will
simply be mussing, with no explanations offered
when the Convention meets at Sydney. Assuming
this to be so, the absence of Queensland and W.A
is so much the more reason why the other tour
provinces should go ahead with renewed vigor.
With only one Tory province left in, there is all
the more chance of building up a decent demo-
cratic constitution. W.A. will come into the
federation without asking questions when the
great slump arrives.

decided to hold the seventh on the mainland and
the event comes off in Melbourne at Cup-time. A
Eono r^ nime i. 0f iin V u ntS l W ' t h P r * zes ranging from

Hall, the young man who smothered his
wife and then squeezed her like a lump of pressed
beef into a tub, is one of those people whose feel-
ings would not be moved by anything short of
dynamite. His callous demeanor during the trial
at Castlemaine went three-quarters of the wav in
driving the jury to the conclusion that he mur-
dered his wife, and the production of a wax
lay figure of a naked woman stuffed in a tub
went the other quarter. Nothing like drama-
tic effect for a jury.. As to this figure, the
police had made up their minds that, to pot Hall
the tub business was the correct card. So a miser-
ably necessitous girl earned a few shillings by posing
aVIL® aether ”at the request of the police
At the Castlemaine July sittings, by the way, the
jury was called upon to decide in only four cases
One of wife-murder; one of giving drugs for an
unlawful purpose; one of incest, and one of
criminal assault. When Eva Scott, the chief wit-
ness, said, after persistent questioning, that Hall
ieft her one night at 12 o’clock, the town-
clock outside the court chimed 12. As counsel’s
speeches were about to begin, Hall took a seat in
the dock with the rope from the window touching
his neck. Sheriff M‘Farlane noticed the omen. *

Explorer Ernest Giles is once more a
Govt, official. In the early fifties he drove a pen
in Melb. G.P.0., and later clerked it in the
County Court. It is W.A. this time.
Out of a total of 37 Mayors of Sydney
only one—Manning—has been Roman Catholic.
The longest record as Mayor is held by John
Harris, who reigned five years altogether as
gutter-boss.
“After all, what a fool Windeyer was,”
(said a Sydney Q.C., the other day,) “to reach for
that P.C.-ship over the head of Darley, Chief-
Justice and Lieut.-Governor ! Surely he might
have known ! ”
Archdeacon King, who died on Saturday
week, aged 74, was a grandson of Governor King,
the third ruler of N.S.W. The Archdeacon was
a kindly old man, whose fad was the hopeless task
of converting the Jews.
From one point of view Australia is demo-
cratic enough. A recent Acting-Governor of an
Australian province is the son of a publican, and
it is related of him that when a boy he used to
play the fiddle in his father’s bar to induce custom.
In the cemetery of St. Matthew's Church,
at Windsor (N.S.W.), is a monument to the
memory of Surgeon Thomas Arndell, who came
out with Gov. Phillip in the first fleet, and after
his service settled on his grant in the Hawkesbury
district.

Who is responsible for this advt. of our doubtful
first parentage?—Saul Samuel or Georgey P.C.
orgy?

Dr. Dicky Youl, Melb.’s comic coroner,
relates how, at one inquest involving suspicion,
Purves, Q.C., appeared on behalf of a certain
witness and resented Youl’s reflections on his
client. “Why do you consider my client is
guilty ?” he asked at last. “ Because,” said Youl,
“ you are here to defend him.”
Australia’s one purely surgical knight,
Thomas Naerhten Fitzgerald, has been surgeon to
Melbourne Hospital for 37 years. Of other Aus-
tralian knighted medicos, Agnew and Renwick
got their handles for political reasons, Roberts for
his services to the Prince Alfred Hospital, Brown-
less for his to Melbourne University, and Hector
for his lustre in the world of natural science.
Another version. An Australian, writing
from London, says that, in the Jubilee procession,
it was mirth-provoking to study the demeanor of
Australasian Premiers. Reid was clothed in an
expansive smile, Turner looked pale and panic-
stricken, Seddonand Kingston, looking immensely
proud, recognised the salutes of the crowd in a
lordly way, but Braddon neither lifted his hat nor
smiled.
The Japanese mail-boat, Sagami Maru,
which came down the coast a few days ago,
dropped 250 Jap. emigrants in one lot at one
North Queensland port. They are going it
very gaily up in the Piebald Land. And,
meanwhile, nice, natty, gentlemanly Jap. consuls
keep assuring the Australian people with a pitying
smile that the Japanese invasion is all a myth—
the little brown man doesn’t emigrate—he is too
much attached to his home and has too much to
do there, and Australia is the last place he would
think of going to, anyhow.
Premier John Forrest, a Vic. paper points
out, rodein the sixth carriage among the “colonial'’
group at the Jubilee procession ; he has been six
years Premier of W.A., is six feet high, has six
brothers, belongs to a Cabinet of six, is a member
of the Six Families, and proposes to borrow
<£6,000,000. He also weighs 16 stone and has a
vote in 16 different constituencies. Also, 606 is
the number of the Beast of Revelations.
O’Malley, M.P., the barmaid abolition
champion in S.A. Assembly, on his enemies
He thanked God he was too strong for the reptiles to
pull down. He thanked God that the wilful, malignant,
heartless, prostituting, cold-blooded, social institu-
tions called drunkeries were not strong enough to
kill him. . . . Why did people like the ant ? They ad-
mired the ant and execrated the bed-bug because the ant
made its living in a respectable way and the bed-bug
made its living by sucking blood.
“Peter” Symmons, of Melb. Argus, was
once accosted at a Presbyterian Assembly meeting
by a parson who desired a few minutes’earnest
conversation with him on the rising of the session.
Scenting conversion, Symmons tried to escape per
back door, but was intercepted. “Ah want to
know,” began the black-coated one, “eef there’s
onny vacancy on the Airgus. You see, ah’ve a
nephew who was in the ir-ronmonger-ry, but of
late he’s taken to dreenk, an’ so we thocht of
getting him on to the press.”
Ed. Bulletin, —Think of New Guinea
M‘Gregor re-naming Mount Owen Stanley, Mount
“Victoria ! ” The name was a fitting one for the
loftiest peak of British Australasia, commemora-
ting, as it did, the gallant young lieutenant who
did so much survey work in austral waters—who
was on board the ship that landed the hapless
Kennedy, and helped to form the Raffles Bay
settlement; but what can “Victoria ” have to do
with it? Fortunately, no one agrees with the
change. The latest writers on geography unani-
mously condemn the action, as against all geo-
graphical rules. The name was given by the
officers of H.M.S. Rattlesnake in 1846-50, during
her long survey cruise, and M ‘Gregor had no more
right to miscall it than he had to re-christen New
Guinea. —E.F.

The present Tasmanian Jack Ketch was
formerly a sweep. Now, in addition to his capa-
city as man-butcher, he ranks as a petty constable.
At the last Tas. execution, the sheriff’s under-
study was incognito, and did his gory job success-
fully disguised by a wig and painted face.

As appearances go, W. J. Lyne and B. R.
Wise stand about level as lost memory champions.
Lyne’s proved lapse in re the Queen’s Jubilee, and
Wise’s apparent lapse in re Ashton, M.P., have
seldom been surpassed as memory-failures in the
politics of Australia.

The Adelaide city acre on which Premier
Kingston lives was bought by his father at
the first sale of town lots in ’36. The elder
Kingston was second-in-command to Col. Light,
who selected the site of Adelaide and conducted
the survey of its streets, square, and park lands.
Arthur Cheke, late taxing-
officer of Vic. Supreme Court,
who died the other day after a
long illness, was, before his as-
sumption of that office, associate
successively to Judges Webb and
Hood. He was closely related to
the late Judge Cheeke (so spelt in
the law reports) of the N.S.W.
bench, and a direct descendant
of the Cheke noted in the per-
secutions for Protestantism in Eng-
land and Flanders in Mary and
Elizabeth’s time. A book on this
Cheke’s sufferings, rather well
written of its kind, is looked
upon as a sort of addendum to the
Reformation martyrology.
Looking at the four marble
statues of Victoria, Albert, “ Tum-
my,” and the Princess of Wales,
which the late W. J. Clarke pre-
sented to Melb. Nat. Gallery, one
is not so much struck by the
grovelsome selection of subjects
as by the sculptor Summers’s
disloyalty to the man who gave
him the order. The four figures,
each seated in a chair, are slovenly
works of art all round, and absurd
misrepresentations of the originals
in three instances. “Wales’s”
wife is the exception.
Ten of the Federal Conven-
tion delegates were born in August.
Deakin was 41, Aug. 3; R. E.
O’Connor had a birthday, Aug. 4 ;
Attorney General Isaacs is 42,
Aug. 6 ; Premier Turner will be
46, Aug. 8 ; Simon Fraser will be
62, Aug. 21 ; Chief Secretary
Moore (Tas.) will be 74, and
Premier John Forrest 50, Aug
22; Education Minister Cock-
burn (S.A.) will be 47, Aug. 23;
R. F. Sholl (W.A.) will be 49,
Aug. 27 ; and Speaker Graham Berry (Vic.) will
be 75, Aug. 28. Then John Henry (Tas.) celebrates
his birthday on Sep. 1.
The Maori contingent had to be clothed
in Wellington before it was sent ’Ome. The
order for boots was given to a local warehouse,
and included “ fourteen pairs of tens and one pair
of thirteens.” The head of the department was
so flabbergasted with this item that he went about
all day and far into the night “ jibbering “ four-
teen pairs of tens and one pair of thir-feens.” The
dusky boys could march and ride far better with-
out boots and stockings. When the contingent
stood at attention with toes out, “Oh, those feet!”
said the onlookers.
The lately - deceased Archdeacon King,
grandson of Governor King, of the beginning of
the century, was one of nearly a dozen direct
descendants of that historic personage, all resident
in Australia. Gov. Bourke, another early monarch,
is the only other N.S.W. potentate of that rank
whose progeny (through the Deas-Thompsons)
abide with us. Lieut -Colonel G. Johnstone,
who, with two others, acted as a sort of trium-
virate Governor during the suspension of Gov.
Bligli, between 1808 and 1809, has also left resi-
dent representatives.
Governor Ranfurly, now on his way to
Maoriland, is particularly vitriolic where Irish
nationalism is concerned. When Gladstone es-
poused Home Rule, Ranfurly howled savagely
and betook him.-elf to Belfast to head the Orange-
men. And yet lie is of Irish descent, and his wife
is one of the In t lineal representatives of Lord
Charlemont, founder of the practically rebel Irish
volunteers. “An uneducated democracy ”is male
Ranfurly’s special aversion. For years he hung
around the Queen as Lord-in-Waiting at J 5702 a
year, and, though his actual presence at Court was
required for only six weeks out of the fifty-two,
his loyalty caused him to linger long and lovingly
about the royal premises.
Sydney University has the right to yearly
nominate two students in the Imperial army. As
a rule, its children are not taking any gore. An
exception has, however, turned up in the son of
the hereditarily-gilded Mat. Harris, M.P., who
not long ago received a commission in the Ennis-
killen Dragoons. Mat. should find footing the
bill for this youthful warrior’s kit about equal to
the expenses of two general elections. But, his
troubles ! To look at the burly Mat., who bears
an almost twin-like likeness to “ Mudgee-gaol-
bricks-Jones ” —save that his silence is more golden
—no one would accuse him of being a B.A. But
he is, all the same—he holds one of the earliest
degrees of Sydney University. Next year will
probably see him Mayor of Sydney.
Subjoined is a complete list of the persons
—ghouls and others —who were present at the
judicial hanging of Frank Butler, on July 16 :
C. E. B. Maybury, Stephen L. Guv, Whitting-dale John-
son, G. Herbert, C. W. F. Taylor, F. Penny, P. Hunter,
A. Kierman, George Lane, F. E. Diamond, M. H. Donohoe,
J. Weymark, Edward Evans, Alexander Barber, Charles
Gibbs, W. Ridley, J. Robinson, J. Holloway, W. Jay,
M. E. H. Felton, G. A. Hyam, J. Roper, J. Lowry, J. B.
Spence, Dr. Paton, Nosey Bob, and an assistant.
Several of the above, as the names show, were
present in an official capacity, but more than
hree-fourths were ghouls, pure and simple. The
reasons assigned for the admission of a full half-
dozen of the crowd would have made Butler die
laughing.

Re the literary feebleness of comic opera books—bar
ring W. S. Gilbert’s-a Bulletin correspondent writes:
“ There are really a score of men in every Australian
capital capable of turning out words equal to the average
imported opera libretto, if it were only made worth their
while.” But how about plot situations, stage business,
and general knowledge of effect-manufacture ? Dialogue
is only a third-rate consideration in an operatic show
First the music ; then the “ guts,” as managers term it;
then the words.
The two Melbourne amateur operatic clubs were deftly
described by a rude man recently as the “ Upper ” and
“ Lower" operatic clubs. The aristocratic Lyric Club un-
veils its upper half, but hides its lower half under a bushel
—of long petticoats. The democratic Opera Club delights
to show how well it can fill its stocking, and—but that is
another story—while it discreetly hides the temperate
zone between waist and neck.
“ Strange that such difference should be
’Twixt Tweedledum and Tweedledee 1 ”
Melb. : “Here is what would pull the public here. A
Natatorium in the city, with lady swimmers, and gentle-
men trying to angle them out with rod and hook, after
the style of the London Aquarium.”
The music-hall artiste who picks out a man in the audi-
ence, and sings amorous or other verses at him, till he
lusts for blood, ought to be howled off the boards. Writer
was present at a variety show in a Vie. mining town when
the lady star did this thing, and the butt in the audience
presently unfolded his great length and stood quietly
until opportunity offered. Then he said in a firm, con-
vincing voice: “Miss, if you will send out the biggest
male member of your company to sing at me I will batter
up the furniture with his head.”

S.G. : “Marshall Hall, arch-hater of concert-chatterers,
should print on his programmes this quotation from
Ecclesiasticus: ‘ Hinder not musick. Pour notout words
where there is a musician, and show not forth wisdom
out of time. A concert of musick in a banquet of wine
is as a signet of carbuncle set in gold. As a signet of
emerald set in a work of gold, so is the melody of musick,
with pleasant wane.”
“Acrobat” to The Bulletin “ Every circus per-
former comes to grief in time cautious or careless;
temperate or ‘indiscreet,’ asrial performer, acrobat,
gymnast or equestrian. Accidents which horrify the
spectators are often the least painful and soonest re-
covered from, whereas apparent trifles frequently cause
great suffering. Hanlon, falling 65ft. from dome to audi-
torium, struck his forehead on the iron back of a seat,
literally slicing the top of his skull off ; yet he recovered,
and was performing again in a few months. I myself
have had the usual run of falls and mishaps from varying
heights, but never, as the result of any of them, suffered
a fraction of what I endured from what seemed at first a
trifle. At juggling practice the occasional dropping of a
small 7oz. ball on a particular spot on my wrist —a
mishap so slight that it could scarcely be called
an ‘ accident’—set up an inflammation of the tendon-
sheath, which caused me acute pain and prevented
me from performing for nearly 12 months. Go into
the dressing-tent of any first-class circus after the
night’s performance. You w ill be surprised to see that
some of the men—who have just previously been bounding
about the ring as though they were indestructible—are
in cold blood almost cripples. Amongst those who have
been any length of time in the profession, wrist-straps,
knee-bandages, ankle-supports, and other extraneous aids
are the rule, not the exception. A feat has been per-
formed successfully thousands of times, in practice and
in public ; but there comes a time when something goes
wrong—a guy-rope gives—a high wind shakes the tent,
the performer ‘takes off’ an atom of a second too soon, or
too late—or gets‘cast’in the midst of a somersault—it
is done in a tick, and the injury is perhaps irreparable.
After one important mistake the boldest performers never
have quite the same courage. Hanlon, after his great
fall, lost his nerve; and shortly after recommencing his
business came down again and was killed. Dashway, a
man of the most reckless daring—nightly doing his
‘double away’from the horizontal bar, at all angles—
janded on the edge of the mattrassat Broken Hill, tearing
the tendon of Achilles clean out of position. His leg was
in plaster-of-Paris for weeks, and on his recovery, when
performing this feat, he always had a man to stand by—a
precaution which he had previously scorned. Moulton,
his partner, another courageous performer, after seeing a
former mate killed by a fall from a flying trapeze in
Ottawa, never again ventured upon serial flights. Amongst
female performers, those who experience maternity lose
their nerve. Many never venture to perform again, and
even the bravest exhibit an excess of caution which itself
leads to disaster.”

Quiller Couch is gaining much kudos in the
English press by verses in which all sorts of sports are
ludicrously mixed up. But “ Q.” is not original here.
The late Geo. Lash Gordon, once well-known on the Aus-
tralian stage, effectively handled the same idea in “ How
Flyblow Won the Cup,” in The Bulletin six years ago.
“ Flyblow,” which has since been extensively recited in
Melb. and Sydney, is a jumble of every sport known to
Australians, and deals with the dastardly attempt of a
gang of ropers to stiffen a horse by getting him to stop in
the middle of the Melb. Cup to scratch a mosquito which
is biting him.

S.S. : “To the public generally. Never travel
in the same railway carriage or ornthe same steamer with
a mob of racing cyclists. The racing cyclist on the loose
is a howling and ruinous nuisance who bellows incessantly
and gallops up and down with the brainless frivolity of
the garden goat. He seems to be in a perennial state of
baby drunk, and there is more peace of mind to be found
in the society of 15 legally certified idiots in a powder-
factory than cooped up in a carriage with several profes-
sionalVheelinen when their Kanaka-like sense of humor
sets them acting the horrid boy, the giddy ox, and blatant
slaverer ail at once.”
* * * »
Ed. Bulletin, —At Bathurst Show this year
there was no prize awarded to the cattle-dogs. On en-
quiring, I was told that the judges did not consider them
worthy of a prize—“ They were not cattle-dogs.” Among
the exhibits were the first and second prize dogs of the
year previous. What is one to infer from this ? Can a
dog be worthy of first prize one year and not even worthy
of a name the next ? —Enigma.

Dear Bulletin,—The opinion that a certain
amount of chivalry, manliness and generosity accom-
panies hard-faced horsey-men got a severe knock the
other day in a back-country (N.S.W.) police-court. Dur-
ing the hearing of an ordinary flve-shilling-fine-drunk-
case, a trooper, in corduroy riding-pants, top-boots and
spurs, unblushingly confessed : “I heard the whole argu-
ment, your Worship ; I had my cap off and was crouched
under the bar window.” Shades of Gordon and Corrigan I
Crouched !_! In top-boots and spurs! It sounded like a
criminal libel on the rig-out. I could almost hear his
horse outside snort with disgust, and I fancied I oould
see'the poor disgraced beast drop a tear and blush with
shame, and lower his bottom-lip right down to his ohest
with indignation. No wonder the Clanoys are leaving
the noble animal and taking to bikes.— 4 xB.
* • • •
Trust a Chinaman to make the most of a good
thing, A big Australian raoing man had a China-
nian cook, who, in collusion with the first mount, got the
cream of the market every time the boss decided to let
loose. The owner’s instructions to the jockey used to be
“To-day, Pigskin,” or “ Not to-day, Pigskin,” as the case
might be, and the jockey then signalled to the oook,
whose commissioner acted accordingly. One day owner
gave his parting blessing to rider, “ Not to-day, Pigskin. **
‘‘ Yes, to-day, Mr. Owner,” was the reply. Thinking he
had misunderstood him, owner repeated : “ No,notto-day,
Pigskin," but Pigskin answered again with a grin, “ Yes,
I said to-day. Mr. Owner”—and to-day it was, for the
mare romped home, and cook and jockey skinned the
market. It proved a dear bit of fun, however, for when
license day came round, the club refused Pigskin’s license
without vouchsafing any reason, and he has “ stood
down” ever since.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—For years the “ Sports ” head-
ing of your paper consisted of a presentment of the late
Walter Bradley, seated in an easy chair, with a glass of
grog at his elbow, a Sunday at Home in his hands, and a
pair of gamecocks having a ding-dong set-to in front of
him. Now, as a matter of fact, although the late genial
auctioneer bred more gamecocks, perhaps, than any man
of his day, it was as show-birds only, and he was only con-
nected with one “ main ”in his life. The Waterhouses at
that time were breeding fighting-stock with a strain of
Malay in them, and Bradley, of course, beat them in the
show-pen with his highly-bred, smaller-looking imported
stock. This led to the Waterhouse-Sparks people laugh-
ing at the imported birds as “ game-cocks,” and the end
of it was a big main, for £lOO a-side, between the heavy
Australian birds and the light English. To the chagrin
of the Australians, the light English birds fairly slaugh-
tered their heavier opponents. And that was the first
and last main ever fought under the auspices of Walter
Eradley.—W.
* * * *
Dear B., —You recently mentioned several men
who think they “ lost their chance ” by following cricket.
But every man has his regrets, and it is not certain that
they would have done any better had the game never
been invented. The brothers Gregory, “ Billy ” Murdoch
“Tommy” Garrett, H. H. Massie, Fred. Spofforth, Percy
Charlton, a few Sydney names which occur on the spur of
the moment, do not seem to have suffered much from the
cricket bias. “ What is to be, will be.”
• * * •
The football match was over, and the hero
of the day was Chowder, the great ’arf-back. The
evening papers, the groups of barrackers that stood
around the corner, all sang his praise. I thought 1
would like to be a Chowder, and just then I noticed a
big, hard-faced, bullet-headed youth playfully pick up a
lady’s poodle and bathe it in the trough, smile, and care-
fully place it alongside the baby in the pram., and then
offer to punch the stuffin' out of an old man who couldn’t
or wouldn’t see the fun. “ Who is that fellow ?” I asked
a grinning youth. “ Blime, don’t cher know Chowder—
’im as kicked three goals and played the game of the
forty!. Oh,’e’s a dandy player.” Ashamed of my ignor-
ance, 1 slunk away.—E.K.
* * * *
The late Bord Falmouth, who did not back even
his own many successful horses, on being asked how
betting could be put down, laconically said, “Legalise
pulling.”
» * * *
The Earl of Shaftesbury— not he of Melbourne,
but his great-grandfather—was one of the most earnest
patrons of the old prize-ring, and on the retirement of
Mendoza, the champion of England, it fell to his lordship
to make the subscribed presentation to the pug. “ Not
only in the ring,” said Shaftesbury, “ but on all sides has
Bill Mendoza made his presence felt. (Applause.) There
has, it is true, been a slight prejudice on the part of some
bigoted people because Bill is a Jew. But we should all
remember that in that respect Bill is like the Saviour of
mankind. The difference,” mused his lordship, “is that
the latter died on the Cross, while Bill has lived on it."
• * * *
Old Mat Hardy, an ornament of the ring in the
old days, and still affectionately remembered by sporting
identities, is now an inmate of Ballarat Benevolent Asylum.

Reported that the metropolitan daily-newspaper
offices are importing, for the use of their reporters,
machines in which the bicycle is combined with the type-
writer. The sordid commercialists who boss the Archi-
medean lever don’t see why their minions shouldn’t tran-
scribe while abike.

“V.” to The Bulletin Australia’s po*
blackfellow, whose boomerang has puzzled philosophers
and scientists, has in two respects revolutionised sport.
It is now reoognised that the fashionable over-arm
stroke was borrowed by English tourists who saw some
blacks swimming in a Ooogee waterhole. Seven or eight
years ago an aboriginal was laughed at when he started,
on all-fours, at Carrington and Botany. Yet that style is
now universal in America, and is becoming prevalent in
England. Tts advantages are that the hands on ground
prevent a p 1. from going over the mark and incurring a
penalty, and that once started he loses no time in getting
* down ’ to his work.” However, this paper remembers
seeing it stated somewhere that Catlin saw the over-arm
side-stroke used by N. American Indians.
* * * *
“ One of the best all-round athletes I ever saw,”
said a veteran Australian cricketer, “ was a long, quiet,
left-handed slab who could do anything, and yet would
$o simply nothing. As a bowler at practice he was a
second Frank Allan. What a wonder we thought we had
got! Play in a match? Not he—the wretch said he
bowled for exercise. At football, in Carlton’s best class,
this useless object could mark higher and kick further
and straighter than any man in the team. Play ? Not a
bit of it. On the river he could push his boat faster than
any man in his olub. But he never took a seat with
them. It was the training he objected to, and he gave it up
Then with the gloves at the M.A.C. he could fool the best
of the fighters, but not a round would he do—and he could
do lots more things, but wouldn’t. I lost him for some
years, but met him again. He was boss cornet-man in the
Army. It was a sad sight to see such talent blowing out
his lungs for the good of his soul. Think what he might
have been ! ” Then the crowd round the veteran dropped
tears into their beer, and said that it was very, very sad.
f “ Sporting Notions ” continued on page 24.)


Chief Justice Way, of S.A., gets no salary
as an Australian Judge on the British Privy
Council, and while he is away in England attend-
ing the Council, S.A. puts him on half-salary.
Apparently he has also to pay his own travelling
expenses, so there seems to be a lot of ruinous
dignity about the business. Little Way has let
go the bone and is clutching at the shadow.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraciough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection —
long sight, short sight, old sight. Eull particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraciough.*

per cent, of that English paper’s matter is undoubtedly
stolen. Possibly enough the very paragraph you instance
was stolen from a Bulletin of years ago. Complaints
like yours generally reach us from people who, if they
were running a paper, would have “The Raven" and
“The Ancient Mariner” traded off on them as “little
things of my own.”
The champion Jubilee poem —by the
Ponsonby (Auckland, M.L.) poet Tomlinson—
who has got it out in good style, printed in black
and gold :
IN COMMEMORATION OF THE QUEEN’S RECORD
REIGN.
Junk 22, 1897.
Your Most Gracious Majesty has been called to reign
Over the British Nation lor sixty years, without a stain—
A people of riches, great and wealthy,
With its important cities and towns, all healthy.
Thou hast had wise and noble men as advisers,
They have sat up late at night and yet early risers—
Men that have made money, yet not misers—
And judges that have presided over the assizes.
Ministers who have guided the helm of the State,
Who first commenced life by writing on a slate,
They have risen to the highest position in the realm,
Broad and sturdy as the oak, and taller than the elm.
On or about the year Eighteen hundred and forty-six,
When John Bright, Richard Cobden and others were in a
fix,
And after great battles fought at the hustings, and in the
House of Commons, they were enable
To give the working classes of the United Kingdom a
cheap breakfast table.
Thine has been a reign of Peace and Prosperity
At home and abroad. At times a call is made for severity—
In 1854 the Crimean war broke out,
And had to be defended by our soldiers, brave and stout.
In the battles of Inkerman, Alma and Sebastopol,
And the brave charge of Balaclava’s Six Hundred,
Who went through fire, powder and shot
As it came from the mouth of the Russian cannons, red
hot.
Most of thy ministers have been men of renown ;
Their talents have been used in the service of the Crown.
They first saw the light of day in some village or town,
Not forgetting thy faithful old servant, John Brown.
The Lord Chancellor, Earl Cairns,
Was once one of the Soottish bairns.
The Lords Selborne and Hatherley,
The Lords Herschell and Halsbury,
Who have in turn sat upon the woolsack,
Whatever their politics, they have trod in the same track.
Thy Prime Ministers, Sir Robert Peel, Lord Derby,
Palmerston, Beaconsfield, Gladstone and Salisbury
Have guided the helm of the State through many a storm
Before many of us now living had ever been born.
Thy late husband and consort, Albert the Good,
Had his name written upon the hearts of the people, and
not only upon stone or wood.
Thy sons and daughters have been nursed at thy side,
And as long as thou livest they will ever abide.
And now Your Most Gracious Majesty, in point of time,
has broken the record,
It is beoause thou and thy people have believed in the
Word of God.
As they have looked into those things which are Divine
And most of thy people have been able to strike out the
[TOP LINE.
Thy reign upon earth has been victorious ;
In that eternal world above it shall be more glorious,
And when thou has finished wearing the jewelled orown
that shall fade away,
Thou shalt wear a crown of glory throughout the long
Eternal Day.
God Save thb Queen.
Yours, &c., G. Tomlinson.
Ponsonby, Auckland, M.L., June 22, 1897.
This is the only J ubilee ode The Bulletin has
read which is absolutely worthy of the subject and
the occasion.
Touching Our Gracious Queen’s “ sweet,
womanly sympathy,” London Figaro tells a yarn,
as coming from an admiral who was showing a
newspaper man over the royal yacht. The
admiral mentioned the incident of the Queen find-
ing a middy in her cabin, trying on her royal
bonnet at the looking-glass. She gave the lad an
awful smack on the ear, which was womanly
enough. Next day the middy—-a “jolly little
fellow ” —was sent home, and his parents got word
to take his name off the loooks. Not much sweet
sympathy about that!
Cook was a lanky, slabsided, simple
Queenslander with a thick, shaggy beard and
£1450 in a Brisbane banjt. At the door of the
shipping office he met a gentleman who, by some
unknown means, suddenly became his bosom friend.
Mr. Derby was so infatuated with Mr. Cook's
company that, after the latter had shown him
his deposit-slip for £1450, Mr. Derby determined
to take a holiday and accompany the backblocker to
Sydney. In Sydney they had a real good time, and
after three days? riot on the fat of the land, Mr.
Cook fell among thieves and said he had lost all his
money. Mr. Derby was angry, and used language
that scorched the paint off the bedsteads. Mr.
Cook proposed a loan of .£5O till he got his busi-
ness fixed up and returned to Brisbane. Mr.
Derby got the money, but at the end of the week
the business was still incomplete although the
money was finished. Mr. Derby raised another
.£lO, and introduced Mr. Cook to two finanoial
gents, who offered him a share in that valuable
parcel of land upon which the new Sydney markets
are being erected for the paltry sum of .£1330,
pointing out that the city was anxious to
buy it for building purposes. Mr. Cook was
suspicious, and after much hesitation de-
lined to buy until he consulted Mr. Derby.
Mr. Derby said he himself was trying to borrow
money with which to buy a share. Cook was
satisfied and next day started for Brisbane to
get his money, Mr. Derby supplying the funds.
When he returned he showed Mr. Derby the
draft, and they arranged to have a little dinner at
a fashionable restaurant after cashing it. When
they arrived at the bank next day, Mr. Derby and
the financial gents determined to wait round the
corner. Cook went in, got the money, and pre-
sently emerged, carrying in his left hand a little
canvas bag, which, whem he joined his friends, he
put into his inside pocket. “ Have you get it ? ”
asked Mr. Derby. “Oh, yes, I’ve got it, and I
d well mean to stick to it, you infernal scoun-
drels ! ” said Mr. Cook, as he poked Mr. Derby
heavily in the wine-vat with his, walking-stick,
while he shouted loudly: “Police! Murder!
Thieves ! ” And while the solitary bobby chased
the flying spielers Mr. Cook quietly walked away,
making objurgations as he went

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Guard of the Bendigo Express.
HOW HE SAVED HIS LIFE.
Perhaps no railway guard is better known in
Victoria than the veteran Mr. Morris Morris, of
the Bendigo Express, and he tells how his life was
saved by using Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure. Railway
men and those whose business necessitates con-
tinual rai’ way riding are especially liable to kidney
disease, caused by the constant jolting and nervous
strain to which they are subjected. Guard Morris
Morris says: “For nearly 25 years I have been a
guard on the Victorian railways, and am at pre-
sent in charge of the Bendigo Express. Until
about twelve months ago I had never lost a day
through ill health. Then I gradually took bad un-
til my case necessitated a doctor. He saidT was
suffering from Bright’s Disease, and recommended
me to resign. A friend casually mentioned to ma
Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure, and in desperation I tried
it. The effect was wonderful. I speedily got
well, and am now in robust health, my life bemg
no doubt saved by this medicine.”*
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific for Lrrii
and Kidney Disease.

POLITICAL
POINTS
George Reid, P.C.—Parent of Cash-basis.
The present N.S.W. Government: Reid
and—Want.
The S.A. Labor-party declines to have
anything to do with Federation, save on the
basis of “one adult one vote.”
Sydney Chinese propose to establish a
brigade to supply food and clothing to the white
unemployed. The Caucasian is played out.
There are plentiful signs in S.A. Assembly
of an attempt by Opposition Leader John Downer
to get on better terms with the Labor-party.
The five Q.C’s. in N.S.W. Leg. Council
seem to dominate the Federal question. Want,
Salomons, O’Connor, and Barton have, so far,
done all the talk.
How is it that N.S.W. Public Library is
so deficient in the works of Protectionist writers ?
This applies to other public and semi-public
N.S.W. institutions. Has the ruling gang tried,
with some success, to stifle opposition opinion ?
. Bombay native paper that said “ God
didn’t give foreigners a charter to rule over India ”
will probably be prosecuted for blasphemy—of
Bull ! Bull thinks it’s his place to give charters
to God ! In fact, Bull isn’t quite sure that he
isn’t God !
The God-fearing N.S.W. Ministry com-
pletes its third year in office Aug. 30th, which
date is no doubt annually set apart for prayer and
thanksgiving in the domestic circles of their
reverences Brunker, Garrard, Cook, Ghould—and
Jack Want.
The Validating Bill rendered necessary by
the N.S.W. Land-tax bungle may precipitate an
appeal to the country before Xmas. If it does,
may the Lord help Commissioner Spiller ! The
“ six-pound-a-weekers ” won’t forget him if they
ever get back.
The late Mr. R. C. WALKER, ex-Public
Librarian of N.S.W.
Photo., Vandyck.
The story goes, in a wide circle, that
Speaker Abbott was offered, but refused, the
N.S.W. Agency-General before Reid went jubilat-
ing; that Brunker was the next to get the offer,
but declined ; and that the choice still rests be-
tween Dibbs and M‘Millan.
Why should Q. object so furiously to
Imperial regulations imposing heavy shipping tax
©n all slavers (and other vessels) visiting the Solo-
fnons? Is it not from the Solomons that nearly
*ll the Q. Kanaka murderers of men and outragers
Of women have hailed for the past 10 years ?
Zox, M.L.A., to Prendergast, M.L.A. :
If there were many men like you I don’t know
what would become of Victoria.” Prendergast to
Zox: “It is men like you who have made men
like me possible in Victoria.” Then the Hebrew
ragman in the white vaith-coat left hurriedly.
Pointed out to Lands-Minister Carruthers
by Christian Endeavor deputation that Queen Vic-
toria had “recognised ” that the prosperity of the
country during her reign had been due to Divine
guidance. “Recognised,” again! “Self—first
prize,” as it were; “ God—honorable mention ! ”
Maoriland Cold-Tea Party
announces that its amended pro-
gramme is prohibition by a bare
majority and that, at future
anti-liquor polls, only two issues
be submitted to the electors :
(1) That things remain as they
are, and (2) that liquor be abso-
lutely prohibited. The third
issue, that licenses be reduced in
number, is regarded as a feeble
pandering to the moderate
drinker. The loveliness of this
arrangement is that when once
any district goes in for Prohibi-
tion, the Cold-Tea Party pro-
poses to give it no further voice
in the matter. Where drink is
already sold the people may vote
either that it be abolished or that
things remain as they are.
Where Prohibition is in force
they are to vote on the same two
issues: (1) That drink be pro-
hibited (when it is prohibited
already), or (2) that things con-
tinue as they are (in other
words, that drink continue to be
prohibited).
Tasmanian enthusiasm for Federation.
Acting-Premier Fysh opened the debate in the
House the other night, and, after an hour’s mono-
tonous wailfulness, broke off to complain that he
was being interrupted by the sound of members’
talking. “ Snoring ! ” corrected an Oppositionist.
Professor Harper, of Melbourne, says that
“ every concession towards secularisation of public
life is taken advantage of, and pressed far beyond
its original meaning.” Ecclesiasticism’s old dodge
—to call everything that is absolutely forced out
of its grip a “concession.” The virtue of neces-
sity !
“What is this Mr. ? A toad, a
creeping thing, a simmering hypocrite, and a
gaping fool. We are only degrading ourselves by
speaking about a thing like him. He is not a man,
biit a thing.” Observations at Melb. Trades
Hall. Fat Man was right, after all. The Trades
Hall does think itself Parliament.
Brisbane Courier of July 30 re Pacific
trading licenses in the Solomons : “ The Kanaka
is so safeguarded and provided for that his con-
dition is the envy even of the whites.” And yet
the other day Polynesian Inspector Caulfield
proved to the hilt in open Court that the poor
Polynesians were often scrimped of their daily
tucker.
That phenomenal railway, the Northern
(Q.) has just improved its previous best record by
about .£24,000. Also the little Bowen line has
hopped up from £978 to =£3054. Wherefore
Townsville, which is ever clamoring for justice to
the North, will, of course, agree to allow the
Northern line to connect with Bowen and so
export the North-western produce through its
natural outlet.
Howe, one of the S.A. Convention dele-
gates, gives the other side of the case. The
papers sat heavily on him for taking £2 2s. per
day, Sundays included, not only while the two
Conventions are sitting but for the interval
between as well. “My election cost me £227,”
says Howe, “and the newspapers took almost all
of it. The fees will just about pay their bills. It
is they who are taking the two guineas.”
London Speaker (the Gladstonian weekly)
says it thinks that woman-suffrage, which it re-
gards as a “ doubtful experiment,” was tried and
abandoned in Viccoria some years before its recent
adoption in New Zealand and Australia ” (sic).
So that all the Jubilee grovel and Hempyah
twaddle have not taught a scholarly English paper
that Victoria is a part of Australia. To say no-
thing of the Speaker’s dense ignorance of Vic.
politics.
“ Result of the godless N.S. W. education
system! ” snorts Salomons, Q.C., because the
State-schoolboys scribble unwashed observations
on his fence. But, even if the shrieky little man
had a Sunday-school alongside, he wouldn’t find
the inscriptions any holier. The teaching has yet
to be invented that could get God very far under
the skin of the healthy male urchin. The juvenile
saint has mostly something wrong with his
“ innards.”
The recent fizzled-out N.S. W. anti-Sunday -
lolly-shop campaign was a move towards the
nauseous Calvinism that principally engineered
the iniquitous Melbourne boom-swindles—the im-
pudent hypocrisy which, while squashing the
Saturday - produced Sunday - papers, carefully
ignored the real Sawbath-breaking involved in the
Monday’s issue of the “respectable” morning
sheets. “ God-fearing ”is a label that has to be
lived down to !
It is the specially-privileged classes who
oppose Federation in Tasmania. Thus a legis-
lator :
The expenses of the Federal Government with its build-
ings, its army of officials and its cocked hats, will swallow
up our Customs revenue, and leave us to pay our national
debt out of direct taxation !
Just so. Tas. Customs revenue just pays interest
on the national debt, and a bit over. Abolish the
Customs and Fat Man is in the fire.
For Federation Reid bids steer !
His colleagues follow the suggestion,
Save Want, who roars : “ No blanky fear !
That fad was left an open question."
For Prohibition Cook’s agog !
Five pals take sides with Holy Joe ;
While Want and Reid avow that grog
Should have free course of ebb and flow.
A Cabinet ’gainst itself divided
Must fall. For fatal is the folly
When Ministers, all undecided,
Fail idem velle atqrn nolle.
J.S.
A WE WARD,
"Well, butcher, have you brought the ox
heart I ordered? ”
BUTCHER : “ Well, mum, the dog shook your heart on me this
mornin’.”
J. ’sP. are being gazetted batch after batch
almost daily in N.S.W. The God-fearing Minis-
try blue-pencils every publican’s name submitted,
but approvingly initials the names of brewers and
their chief men. However, bush storekeepers are
allowed through, and thus the outcast publican
will have a step-brother on the bench.
Smellbourne’s Fatmen appear confident
that Duncan Gillies will carry the Toorak elector-
ate, in spite of the Age’s furious contumely.
They consider him alive and kicking, in a politi-
cal sense, whereas the “ unrespeetable ” class
would fain believe that the shiny little jobber is as
dead as £. £. Smith, Bent, O’Loghlen, and a
dozen others, boys of the old brigade. The ques-
tion is—-Was Duncan’s defeat at the Federal Con-
vention tantamount to a public funeral of his cold
remains ?
C. : “ Yery rough on Isaacs and Peacock
to expect them to decide respective merits of
Orange and Green —especially at election times.
The Yic. law about 4 illegal processions ’ is vague
and shaky. At any rate, such questions should
be decided by the judiciary—-not by the political
party on top. Wanted, amended law, enabling
courts to be moved to grant an injunction pro-
hibiting sectarian processions, before the danger
is done. Orange and Green would then fight in
the court with hired champions.”
Expenditure of loan moneys in Victoria
during the Turner Government’s three years’
term of office has averaged about £184,000 a year
—total, ,£550,111 for the three years. For the
same three years N.S.W. got through about
<£4,l32,ooo—that is to say, it supplied continuous
work for nearly 50,000 inhabitants out of loan-
funds, whereas Vi. toria found employment from
the same source for only about 3700. The good
old loan keeps quite a lot of the population in
work while it lasts, and swells the local industries
beautifully.
Yic. Labor-member Hancock on Federa-
tion: “All the talk about the consolidation of
loans is a fraud. It could only be done as the
loans mature, and that means waiting 30 years
before there is any possibility of making a saving.”
What beautiful logic ! There are loans maturing
every year, and as each loan fell due and was
converted into Federal stock, something would be
saved. The saving would begin almost in the
first month of Federation, and would go on in-
creasing every year till the last provincial loan
was consolidated.
Hancock, M.L.A. (Victoria), says that
“ Federation is not popular in countries where it
exists. ” The principal countries where Federation
exists are Germany, Austria-Hungary (which is a
species of Federation, though rather an awkward
one), Switzerland, United States, Canada, Mexico,
Argentina, Brazil, Colombia and Venezuela. Pro-
bably all these countries grumble at their Govern-
ments —no one expects even a Federal Government
to be perfect—still Federation is so popular with
them that not one of them makes a move towards
any other system.
Note well the following figures for the year
1896. Total male Polynesians in Queensland, 749.
Total females, thirty-three ! The Solomons con-
tributed 423 of this number ; the Hebrides 286,
and the Banks group 73. The death-rate was
31 '62 per thousand—an increase of 2 per thousand
on the previous year. Hence Kanaka morality is
as dubious as ever ; Kanaka cannibals are on the
increase, as the Solomon importation testifies ;
and, last but not least, the Queensland canefields
are not likely to lack human-flesh-and-bone manure
for some time to come.
Dear Bulletin, The rabid parson's
pathetic ignorance of men’s real minds is well
exemplified by Rev. Dill Mackay’s assertion that,
with the exception of a very few infidels and
agnostics, all the people of N.S.W. want God’s
name in the Constitution. The infidel-positive
certainly doesn’t grow on every tree, but the
ostensibly orthodox person who doubts-and-
daren’t-say-so is nearly as common nowadays as
the outsider who simply laughs at the notion of
taking a fellow-creature’s word for what can’t
possibly be known.— Heretic.
Re Papa Brunker’s boorish refusal to house
those belated French soldiers, what conception of
“ the large, fine courtesies ’twixt State and State”
was to be expected from, a man who, born at Mait-
land, where they still use type cast in the 18th
century, had never set foot outside N.S. Wales
until sent to Adelaide, as a snuffle-selected dele-
gate to the Federal Convention, and who is
possibly under the impression that Anti-Christ is
still ramping round somewhere in the person
of the First Napoleon, and that Gaul and Briton
are as anxious as aforetime for each other’s scalps.
Vic. A.cting-Premier Isaacs also wants to
arrange for the “ recognition of God ” in the
Federal Constitution. What about the recogni-
tion of Joss? Presumably every one hopes that
Queensland and W. A. and the Northern Territory
will be included in the Commonwealth, and with-
out the recognition of Joss what"inducement is
there for them to come in ?
The land-tax assessment, it is alleged, has
revealed the fact that for one-tenth of the pri-
vately-owned land in N.S.W. no owners can be
found. Yet they can’t have all gone prospecting
with Butler. One casual advantage of the land-
tax is that it will provide owners in course of time.
If somebody doesn’t turn up and pay within a
certain period the State will sell the property for
arrears.
Van Dobson is irreclaimable. In Tas.
Assembly he admits that since getting away from
the influence of the silver-tongued eloquenoe of
Deakin, Reid, Isaacs and Trenwith and other
Democratic Federal-Conventioners, he has seen
the error of their ways, and is more Conservative
as regards State Rights and Manhood Suffrage
than ever. Says Dobson :
I will go to the Sydney Convention and fight as I never
fought before, and insist that there should be a strong
Senate.
This should mean sport for the mainland delegates.
The decision of Dobson to fight for a property-
vote “ as he never fought before ” is a plucky one,
for it means that he will be laughed at as he never
was laughed at before. And ridicule is no joke
to a man of Van Dobson’s temperament.
Acting-Premier Wittenoom of W.A. has
not John Forrest’s capacity for making things
look good. He admits that of the late £1,000,000
loan —which only realised about £935,000 —no lea,
than £750,000 was spent in advance as far back as
March last. The rest has since been dissipated*
and the Government is now keeping its public
works going by Treasury bills, overdrafts and by
loans from revenue. Another big loan will be
wanted by August, and a good part of it will be
spent in advance. Last loan floated with difficulty
at a low price, and that was only a few months
ago. The next one might not float at all, for
W.A. has been going on such a howling riot that
its paper is not in high esteem now, and then the
Forrest policy would crumple up like a Chinese
lantern struck by the Day of Judgment.
OUTSIDE A SYDNEY FISH-SHOP.
OLD GENT (excitedly) : “ Dear, dear; what a
splendid specimen of the ornithorhyncus
paradoxus !”
MR. BIGSCRUB: “No blanky fear — it’s a platypus !"
Pertinent questions addressed by Schey,
M.L.A., to the pious Brunker, N.S.W. Acting-
Premier :
(1.) Is it his intention to direct the Inspector-General oi
Police to prosecute the Reverend William Curnow foi
desecrating the Lord’s day by writing articles for the
editorial columns of the Sydney Morning Herald?
(2.) Is it also his intention to direct the necessary stepa
to be taken to restrain the firm of James Fairfax and
Sons from keeping their shop open on the evening of the
Lord’s day, and employing Christian young women to set
in type on the Lord’s day evening the Reverend William
Curnow’s leading articles?
(3.) Is it also his intention to instruct the proper officer
to prohibit James Fairfax and Sons’ reporters from follow-
ing their avocation on the Lord’s day by reporting the
sermons of the Minister in charge of the Pitt-street Con-
gregational Church and of other Ministers?
(4.) Does he intend so to enforce the laws of the country
as to restrain the Postmaster-General from carrying on
Lord’s day letters and despatches ?
Part of the vitriol in this lies in the fact that
Editor Curnow is Brunker’s brother-in-law. The
pained expression of Brunker’s countenance is the
only answer which Schey is likely to get. The
man who alludes to the stately pile where Parson
Curnow cogitates over his leading article as a
“shop”—-as if the Fairfaxes sold five cigars for a
shilling—is too dreadful to be answered seriously.

After N.S.W. Attorney-General Want had
stated his case for the Little Cornstalk Party with
much fury and damned Federation in all its aspects,
R. E. O’Connor, M.L.C., floored him badly by
reading out of Hansard an old speech in which
King Jack declared himself an ardent Federa-
tionist. That warlike relio of the cabbage-tree-hat
mob can seldom recollect what his last year’s
opinions were—he forgets his political oonvictions
while hunting up worms for bait —and the news
that he had once been a shrieking advocate of the
Commonwealth crumpled him up. But only tem-
porarily.
Some Jubilee newspaper-titles were ex-
pected in Australia, but none came. “ I believe I
am right,” says Sydney D.T.’s London special,
“in stating that after conferring together, Mr.
Reid, Sir George Turner, Mr. Seddon and Mr.
Kingston urged Mr. Chamberlain to mark the
Jubilee by some recognition of the Australian
press, each Premier naming a suitable eminent
editor for titular distinction. Unfortunately, cer-
tain rivals of the gentlemen nominated who are
now in England got wind of the project, and ex-
hibited virtuous and disinterested indignation.
Mr. Chamberlain thereupon roundly condemned
the small-mindedness of colonial newspaper folk,
and declined to put his head in a hornets’ nest to
oblige.” In other words, putting facts together,
editor Finlayson who, when at home in Adelaide,
runs the Tory Register, derided the idea of his
deadly rival Bonython, of the Advertiser (King-
ston’s friend), being bebaubled. Also, such of the
Fairfax brigade as happened to be on the spot pos-
sibly jeered at the proposed making of Sir Lachlan
Brient, of Sydney D.T., proposed in all gratitude
by Premier Reid. Also—several other things.
Salomons, Q.C., in the course of his anti-
Federation speech in N.S.W. Leg. Council, re-
lated a remarkable number of anecdotes, most of
which, ouriously enough, had reference to women.
Speaking of the close resemblance of Barton and
O’Connor in the Federation movement, he said he
was reminded of the man who married a girl who
had a twin sister very much like her. When
somebody asked how he’ managed to distinguish
between them he said, “ I don’t try ! ”
A vice-regal visit to an inland centre
being in view, the private sec. of an Australian
Governor wired to the local M.P. asking him to
secure rooms and a carriage for vice-regal use
during the stay. The M.P. complied, and when
the Govt. House people arrived their appreciation
of the hotel accommodation was only discounted
by the scorn with which they regarded the carriage
hired for them. In vain did the member plead
that it was the best vehicle available. They could
never ride in a thing like that; and their grief
seemed so pathetic that the M.L.A.’s heart was
touched to the extent of offering his own turn-
out on the understanding that he had the hired
one. Thanking him effusively, the suite luxuri-
ated in his victoria, but, on the termination of
their visit, when he forwarded the livery-stable
keeper’s bill for hire to the private sec., a polite
answer came back expressing surprise that “Mr.
Dash, M.L.A., could expect His Lordship to pay
for the hire of the carriage, seeing that Mr. Dash
himself had the use of the vehicle.” The M.L.A.
sent his answer, which was strong and to the
point, and per same post Govt. House received a
note from the hotel proprietor demanding im-
mediate settlement of his account and requesting
His Lordship to patronise another house next visit.

It is explained that the ruddy color of the
British—haw ! —new-ehum who lands on these
shores is largely due to the British habit of using
Scrubb’s ammonia at the toilet. This celebrated
preparation has a multitude of remarkable cleans-
ing virtues, and is for sale by all reputable Aus-
tralian chemists. *
Messrs. Wallen and Woods, the latest
share-dealing firm established in Melb., embraces
Mr. Frank Wallen, son of the late Mr. Robert
Wallen, one of the founders of Melb. Stock Ex-
change, and Mr. Norman Woods, also well known
in Southern mining circles. Both partners are
from the firm of Clarke and Co., Melb., where
they have occupied leading positions during the
past ten years.*
Stock Returns. —End of’9l there were
61,831,416 sheep in N S.W. : in ’96, 48,318,790.
Opinions vary from 5 to 20 million on the probable
shortage this year. Perhaps 10 millions is a
shrewd estimate, which means about 38 million as
the colony’s present woolly wealth. A redeeming
feature is that Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd. are
selling stores at double the prices going before the
break up.*
“ H.”: “Two men, both residents in
Little Leveson-street, N. Melb., were killed by
accident, one day this week, at opposite ends of
the city. Little Leveson-street is only 100 yards
long, with houses on only one side. More Long
Arm.”
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free’ or 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The T. A. Dibbs presentation at the head-
office of the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney,
on Aug. 1, by his “ brother officers,” was a strong
and moving lesson to the young bloods of the
service. No man of the many present can ever
hope to look as T. A. Dibbs looks with 50 years’
hard service on his forehead, and probably no
other man present could speak honestly from his
heart with more affecting and simple earnestness
about a woman. Mr. Dibbs stumbled, broke down,
cleared his throat—-“nothing could have pleased
him more than the presentation of this oil-paint-
ing of my wife; the officers all know that, if
they want the general manager, and can’t find
him at the bank, they are sure to find him
at home. If there is a silent advocate for the
officers of the bank, it is my wife. ’’ Une of the
bank’s messengers was crying. Meanwhile, big
brother' George looked on with the interested
air of an amiable bear. The gathering at the pre-
sentation matched the occasion in size and im-
portance.

Various fancies for the Caulfield G.N. Hurdle
Race, run on Saturday (7th), have lost their rosy appear-
ance within the last week or two, so at time of writing-
nobody seems to be sweet on anything. Response suffered
a fall at Moonee Valley, the other day, whilst Tagus and
Hive were shaping moderately in the same event. Of
these three G.N. horses, Hive would carry The Bulletin’s
hopes on Saturday if he were at his best, seeing that the
Millerites lately talked of him as a likely “double”
winner. If he goes to the post, well backed by the stable
in preference to Insurance, punters will follow Hive and
perhaps get a win for their money ; also the same may
be said of the mare Insurance if she goes instead of
Hive. Another dangerous pair are Glendower and Zouroff,
though the latter seems more threatening for the Steeple-
chase, a week later. The outlook is very cloudy. In sug-
gesting that Hive, Zouroff, Glendower, and Insurance
may possibly include the winners of the two big events,
The Bulletin doesn’t mean to suggest that its “tip” is
necessarily worth the paper it is printed on. Meanwhile,
this journal has no fancy for Hayseed’s chance in the
Steeplechase, which may be a point in the horse’s favor
rather than otherwise.

The V. A.T.C. is so close-fisted repress tickets
that when the reporter for Melb. Paunch gets his free pass
he grovels before the “courteous secretary ” in this style:
“The general arrangements at Caulfleld since Mr. Tom
Moule took charge of affairs have been so excellent that
to speak of them now is like
‘ To gild refined gold, to paint the lily,
To throw a perfume on the violet.’”
Quite so ; it is as unnecessary as the addition of new odors
to the onion I but Air. Moule is not the only fragrant
vegetable attached to the club. There are—well, it would
be really cruel to name one member of the committee.
* * * *
The Alexandra Plate (three miles), at Ascot,
once won by the well-known Musket, though valued at
£l5OO this year, resolved itself into a match'in which St.
Bris, started at 100—7 on, managed to just get home bv a
short neck. Long-distance racing is evidently at a dis-
count in Eng.
* * * *
Irish-bred horses are carrying all before them
in Eng., for, in addition to Gaitee More’s successive vic-
tories, Count Schomberg accounted for the Queen’s Gold
Vase at Ascot, and more recently he won the Goodwood
Cup (2| miles), for which event Persimmon was scratched.
• * * •
Canterbury Park (Sydney) Cup was run off in
three divisions, Survivor winning his heat and the final.
Although the innovation was made in the interests of the
lives and limbs of horses and jockeys, the course-proprie-
tors may hear from the A.J.C., as it is opposed to the
latter’s rules.
* * * ■*
Mr. Buckland did not have the luck he deserved
for taking his trotting stud to Victoria. Viva ran a great
race, when starting from 45sec. behind, and was only
beaten by a few' inches. The course was too heavy from
rain for Fritz to have any show of breaking his record,
but, to prevent disappointment, Mr. B. sent him a mile,
which he covered in 2min. 25sec.
* * * •
The M.L. and N.S.W. final football-match
began with the usual lunatic remarks “ Rick, Rick,
Ricketty Dick, houpla-ha ! ” from the N.S.W. side, and
the usual insane response “ Ake, ake, kia kia” from the
Maorilanders, and, directly these noises ceased, the
crow T d guffawed with its usual tremendousness, after
which both teams walked over to the grandstand and
hurrahed Gov. ’Ampden. Why thev didn’t “houpla”
or “ake” H.E. is explained by the fact that the football
mind doesn’t reckon ’Ampden worth such an honor.
“ This is what I like,” snorted one Jumbo with sarcastic
emphasis, “ Ch-er ’im—’Ampden ; strike me!” but he
hurrahed louder than anybody. This shows to what base
uses a person with a noble name can come in Australia.
On Monday the ALL. team, in a more or less incoherent
state, tumbled itself into two drags, and, after throwing
kisses to the ahlgails of its hotel, drove off and em-
barked for home. Ake, ake—hooray !
