Port Adelaide beat South Adelaide, and is now
at the head of affairs in Adelaide football. But all the
clubs ought to be disqualified for suspicious play in the
interests of the “ gate.”
* * * ,
In one respect, perhaps in several, Rugby foot-
ball has gone back during the last 18 years. Formerly
the men behind the scrum were one full", two halves, and
two quarter backs. The “full” remains; the two
“ halves” have increased to four, and are called “ three-
quarters”; the old “quarters” have become “ halves,”
but without a numerical increase. Now, Sydney clubs
have a “ five-eights,” and the “ wing-forwards” are really
“ one-eight,” or perhaps “ quarter” back.
* * * •
The bicycle craze has reached its climax. When
the s.s. Wainui struck the rocks near Nelson, ALL., on
July 12, and commotion ensued, and men ran about the
saloon in their night-shirts and pyjamas calling loudly for
life-belts, a wheel-enthusiast of the frailer sex cried, not
for help or a life-belt, but “My bike ! Aly bike ' Oh
save my bike ! ”
* « » •
A champion horror was the death of a man
thrown from a cycle, the other day, in Chicago, while
carrying round his neck a bike-frame, which got jammed
on his windpipe. Then people stood round and grinned
at what they thought were his drunken antics till the
poor wretch was dead of strangulation.
• * * •
London papers call attention to the well-mean-
ing dishonesty of a young man who “ shook ” two
bicycles. With the proceeds of the first theft he opened
an account in the P.O. Savings Bank. The second was a
lady’s bike. He exchanged it for a sewing-machine, which
he gave to his wife as a birthday present.
* * * *
All hope that the N.S.W. footballers would on
Saturday last repeat their victory over the M.L. team
was soon dispelled when play began ; the visitors scored
a try almost as soon as the ball was set rolling, and sim-
ply romped over the local men for the rest of the game,
winning by 20 points to 3. The N.S. Wales men, as usual,
played a selfish game in contradistinction to the Maori-
landers, who, in combination and short passes, were as
nearly as possible perfect. Whilst the individual play of
the N.S.W. men was in many instances excellent, their
lack of combination and determination to hang on to the
ball until it was fairly “ vvrastled ” from them made vic-
tory impossible against such a team as opposed them on
Saturday.
* * * •
That unhappy combination, the Australian base-
ball team, has disbanded in London, and some of the
members have cabled for funds to carry them home.

Percy Cavill was beaten by both Derbyshire
and Green in the half-mile swimming championship at
Southport, Eng., on Saturday. Derbyshire won by 15yds.
m 13min. 38sec., whilst Green beat Cavill by a touch.
Amateur A. T. Kenny swam half a mile in Australia in
1891 in 13min. 12 4-ssec.
Hall, the Vie. miner convicted of murdering his
wife, was one of the most brilliant footballers of the pro-
vince. He intended moving' to Melb. and playing with a
metropolitan club this season, but Fate ordained other-
wise.
* . * • •
“ Life in the old dog yet.” Last year Spofforth
had a better average than any of the first-class English
bowlers, and did not come far behind M'Kibbin. Though
he did not do too much, and was not pitted against killing
batsmen all the time, still the performance is noteworthy
when we remember that it is nearly 20 years since his
bowling first electrified the world. The fairness of his
delivery was never questioned—a remark which does not
apply to M'Kibbin.
if., ... * * *
Vic. rowing circles are still agitated re the
amateur question. Recently a rule was passed excluding
from competition athletes who had competed for cash in
any branch of sport whatever. This was brought about
chiefly by the harriers, callow youths who indulge in aim-
less runs across paddocks on Saturday afternoons. It
was pointed out at the time that such a step was unneces-
sary, if not harmful, and that it would be enough to let
the sport stand on its own basis. However, the bigoted
amateur party prevailed. The country oarsmen were en-
tirely against the innovation, and protested vigorously.
They now propose to form a new association in opposition
to the present V.R.A., whose most striking features are
its state of chronic insolvency and utterly hopeless future.
Consequently the new association, if started on a capital
of nothing, with an annual income of ninepence, would
have an immensely brighter outlook.
Anyhow, rowing enthusiasts need not worry—
there is no fear of rowing becoming a professional sport
at present. As an instance, the Maiden Eight at the
Association regatta, in Feb., ’96, got an entry of eight
crews, Which, at 7s. 6d. an oar (£3 per crew) gave £24 in
entry-money. The winning crew got its prizes about three
months ago—a trophy, value £2los. for each man, or £2O
for the crew. The affair was thus even worse than a
sweepstake. The association not only' added no money,
it stuck to one-sixth of the entry-fee. A man who could
make money out of such a sport, after paying his training
and other expenses, club membership-fees, and giving his
cox. a trifle as well, would be a genius.
The Vic. A.N.A. has decided to hold another
art-union in January' in alleged support of the charities.
No account is ever made of the actual number of tickets
sold to the public. The one just past probably absorbed
£7OOO of public money, of which £l7OO went to the
charities, £l5OO went in art-union prizes, the balance
being swallowed up in bicycle prizes and expenses—chiefly
expenses. Tasmania will have no more A.N.A. gambles.
It has reserved the Tight Little Island for Tattersall.
Tattersall returns 18s. in the £ as prizes. The A.N.A.
returns something less than ss. in the £. N.S.W. public
will not have further opportunities of participating in this
fool-gamble, as the Attorney-General has put down his
historic foot on the biz. Art-unions are a fruitful source
of bad debts. Some thousands of A.N.A. tickets were
sent to Westralia, but not a penny received in return.
Some of the tickets were got back after expensive tele-
graphing.

The deadly earnestness of the Australasian
riflemen seems to have stimulated British shooting
just as Murdoch’s first team shook up British cricket,
and the effect was more immediate. The record per-
formance of the Victorian eight in the Kolapore Cup
shooting was followed by bigger individual records in the
Queen’s Prize tournament.
• * * »
Cyclist Kellow tells a yarn of a certain sassiety
rider who, in the first days of the pneumatic tyre, gave
strong indications of becoming a champion. He had
raced as a pro. with some success, but suddenly' was
missed from the training-track, and appeared there no
more. Meeting him in the street one day, Kellow asked
the of the toney one’s retirement. “Well, you
see, replied the youth, “ the guvnah objects to my asso-
ciating with the class of fellows one meets on the track.”
A year or two later Kellow set out for Coolgardie, and the
first figure he saw on entering the dusty township was
that of the late cyclist. He was clad in moleskins with
knee-strings, flannel-shirted, and was engaged in beltin"
a stubborn horse with the flat of a spade amid the cheers
and blasphemy of the aggregate local Bill. “Hullo,”
said Kellow, “ doesn’t the guvnah object to vour asso-
ciating with this class of fellows ? ”
* « * *
Golf, as played around Sydney, needs suppres-
sing. Clubs at Bondi and Randwick play across a public
road, and any pedestrian who doesn't want to be an angel
rather suddenly must watch the players, be on the alert
to dodge, and sidle over the track like a crab. For those
not quick at dodging, the safest place is close to any of
the holes : the caddies preserve their lives by keeping
near them. r °
* * * »
Vic. Public Works Department, while improv-
ing the Yarra t.y dredging and dirt-shifting, is utilising
said dirt in building the foundation of what should be
one of the finest cycling tracks in Australia—in the
adjoining Friendly Society Grounds. The present founda-
tion—chiefly clay—allows for a track 50ft. wide, banked
up to a height of 14ft. on the outside, and will go about
3£ laps to the mile. There is abundant room for stands
bars, spectators, &c., but—isn’t the day of the bike-race
departing ?
* * * *
N.Q. fish perjury. One of a party fishing
lately in the Barcoo abandoned his tackle and went up
the bank to All his pipe. A fish collared line and hard-
wood rod and made off for deep water. In the afternoon
higher up stream, another fisherman got a bite, and
hauled in fish, rod and tackle to the joy of the sorrowing
owner. 6
* * * *
An English cycle-riding teacher named Mark-
ham lately went through the London traffic accompanied
by five bicycles. He carried two across his back and
shoulders, led one in either hand, and rode the fifth
“ hands off.” Not quite so mad as the ’Murkan who rode
down U.S. Capitol steps, losing his pedals soon after the
start, and clearing the last flight at one bound. He got
out of it luckily. Ninety-five per cent, would break a
limb or probably a neck.
* • * *
The main difference between the bookmaker
and the insurance man, considered as gamblers, remarks
a Bulletin correspondent, is that the insurance man
doesn’t knowingly lay against a stiff, whereas the bookie
does, whenever possible.
* * * *
The vice-president of a newly-formed cycling
club is responsible for the following. A local cyclist
had slung his bike by a rope in the cellar. During
the night a mouse on an exploring expedition climbed
down the rope and landed on the front wheel of the
machine, causing it to revolve, and the more frantically
mousie tried to climb the more furiously did the wheel
rotate. . . In the morning a grey and gaunt animal
was discovered whirling round despite his most strenuous
efforts, and the cyclometer registered a distance too
fabulous to chronicle in this unbelieving age
*** " B »
The English track and road 24 hours’ records’
viz., G. Patterson with 502 miles, and M. A. Holbein with
402 miles, have lately been established on Dunlop cycles
and Dunlop tyres. For the week ending 9th June last
English riders won in all 618 prizes on Dunlop tyres. W
Smith, winner of the recent one-mile amateur champion-
ship at Brisbane, and C. 11. New, winner of the five-mile
scratch at the same meeting, rode Dunlop tyres. A.
Calder, winner of the Port Melbourne C.C. road race on
July 31st—20 miles in 60min. —rode a Dunlop-tyred Hum-
ber. The Dunlop Tyre’s Australian business is one of the
most prosperous concerns in the country.*

The Austral Cycle Agency will shortly open
new premises in Sydney, next door to its present big well-
known establishment. Besides a magnificent stock of
cycles, this firm is in the van of the times with such useful
accessories as the “ Martin Silent Tool Bag,” “ The Dunlop
Mud Guard," and the “ Dunlop Carrier.” Its cycles are/
always getting first places on the racing-track, while upor if
tme road they have a most enviable reputation.* /

Q. Central Board of Health has reported
adversely to the shriek of Brisbane W.C.T.U. in
wanting the C.D. Act abolished.
Melbourne Gas Co. makes elaborately
obscure explanations about the two women who
were found suffocated in a cottage the other day.
The house-father, who—poor wretch ! —had been
working in the country, came home and banged
upon the locked door, but received no answer, so
concluded that his wife and daughter were
out. By-and-bye he banged again, to no pur-
pose. Later he came back and banged some more,
and, growing alarmed, he burgled the cottage.
Then he found that his first idea was not wholly
correct, though to all intents and purposes they
had gone out permanently. They had been suffo-
cated by gas two or three days previously, the gas
having leaked from a broken main into a broken
drain-pipe, and filtered through the sink into the
room while they slept. Now, the gas co. people
explain that nobody was really responsible. The
leakage had been reported to a clerk, and he had
reported it to the indoor inspector, and as he was
unable to locate it he reported it to the superin-
tendent or somebody, and he was in the act of
gradually drawing the attention of the outdoor
inspector to it—or something of that sort —when
these two beastly sudden people went and died.
And the general attitude of the co. is that, if they
had only followed the proper routine, and given
three days’ notice in writing on the proper form
j which could be obtained at the office that they
intended to be suffocated on such a date, the clerk
would have given the form to the managing
director, and he would have laid it before the
board to be initialled and filed, and then things
might have panned out quite differently.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away

Is. bottles. Made in London *
Unless Australia’s old friend, Flora Shaw,
of the London Times, is romancing very wildly,
she was the main cause of Jameson’s famous
Transvaal raid. Her story is, practically, that
she cabled to Cecil Rhodes assuring him that
Chamberlain was favorable to the invasion, and
thus set the whole trouble in motion, whereas
Chamberlain swears that he knew nothing about
the scheme. Miss Shaw acknowledges now that she
had no reason to believe Chamberlain was favor l
able, so there is a lot of Ananias about the matter
somewhere. Possibly Rhodes wasn’t deceived by
Flora’s cablegram, and it was only fixed up that
he might have some excuse to fail back upon in
case of need. Possibly, also, Chamberlain did
know something, but as he can’t acknowledge that
now, it may be that Flora is left in the hole by
mutual agreement. Possibly she is only in the
hole gratis to gratify her devouring vanity and to
pose as a person who started wars and shook up
empires all “on her little own.” Flora, by her
own tale, is responsible for Rhodes’s downfail, the
Transvaal raid, the Matabele war which naturally
followed, and the awful butchery which was the
natural consequence of the war. And she didn’t
get into gaol. She wasn’t even informally shaken
by the Parliamentary Committee and her bonnet
knocked endways, which seems to be the very
least that could have been done to even such a
nice woman.

Twelve Months of Torture.
A CHIEF STEWARD’S STRANGE STORY
That section of unfortunate humanity who have
experienced and still experience the tortures of
sciatica and rheumatism will be profoundly in-
terested in the story of Chief Steward Ernest
Buckhold, of s.s. Menmuir (Eastern and Australian
Steamship Company). Mr. Buckhold is now in
his 51st year, and was born at Hamburg, Ger-
many. He has been seafaring for 32 years; is
well known in Sydney, and has been a householder
at Ash field and Burwood for 14 years. His con-
nection with the E. and A.S.S. Company extends
over 10 years, and he has during that time been
trading between China, Japan, and Australian
ports. His case having attracted much attention
and caused considerable comment in local shipping
circles, a press representative called upon Mr.
Buckhold at 226 George-street, Sydney. The chief
steward readily volunteered to recite the circum-
stances of his case, and Mrs. Buckhold, who was
present, assisted and confirmed her husband in the
following statement:—
“ I arrived in Sydney in May, 1896, from Japan
suffering severely from pains in the right leg. The
trouble began gradually, but increased daily to an
aoute stage. I ascribe the malady to exposure and
wet feet. The pains were first in the hip, and
occasionally shot to the calf and the foot. Owing
; to the severity of the punishment, I had
no peace or rest night or day. I could not
sit _ down to a meal, but had to lean heavily
against the table. The pain took away all desire
for food, and I could not sleep. I had to leave the
ship, and take to bed at Ashfield. I got worse,
and was bed-ridden, and in great agony for three
months from the latter end of May. My hip grew
out and the joint bone in the back protruded.
After three months I improved slightly, and was
able to get about the house with the assistance of
two sticks. But it was a great struggle to move,
and caused excruciating pain. I could not bear
to put the foot to the floor. Two months
afterwards, in September, I had to take to bed
again. ”
“Yes,” interposed Mrs. Buckhold, “and the
doctor would almost cry when he came to see him
to inject morphia. Eor 70 nights morphia was
injected ; sometimes the doctor would call as late
as midnight, and when he did not call I adminis-
tered the injection.”
“ Well, ” continued Buckhold, “ I remained in
bed till the 31st December last. It was then the
doctor urged me to go to the Camden Convalescent
Hospital for a change, and I must say the doctor
had been most kind and attentive, and tried hard
to cure me. Through being ill and out of work so
long, my circumstances became reduced, and I had
to give up house and home. I could not keep up
an establishment any longer, and came to live in
Sydney with friends of ours, on the 7th January
of this year.”
“I sa » Captain Green, the superintendent of
the E. and A.S.S. Company, and he granted me a
passage to Japan as an assistant in the steward’s
department. This was with the hope that the
change would effect a cure. Five days after leav-
ing Sydney the Australian arrived at Townsville,
and the full fury of the malady returned. I was
unable to go to the table for food, and would rather
go withont a meal than walk a short distance. The
slightest movement meant indescribable torture.
Yet, there there was no cessation from suffering
while I kept quiet. When I arrived in Japan, I
thought to take a position in an European club at
Kobe, but I found that the incessant pain in-
capacitated me for any work. I was compelled to
return by the Australian, and arrived in Sydney
on the 13th May last, a physical wreck, and a bag
of bones, and with undiminished pain. Two days
latter I met Captain H. Craig, of the s.s. Guthrie
(E. and A.S.S. Company), and he advised me
to try Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. At
the same he handed me JJ1, and told me to get the
pills at once, which I did. I followed the direc-
tions, and, after taking three boxes, I felt much
improved. I continued with the fourth, taking six
puls a day, two after each meal, with interesting
and happy results. Of the fifth and sixth boxes I
took nine a day, three after each meal, and had
four pills left when I had become perfectly strong,
healthy, and robust. I can now walk any distance
without fatigue, and suffer no pain whatever. As
for my appetite, it is disgracefully keen and in-
satiable. The recovery was gradual at first, and
then rapid towards absolute cure. I began the
pills on the 15th May, and put the unswallowed
four aside on the 14th June. I have taken none
since, for my health has been tliororougly re-estab-
lished. And this will prove it: I was called to
the shipping office to see Captain Green, and he
handed me a note for Captain Legg, of the s.s.
Thornley, at Messrs. Gibbs, Bright and Co.’s
office, Pitt-street; there I arranged to go as chief
steward as far as Adelaide, to ascertain how I
would get along on a sea voyage. Well, the work
was hard, and the weather bad, and my feet and
clothing were often wet, but I fared first-class
and suffered no ill-effects. I returned to Sydney
on the 5th July by the A.U.S.N. Company’s
steamer Waroonga in perfect health and strength.
I now feel fit for any work. ”
“Yes, I passed through twelve months of un-
speakable suffering, which I am sure would have
been averted had I known of the extraordinary
efficacy of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. However, I trust my experience may
bring hope to the hearts of others suffering from
sciatica.”

Bananaland cannot be called illiberal in
the matter of its education vote. Unfortunately,
a considerable section thereof is wasted on the
fancy fripperies of Superior Grammar-school edu-
cation. At the same time, there are hundreds and
hundreds of bush children running almost wild
and growing up veritable savages, just for want of
a peripatetic solioolmaster.
“This man,” said a Wellington (M.L.)
Benevolent Society trustee, pointing at a long,
black object that had applied for relief, “ is a
living example of how dirty a man can be if he
cares to.” The grimy apparition appeared before
the board about Jubilee time, and acknowledged,
with a happy smile which cracked the landed pro-
perty on his countenance, that he hadn’t washed
or changed his'clothes since Nov., 1896. the
board walked slowly round the earthy applicant
and took stock of him, and he took it all cheer-
fully until an order was issued that he should be
immediately scrubbed and have clean clothes.
Then he opened a mouth that was hung round
with a loamy beard, and protested that the
clothes he had on were perfectly comfortable.
He was hunted off by the board’s office Bumble on
his way to the tank still protesting, and the last
that was heard of him was a voice dying away in
the distance which complained bitterly that this
sort of thing would lead to pneumonia for certain.

Editor Bulletin, —I have read the article
in your issue for 17th July, p. 13, commencing
“ The admission of the budding poet.” Your cor-
respondent does not know what he is talking
about. Barristers never have to serve their articles
in England or elsewhere. (1) In England, they
have to “eat dinners”, and pass a very stiff
final examination for the call. It is quite as easy
for English barristers to be admitted to practise
both as barristers and solicitors in Victoria as in
Maoriland ; (2) and I could mention a barrister
who has so been admitted in Victoria some years
ago, and is in practice now. (3) As a matter of
fact, the difficulty in Maoriland is greater, because
they have there to pass a special examination in
M. law, in so far as it differs from the law of
England. And in neither province can persons not
previously admitted practise as solicitors without
having served articles, or undergone some equiva-
lent education and training. (4) As for being an
ex-footballer, (5) I can’t see the point. It would
do your correspondent good to take up football
himself. J. G. Collins.
[Mr. Collins, despite the facts, as detailed in one of his
affidavits, that he is the author of a poem entitled “ The
Wisdom-Giving Sea,” and that he has studied “ the
abstruse and forgotten science of metaphysics,” misappre-
hends. (1) The paragraph complained of doesn’t make
any statement on this matter. As a matter of general in-
terest, it may be mentioned that Mr. Collins is right as to
England. (2) This is not so. Admission in Vic-
toria is absolutely barred to English barristers pure
and simple, on account of the absence of a recip-
rocal right of admission in England to Victorian
barristers and solicitors. (The exceptional cases specially
provided for may be disregarded here.) In Maoriland, no
conditions of reciprocity obstruct the English barrister
(see Legal Practitioners Acts, 18S2 and 1883). But, in
addition to this bar against the admission of English and
N. barristers in Vic., it was thought, until Mr. Collins’
case, that the absence of articles in the Eng. and N.S.W.
bar-course was an additional chevaux defnse against their
admission. In fact, a N.S.W. barrister, Forbes, was re-
fused admission by Vic. Court on grounds that satisfied
several Eng. barristers, who had come to Vic. for the ex-
press purpose of being admitted, of the futility of
applying. But now, if this case can be taken as a
precedent, an English or N.S.W. barrister who has had
some actual experience in his profession may, by getting
admitted in a province reciprocating with Victoria ( e.g .,
M.L. or Tas.), get—if his educational qualification is suffi-
cient-admitted in Victoria. The decision is important
if that is its effect, for it will remove the rather galling
checks at present existing on the power of a lawyer to go
from province to province as the state of business calls him.
The result of the Vic. Supreme Court refusals in t.he past
has been the shutting of the door with a slam in the face
of Victorians by the courts of N.S.W., Queensland, and
W.A. It is, accordingly, to be regretted that there is no
better report of the case than the rather lame one
in the current notes of the Argus law reports. (3)
It would not be difficult to name 20 or 30 English
barristers admitted in Victoria, but not one, with the
exception of Mr. Collins himself, since the coming into
operation of the Vic. amalgamation system in 1891.
(4) Mr. Collins seems to he hazy on another point. No
person can be admitted in Victoria as a barrister only, or
as a solicitor onlv : he must be admitted as a barrister
and solicitor. (5) Now, re Mr. Collins’ reference to the
remark that he was an ex-footballer, we can only refer
him to his own affidavit as published in the Age of 27th
May last, in which he is made to state that he “had
obtained a certain amount of prowess” in gymnastics,
<< as well as in the football field, but that he did not regard
excellence in athletics as one of the chief objects of his
education." Such matters are surely of sufficient interest
in a sport-loving community to justify passing allusion.—
Ed. B.j
A Boon to Sufferers.—A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address —J. KILIAN, Store
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*
For the first six months of 1897, N.S.W.
lost 106 people to Victoria and 704 to Queensland
by excess of departures over arrivals. On tin-
other hand, it gained 2397 from S.A.—mostly
Broken Hillites, who had formerly gone to Wes
tralia, and are now plodding back in disgust to
their own lead-poisoned town, and giving up tin
land of red dust and booms as a bad job.

THE EXPERIENCE OF A LONDESBORO
YOUNG LADY.
A VICTIM OF SEVERE PAINS, DIZZI-
NESS, AND WATERY BLOOD—AT TIMES
COULD NOT GO UP A STEP-HOW SHE
REGAINED HEALTH AND STRENGTH
From the Clinton New Era.
Miss Kate Longman is a young lady of about 22
years of age, who lives with her mother in the
pretty little village of Londesboro, six miles from
the town of Clinton. Both are well-known and
highly esteemed by their many friends. The New
Era having learned that Miss Longman had
been a great sufferer and had recently been re-
stored to health by the timely use of a well-
known popular remedy, despatched a representative
to get the particulars of the case. In reply to the
reporter’s inquiries, Miss Longman said that if
her experience might be the means of helping
some other sufferer, she was quite willing that it
should be made public. “For a long time,” she
said, “1 was very poorly I was weak, and run
down, and at times suffered pains in my back
that were simply awful. My blood was in a
watery condition, and I was subject to spells of
weakness to such an extent that I could not
step up a door - step to save my life. I
doctored a great deal for my sickness, but
without avail. At last, after having fre-
quently read in the New Era of cures effected
by the use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, I de-
termined to give them a trial. The result was
that my health soon began to return and the
pains and weakness left me, and I was again
restored to strength.” At this moment Mrs.
Longman entered, and being informed who the
visitor was and what was his mission, said : “ Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are the greatest medicine
known. My daughter was so sick that I feared
she would die, and she continually grew weaker
until she began the use of Pink Pills, and they
have cured her, and she has not had a recurrence
of the trouble since.” Miss Longman is now the
picture of health, and declares that Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills are entitled to the credit. The New
Era knows of many others who have benefitted
by this remarkable remedy.
Pale and Sallow Girls .
What can be more distressing than to see a
girl drooping and fading in the springtime of
youth ? Instead of bright eyes, glowing rosy
cheeks, and an elastic step, there are duu eyes,
pale, sallow, or greenish complexion, and a lan-
guidness of step that bespeak disease and an
early death if proper treatment is not properly
resorted to and persisted in until the impoverished
blood is enriched and the functions of life become
regular. Upon parents rests a great responsibility
at the time their daughters are budding into
w manhood. If your daughter is pale, complains
of weakness, is “tired out” upon the slightest
exertion ; if she is troubled with headache or
backache, pain in the side, if her temper is fitful
and her appetite poor, she is in a condition of ex-
treme peril, a fit subject for the development of
the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly ; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks ; bright eyes and light-
ness of step will surely follow their use, and all
danger of consumption and premature death will
be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will insist
upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’ Pink

A DIAMOND DIGGER’S REMARKABLE
STATEMENT IN SOUTH AFRICA.
HE CONFIRMS HIS ASSERTIONS
BEFORE A J.P.
A press representative, while on a visit to Kim-
berley recently, had an interesting interview with
H. C. T Fischer, of that town, which may be of
interest to our readers. Mr. Fischer for years
was a gold digger, prospector, and diamond digger
in and around Kimberley. Naturally, during all
these years, Mr. Fischer had been leading a life
of hardship and exposure Often for days at a
time he was wet to the skin, and he has on more
than one occasion been without food or shelter of
any kind. It is not to be wondered at, therefore,
that Mr. Fischer fell a prey to rheumatism, and
that terrible malady, when it had entered his
system, took so strong a hold on him as to make
his life one long period of misery. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, however, have now thoroughly cured
him, as will be seen from the following statement,
y-tK
/t
1
which Mr. Fischer, for the benefit of suffering
fellow-creatures, and out of gratitude for the
marvellous benefits he has received from the
medicine, made to this representative :—“ It is
now about ten years ago since I first began to
suffer. At first the rheumatism used to afflict me
for only a few weeks at a time and then would
leave me, but whenever I got wet I was always
certain of an attack. The disease gradually got
worse and worse until in 1895, when I was living
at Klerksdorp, I was taken very badly with it,
and was compelled to give up work. I consulted
doctors, and used every conceivable medicine, but
still I got worse and worse. I became so bad
that I could not bear to be touched, and so
terribly weak was I that I could not even lift
myself in the bed to take refreshments, but had
to be gently raised and lowered again. Every
movement gave me intense agony. The pain ex-
tended through my arms, shoulders, spine, thighs,
knees, and ankles, in fact, I was one mass of pain.
So bad was I that I often used to shout out with
agony. My head constantly ached, I could
neither eat nor sleep, and when from exhaustion
I did fall into a doze, I never lost the terrible
pain, and my dreams were horrible. You can
imagine how bad I was when my wife had con-
stantly to attend me, and could never leave the
house. I cannot think it possible that any man
can ever have suffered more that I did. If any-
one offered me .£20,000 to go through the same
agony again that I experienced during those
eight months I would say, ‘ No, keep your money,
I prefer to be as I am.’ At last, after consulting
any number of doctors and using all the medicines
possible to think of, a friend of mine, Mr. Wil-
helm Kruger, who is a farmer living at Klerks-
dorp district, recommended me to try Dr. Wil-
liams Pink Pills, as they had thoroughly cured
him. I took his advice, and am now reaping the
benefit of it. The first box of the pills, although
it by no means cured me, made me feel so
much better that I could get about on crutches,
rnjd therefore decided to continue the remedy.
The second box had a marvellous effect. I was
able to throw my crutches away, but as I still
felt a little shaky on my legs I used a stick to get
about with. To make the cure positively certain,
I took a few more boxes of Pills, and after their
use I was able to get about as if I had never been
ill. Since then I have never had a day’s illness,
and although I have been out in all sorts of
weather, and have been soaked to the skin many
and many a time, I have never had the slightest
return of the rheumatism.” When asked by the
representative whether he was absolutely certain
that it was Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills that cured
him, and nothing else, Mr. Fischer replied: “J
am certain that it was the pills alone that saved
my life; and not only have they cured me, but
they have been of marvellous benefit to mv little
daughter also. She could not walk, and her head
troubled her a great deal. She could also neither
eat or sleep, but used to lie in bed crying with
pain. When Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured me
I determined that I would use them for her also.
The result is that now she is as well and strong
as a child could be, and plays all day long as
happy and bright as possible.” Mr. Fischer’s
declaration before D. J. Haarhoff, Esq., J.P.,
M.L.A. : “I, Henry Charles Thomas Fischer, of
1 liompson - street, Kimberly, solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that the statements contained
above concerning the miraculous manner in which
cure d of chronic rheumatism by Dr.
illiams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, are the
Truth, the whole Truth, and nothing but the
Truth. I have given the account of my case
freely and at my own request, hoping that by
making the facts known other sufferers may benefit
by my experience. H. C. T. FISCHER.”
Declared at Kimberley the 21st day of January,
1897. Before me,
D. J. HAARHOFF, J.P.

“F.D. ” from Melbourne to The Bulle-
tin :
The Age, in its usual fashion, mentioned that certain
of the Brunswick rioters were civil servants. It was pro-
bably only an error of memory that caused it to omit
mentioning that a goodly number of the Orangemen in
the procession, which was certainly of the forbidden
party-political nature, were also civil servants. It will he
interesting to note how many of these processionists who
belong to the secret Promotion lodge of the Yellow Pup,
which is confined to members of the public service, wi.l
be hoisted up a yard or two when the well-log-rolled re-
classification dodge comes along as promised (just before
the general elections).

A Sabbath Mom at Waddy .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SxJNDAT-school was “in ” at Waddy. The classes
were all in place, and of the teachers only Brother
Spence was absent, strange to say. This
was the first Sunday of the new superintendent’s
term, always an evil time for grace, and a season
of sulkiness, and bickering, and bad blood. Each
beloved brother coveted the dignity of the office,
and those, who failed to get it were consumed with
envy and all uncharitableness for many Sabbaths
after. Some deserted the little wooden chapel on
the hill till the natural emotions of prayerful men
pent in their bosoms could no longer be borne, and
then they stole back, one by one, and condoned in
hurricanes of exhortation with rain and thunder.
Brother Nehemiah Best occupied the seat of
office behind a deal table on the small platform,
under faded floral decorations left since last anni-
versary. Rumor declared that Brother Best was
unable to write his own name, and whispered that
he spent laborious nights learning the hymns by
heart before he could give them out on Sunday,
as witness the fact that he “read” with equal
facility whether the book was straight, or end-
ways, or upside-down. Brother Best was thin-
voiced, weak in wind, and resourceless and
unconvincing in prayer. No wonder Brother
Spence was disgusted. Brother Spence could
write his own name with scarcely more effort than
it cost him to swing the trucks at the Phoenix;
his voice raised in prayer set the loose shingles
fairly dancing on the old roof ; and his recitation
of “The Drunkard’s Doom ” had been the chief
attraction on Band of Hope nights for years past.
Ernest Spence had not hesitated to express himself
freely at Friday evening’s meeting :
“Ay, they Brother Best, he no more fit for
Ejurin’ out the spirit, yon, than a blin’ kitten
ook at the chest of en ! ”
“ True for en, Ernie ! ” cried Brother Tresize.
“ They old devil, yon, he laugh at Best’s prayin’,
sureli. Brother Spence come tuss, you.”
But Brother Spence had left the meeting in a
state of righteous indignation. Yet here were
Brothers Tresize, and Tregastis, and Praetor, and
Pearce, and Eddy. True, they all looked grim
and unchastened, and there was an uneasy, shifty
feeling in the chapel that inspired boys and girls,
young men and young women, teachers and choir,
with great expectations.
* * * *
Brother Best, in his favorite attitude, with one
arm behind him under his coat tails, his right
hand holding the book a yard from his eyes, his
right foot thrust well out, the toe touching the
floor daintily, made his first official announce-
ment :
“ We will open they service this mornin’ by
sinking hymn won, nought, won.”
Then, in a nasal sing-song, swinging with a
long sweep from toe to heel and heel to toe, he
gave out the first verse and the chorus, ending
unctuously with a smack of the lips at the line: —
Thou beautiful, beautiful Poley Star !
Nehemiah was a dairyman, and had a fixed
conviction that the polar star and a poley cow
had something in common.
The hymn being sung, the superintendent
engaged in prayer, speaking weakly, with a weari-
some repetition of stock phrases, eked out with
labored groans and random cries.
Brother Tresize could not disguise his cynical
disgust, and remained mute. A prayer to be
successful amongst the Wesleyans of Waddy must
make the hearers squirm and wriggle upon their
kjiees, and cry aloud. Brothers and sisters were
Ml happy when moved to wild sobbing, to the
utterance of moans, and groans, and hysterical
appeals to heaven, and when impelled to sustain
a sonorous volley by the vigorous use of pocket-
handkerchiefs ; but that was a spiritual treat that
came only on ce in a while, with the visit of a
specialist, or when the spirit moved Brother
Spence or Brother Tresize to unusual fervor,
The superintendent’s prayer did not raise a
single qualm ; and the boys of class 2 straggled
openly over the forms, pinched each other, and
passed such rubbish as they could collect to Dicky
Haddon, the pale, saintly, ginger-headed boy at
the top of the class, who was in honor bound to
dro P everything so sent him in amongst the
mysteries of the old, yellow, guttural harmonium
through a convenient crack in the back,
Throughout the service Brother Best, proud of
his new office, watched the scholars diligently,
visiting little boys and girls with sudden sharp
ra P s or twitches of the ear if they dared even to
sneeze, but judiciously overlooking much that was
injurious and unbecoming in the bigger boys of
class 2 > who had a vicious habit of sullenly kicking
elderly shins when cuffed or wigged for their mis-
deeds.
m. * ... * , , , *. . .
Tbe Bible-reading, with wonderful, original ex-
Positions of the obscure passages by horny-handed
miners, occupied about half-an-hour, and then the
superintendent stilled the racket and clatter of
Rowing away the tattered books with an authon-

If he was great he could be merciful,

great deliberation spreading a handkerchief large
enough for a bed-cover to save the knees of his
sacred black-cloth trousers, hitching up the latter
*> prevent bagging and finally loosening his paper-

this little corner of Thy vineyard ah Oh Gwad
ah ! be merciful to they sinners what be assembled
here, ah ; pour down Thy speerit upon they, ah,
make they whole, ah. ‘ Oh, Gwad, ah ! Thoo
knowest they be some here, ah, that be wallerin’
sin, ah, some that be hippycrits, ah, some that
b e cheats, ah, some that be scoffers, an’ misbe-
lie vers, an’ heathens, oh, Gwad, ah ! Have mercy
on they people, oh, Gwad, ah! Show they fires
of hell, ah, an’ turn they from the wrath, oh,
Loord Gwad, ah ! ”
Brother Tresize was evidently in fine form this
morning; already the windows were vibrating
before the concussions of his tremendous voice,
and the floor bounded under the great blows that
punctuated his sentences. As he went on, the air
became electrical, and the spirit moved amongst
the flock. The women felt it first,
“Ob, Gwad, ah ! ” interjected Mrs. Eddy from
her corner,
“ Throw up they windies, an let the speerit
in ! ” sobbed Mrs. Best.
Brother Praetor blew his nose with a loud report,
a touching and helpful manifestation.
Brother Tresize prayed with every atom of
energy he possessed. His opinion was on record :
“ A good prayer Sunday mornin’, you, takes it
out of en more’n a hard shift in a hot drive, you.”
When his proper momentum was attained, he
oscillated to and fro between the floor and the
form, swaying back over his heels till his head
almost touched the boards—a gymnastic feat that
was the envy of all the brethren—he shook his
clenched fist at the rafters and reached his highest
note. The plunge forward was accompanied by
falling tones, and ended with a blow on the form
that made every article of furniture in the build-
ing jump. The perspiration ran in streams down
his face and neck ; dry sobs broke from his laboring
chest; long strands of his moist, well-oiled, red
hair separated themselves from the flattened mass
and stood out like feelers, to the wild, ungodly
delightof class 2; and whilst he prayed the brethren
and “ sistern ” kept up a continuous fire of
interjections and heart-rending groans.
‘ ‘ They be people here, ah, what is careless of
Thy grace ; chasten ’em with fire an’ brimstone—
chasten ’em, oh Lord, ah ! They be those of uz
what go to be Thy servants, oh Gwad, ah ! an’ to
do Thy work here blow, ah, what is tried an’ found
wantin’, ah —some do water they milk, oh Gwad,
ah ! an’ some do be misleadin’ they neighbors’ hens
to lay away. Smite they people for Thy glory,
oh Loord, ah ! ”
A great moaning filled the chapel, and all heads
turned towards Brother Nehemiah Best, kneeling
at his chair, with his face buried in his hands,
trembling violently. Nehemiah, two years earlier,
had been fined for watering the milk sold to his
Ballarat customers; quite recently he had been
thrown into the Phoenix slurry by an unregenerate
trucker, who accused him of beguiling his hens to
lay from home. Brother Tresize was wrestling
with the superintendent in prayer, and the excite-
ment rose instantly to fever heat.
“ They what do not as they wad be done by,
pursue ’em, ah, smite they with Thy right hand,
oh, Lord Gawd, ah ! so they may be turned frum
they wickedness, ah. They what have better food
to they table for theyselves than for they children
or they wives, ah, they what be filled with vanity,
ah, they what havin’ no book-learnin’ do deceive
Thy people, an’ fill the seats o’ the learned, ah,
deal with such, oh, Gwad, ah ! ”
Brother Tresize was now almost frantic with the
ecstacy of his zeal. His exhortation was continued
in this strain, and every word was a lance to prick
the cowering superintendent. The women sniffed
and sobbed, the men groaned and cried “ Ahmen,
ah ! ” It was a great time for grace.
* • « *
But suddenly a new voice broke in—a shrill,
thin voice, splitting into that of Brother Tresize
like a steam-whistle. Brother Best had assumed
the defensive.
“ Oh, Lord, ah ! ” he cried, “ give no ear to they
what bears false witness against they neighbors,
to they what backbite, ah, an’ slander, ah, an’
bear malice, ah ; heed they not, oh, Lord, ah ! ”
Abel Tresize rose to the occasion. It was a
battle. His voice swelled till it rivalled the roar
of the ravening lion ; he no longer selected his
words or cared to make himself understood of the
people ; it was necessary only to smother Brother
Best, to pray him down, and Abel prayed as no
man had ever before prayed at Waddy. A curious
crowd, the Irish children, Dan the Drover, an
old shepherd, and a few cattlemen from the Red
Cow, attracted by the great commotion, had as-
seinbled in the porch, and were gazing in open
mouthed, delighted.
Tresize persevered, but Best’s shrill, penetrating
voice rang out distinctly above all. Brother Best
was transformed, inspired ; under the influence of
his great wrath he had waxed eloquent; he smote
his enemy hip and thigh, he heaped coals of fire
upon his head, and marshalled St. Peter and all
the angels against him.
The severity of his exertions was telling heavily
upon Abel Tresize ; he was dreadfully hoarse, his
great hands fell upon the form without emphasis,
he was almost winded, and his legs wobbled under
him. He pulled himself together for another
effort, and the cry that he uttered thrilled every
heart, but it quite exhausted him, and he went
over backwards, striking his head upon the floor,
and lay in the aisle convulsed in a fit.
Instantly the chapel became a babel. The
teachers ran to Brother Tresize, and bore him into
the open air, the wondering children crowding
after, and left the new superintendent sobbing on
his table like a broken-hearted boy.
EDWARD DYSON.

When the Junior Partner Came .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Things have changed a little lately
In our office in the town,
And though no one’s wondered greatly,
It has pulled our spirits down ;
For the “ boss,” with vigor failing,
Now elects to stop away,
And a partner comes in sailing
Whose commands We must obey.
Junior partners ne’er are relished
Till they overcome the dread
That, with sundry fears embellished,
Fills each clerk’s expectant head—
Fears of reconstruction drastic,
Visions of the awful “sack,”
That, with minds howe’er elastic,
Make one long to see their back.
O’er the place a gloom is hanging,
Every footstep makes us start,
And each time a door goes banging
Comes a thrill to every heart;
Yet we cannot give the reason
Why we do not feel the s'ame,
But that we’ve been full of treason
Since the junior partner came
Nothing in the place is shifted—
Not a book, or desk, or stool:
Not a ruler has been lifted,
Yet suspicion is the rule.
Not a soul’s been reprimanded,
Everything is still the same ;
Nothing has been countermanded
Since the junior partner came.
Yet we all feel discontented,
Each one irritation shows,
And we wish our much-lamented
Whilom “boss ” would “show his nose
And we shake our heads despondent,
For our thoughts we cannot frame,
But regret, with minds respondent,
That the junior partner came.
Still we cannot eease from working,
Though we don’t like Number Two ;
I f he caught us duty shirking
We would soon have none to do.
Twenty partners would not matter,
Business still would be the same ;
And much better is the latter
Since the junior partner came.
But the “ old man ” yet will linger
In the mem’ry of his men ;
Still we see him rub his finger,
Marked with ink-stains from his pen $
And his honest laugh and hearty,
That convulsed his very frame,
Made us quite a happy party
Till the junior partner came.
Well, heigho ! those times are vanished,
Methods new have got the hold ;
/And the new “old man” has banished
All remembrance of the old.
It may be the better for us
And our ardour may inflame;
And we’ll yet rejoice in chorus
That the junior partner came.
M.L.
JOHN LANARK.
His First-Born.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in the days before the dangerous cliff on
the seaward side of the “Gap,” at South Head,
Sydney, was fenced off.
The spot choSen by the young people to cele-
brate the anniversary of their wedding-day was
about GO yards from the edge of the cliff. The
little picnic for three had been a blissful success,
only disturbed by the necessity of recaptur-
ing Master Bobbie whenever he toddled away to
make a closer acquaintance with the “ big splashy
splash ” that stretched away before his wondering
eyes m sparkling blue wavelets dotted over with
white sails.
But the little fellow had grown tired,and dis-
couraged in his vain attempts to reach the big
bath that looked so temptingly near ; and when
at length he ceased his efforts to escape and lay
down by his mother’s side, she took up her novel
and, with a sigh of relief, resumed her reading.
The husband was stretched out comfortably on
his back, his hat tilted over lus forehead, study-
ing the weights for the Summer Cup. But a hearty
dinner, on a blazing day in December, is not con-
ducive to mental activity, and soon the drowsy
hum of life below in the bay, and the soothing
murmur of the summer surf on the rocks under
the cliff, had their effect. The print danced in
a purple mist before his eyes, the pipe dropped
from his mouth, and he slept—with the paper
lying on his face.
God, what a shriek !
He sat up, wide awake, and his startled eyes
took in the situation at a glance. His little boy
stood a few yards from the cliff looking back over
his shoulder at them, and his laughing eyes chal-
lenged his mother for another race. She sprang
to her feet, but the man’s hand shot out and
dragged her down so fiercely that her dress tore
away from the bodice.
“Quiet!” he said in a savage, husky tone of
command. “If you chase him over the cliff I’ll
throw you after him ! ”
The woman cowered away before his fierce
glance and hid her face in the grass. It was the
first harsh word or look she had received from him
since the happy day they were celebrating.
The man’s brain worked swiftly under the
powerful incentive of his absorbing love for the
boy. It was a more intricate problem to solve
*-han the difference between “Inspiration at 9st.,
and Surprise at 6st. 121 b.” - He had seen, at once,
that it was hopeless to attempt to cover the
ground between him and his son before the little
fellow gained the edge of the cliff. At the first
step he took the child, he knew, would run for the
coveted goal, too excited by the fun to see any
danger. His heart stopped a moment; and all
sound suddenly ceased, save the ominous droning
in his ears as, in a flash of mental torture, he pic-
tured his bonnie boy lying on the rocks under the
cliff, a shapeless heap. It was only a momentary
hesitation. The boy’s soft, musical laugh stiffened
his nerves. He was poised ready for a fresh start,
lhe mother looked up at that familiar sound ring-
ing so clearly through the still air, and cried :
Do something, for God’s sake ! ”
The man cleared his throat and called “ Bob !
Bobbie ! come here. I want you ! ”
His voice barely rose above a hoarse whisper;
out Bobbie saw the accompanying gesture of
recall, and, laughing in high glee, ran a few more
paces, looking backward all the time.
His father, moistening his parched lips, took
the watch from his pocket, held it up by the chain,
and called again in a stronger voice.
“ Bobbie, come and see what daddy’s got for
you ! ”
The little fellow stopped. The sunlight, spark-
ling on the gold cases, which the man had opened
to make the lure more attractive, arrested the
child’s attention. He retraced his steps a little
way—slowly and doubtfully, as though he had
some recollection of being caught by that delusive
prize before. Just then his mother looked up,
and, seeing her child approaching, sprang to meet
him. The movement confirmed the child’s sus-
picions, and, laughing defiantly, he turned and
ran. In a fury of disappointment the man looked
at his wife as though he would strike her.
“ Damn you for a fool! ”he hissed with savage
intensity ; and, holding her firmly by the arm, he
shouted, “ Here, Bobbie, catch it! ” and this time,
having fixed the child’s attention, he hove the
watch twenty yards towards him. Bobbie saw it
now, flashing in the sunlight, unprotected ; and,
recognising that it was a fair deal, he ran for it as
fast as his stout little legs would carry him. But,
even as he grasped the prize, he was, in turn,
clutched by his father and hugged with a fervor
that elicited a howl of remonstrance. His mother
was even more unsatisfactory in her greeting.
She only shook him by the arm, and called him a
“ bad, wicked boy.” Then she turned a reproachful
gaze upon her husband. Womanlike, she sought
solace for her wounded feelings now that the boy
was safe.
“It was not all my fault, Robert. You went
to sleep also, you know.”
“ I don’t blame you for going to sleep.”
“ Then why did you behave so savagely to me,
and use such dreadful language ? ”
“ There was no time to waste upon politeness,”
replied Robert, as he kissed his first-born and
hoisted him on to his shoulder. “I’m very sorry
I spoke so roughly; I wasn’t answerable for my
words at the moment. Forget them, my dear, and
' let well alone.’ ”
But the woman was not quite satisfied. An
idea, that was previously only a shadowy impres-
sion, had been hurried by Bobbie into a painful
certainty. His escapade had acted upon her hus-
band as the developer acts upon the picture hidden
on a dry-plate.
As the pair walked down the hill to the little
jetty where the Sydney boat lay, the woman again
returned to the subject with a tentative question :
“ You didn’t really mean what you said about ”
(she paused to control her voice) ‘ about throwing
me over the cliff. Did you, Robert ? ”
The man, fondling his boy’s chubby little fist
that gripped him firmly by the ear, replied
grimly •
“ I’m afraid I did —at that moment.”
The little woman looked up keenly at her hus-
band s face; and she noticed that the faint line
across his forehead had grown suddenly distinct.
Then she dropped behind “ to pm up her skirt.”
The boy looked back at her and, waving his hand,
cried roguishly—” Ta-ta, little mammie ! "
But his mother took no notice of him, for her
eyes were blurred with tears.
On the steamer, Bobbie accidentally wiped his
dusty shoes on his mother’s dress, and, much to
his amazement, she swooped upon him joyously
and spanked him with unusual vigor.
When Bobbie escaped he ran to his father,
sobbing out his pain and indignation in a storm
of tears. But the man remained unmoved. He
understood now; and was more sorry for his wife
than for his first-born. The child’s grief would
soon pass and be forgotten. But hers ?
Bingara, N.S.W.
CRIPPS CLARK.
A Little Leal Life .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene : Over-crowded Manly steamer, Sunday
afternoon. A little, shrivelled-up, 5ft. woman,
who looked as if she had worked hard every .day
of her life, and yet enjoyed it thoroughly, and
evidently dotingly in love with her very good-
looking husband, head and shoulders taller than
herself, and (say) 12 years her junior. He must
have married her for her sterling qualities—she
could never have had any good looks—but if ever
unselfish love was in a woman’s face it was in hers.
There were no seats available, and he sat on
the railing at the steamer’s side. There was
something pathetic in the way she tried to get
up beside him, but she was too tiny. I overheard
them talking. He looked ashamed of himself, but
was trying not to show it. She looked as if she
were forcing herself to make the best of a bad job.
Presently she said : “I won’t ever go to Manly
to see them again. I couldn’t after to-day. Who
was it you were with ? ”
He : "It wouldn’t make it any better for you to
know, little woman.”
She : “ And me telling them so grandly that I
knew you had brought your niece down. What
a fool they must think me ! ”
He (defiantly) : “Well, I did take my niece
down one day.”
She : “ Yes, but not on that Sunday.” After a
pause, “ Well, it won’t be for long. Soon, when
you get old, you won’t want to do that sort of
thing. You will be satisfied with your own old
woman. You are getting old now, you know.”
And then the steamer reached Sydney and I
heard no more.
M.M.
One expects to find Quong Tart’s name
bobbing up ’mongst all sorts of things Australian,
but one does not expect to see it—the only “Q ”
in the index—in a work “In Tropical Lands,” by
A. Sinclair, because Mr. Sinclair’s book is mostly
Peru and S. America, and there is no Australia in
it. The übiquitous Quong is thus mentioned by
way of swaggering up the Peruvian educated
Chinaman :
This gentleman has his prototype in Quong Tart, one
of the most wealthy, benevolent and distinguished
citizens of N.S.W., whose command of pure nervous
English—only second to that of Sir Henry Parkes—makes
him an acquisition at every public meeting, while at
popular concerts he is simply indispensable ... in
the race for wealth and position not only has he out-
distanced the Scotch colonist, but he has completely
eclipsed him in his own particular line. Mr. Quong Tart
is unquestionably the best singer of Scotch songs in
N.S.W., while in Highland costume he can render the
Highland fling to the manner born.

No one to look to-day at Miss Sophie Kiefer, a pretty and stylish young belle,
whose portrait is given above, and who lives at the northwest corner of Fourth and
Queen streets, Philadelphia, would imagine that she had ever been in anything but
the pink of health. Her skin is clear and creamy, her teeth are white and even, she
has luxuriant dark hair, and her eyes are large and bright, of a lustrous brown. Yet,
notwithstanding Miss Kiefer’s splendid appearance, only a year ago she was in
extremely poor health, and her blood was in a very bad condition. Miss Kiefer’s
story is best told in her own words :
“ When I began to take Ayer’s Sarsaparilla I was not only run down, but I was
thoroughly discouraged. Doctors and proprietary medicines both failed to help me.
My blood was disordered. But the first bottle of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla helped me so
much that I decided to keep it up, and did so. Then, inside of a month, I began to
think that the Sarsaparilla was a wonder. It cleared my skin completely and restored
my appetite to its former condition. My general health was recovered and the third
bottle removed every appearance of disorder from my blood. Not only that, but it
left me better than ever. For several months I had been ill and complaining. My
health in general, aside from the condition of my blood, was very poor. I remember
one day, after attending a ball the night before, when I felt so miserable that I was
afraid I was going to die, almost. My appearance, of which I always took consideraj-
ble care, was the thing that worried me most of all, I will acknowledge. But as I
tell vou, Ayer’s Sarsaparilla rejuvenated me. I would take it any time in preference
to toilet preparations and skin soaps and washes, for it worked wonders with me. I
can recommend Ayer’s Sarsaparilla to anyone whose blood is poor or whose general
health is unsatisfactory.”
